do país não seja clara em permitir ou proibir expressamente um determinado pagamento e os demais elementos do crime estejam presentes, o pagamento será considerado ilícito, independentemente do valor, salvo brindes e pequenas cortesias sem a intenção de corromper ou influenciar indevidamente o funcionário público estrangeiro. Um bom parâmetro para se preencher esta lacuna é o recurso ao Código de Conduta da Alta Administração Federal, cujo art. 9 º regula detalhadamente o que deve ser considerado como limite para ser aceito pelo funcionário público.32 Por outro lado, o mesmo Código de Conduta da Alta Administração Federal apresenta falhas, ao permitir a "participação em seminários, congressos e eventos semelhantes, desde que tornada pública eventual remuneração, bem como o pagamento das despesas de viagem pelo promotor do evento, o qual não poderá ter interesse em decisão a ser tomada pela autoridade", 33 sem atentar para as implicações.34 De uma forma geral, a OCDE considerou que o Brasil cumpriu com o acordo e indicou temas pontuais que necessitavam de melhorias, tais como a alteração da lei de defesa da ordem econômica (Lei nº 8.884/94) para incluir a corrupção como um ato expressamente proibido. Outro tema que preocupou a OCDE foi a possibilidade de as empresas mascararem as despesas com corrupção como despesas comerciais legítimas, passíveis de dedução tributária. Inexistem punições severas neste tocante. Por outro lado, a segunda fase da avaliação da OCDE sobre a implementação do acordo no Brasil, ocorrida em dezembro de 2007, criticou o Brasil por não ser tão enfático no combate à corrupção de funcionários públicos estrangeiros quanto com o combate à corrupção de nacionais. Além disso, o Brasil foi duramente criticado por ainda não ter implementado instrumentos legais capazes de impor sanções efetivas e dissuasivas às pessoas jurídicas condenadas por corrupção internacional. Para lidar com parte das críticas, o governo enviou ao Congresso Nacional o Projeto de Lei nº 6.826/10 para dispor sobre a responsabilização administrativa e civil de pessoas jurídicas pela prática de ato contra a administração pública, nacional ou internacional. Na sua exposição de motivos, lê-se claramente o propósito "de atender aos compromissos internacionais de combate à

corrupção assumidos pelo Brasil ao ratificar a Convenção das Nações Unidas contra Corrupção (ONU), a Convenção Interamericana de Combate à Corrupção (OEA) e a Convenção sobre o Combate da Corrupção de Funcionários Públicos Estrangeiros em Transações Comerciais Internacionais da Organização para Cooperação e Desenvolvimento Econômico (OCDE)." Em realidade, o Projeto de Lei nº 6.826/10 trata da corrupção de funcionários estrangeiros de forma mais detalhada que o disposto no Código Penal, prevendo expressamente a inclusão dos órgãos e entidades estatais de qualquer nível ou esfera de governo; representações diplomáticas; e empresas controladas, direta ou indiretamente, pelo poder público de país estrangeiro. As sanções previstas no Projeto de Lei nº 6.826/2010, conforme texto originalmente submetido ao Congresso Nacional, incluem: (i) multa de 1% a 30% do faturamento bruto; (ii) ressarcimento integral do dano; (iii) declaração de inidoneidade; (iv) publicação extraordinária da decisão paga pela empresa condenada em meio de comunicação de grande circulação na área da prática da infração; (v) afixação de edital informando da condenação, por prazo mínimo de trinta dias, no estabelecimento ou local de exercício da atividade da empresa, de forma visível ao público; (vi) proibição de contratar com o poder público; (vii) proibição de receber incentivos, subsídios, doações ou empréstimos do Poder Público por até 10 anos; (viii) suspensão ou interdição parcial das atividades da empresa; e (ix) dissolução compulsória da pessoa jurídica. Se aprovado, o Brasil terá instrumentos semelhantes aos Estados Unidos e ao Reino Unido para punir as empresas condenadas por suborno de funcionário estrangeiro e outras práticas que atentem contra o patrimônio público nacional ou estrangeiro, contra os princípios da administração pública ou contra compromissos internacionais assumidos pelo Brasil. Um ponto interessante se refere ao enforcement da legislação de combate à corrupção internacional. Até o fim de 2007, a OCDE observou que os tribunais brasileiros ainda não tinham julgado qualquer caso relacionado à corrupção de oficiais estrangeiros. Em vista disso, recomendaram um aumento dos recursos direcionados ao órgão responsável pelas investigações para melhor capacitação dos profissionais e aumento das investigações. A OCDE também sugeriu que o Brasil demonstrasse maior clareza na proibição de dedução fiscal de subornos, através de legislação especial ou instrumento similar. Por fim, foi recomendado que o Brasil aumentasse seus esforços para incentivar as empresas na implementação de programas de prevenção e repressão interna ao suborno de funcionários estrangeiros.35

6. As Convenções da OEA e da ONU
Após a aprovação da Convenção da OCDE, as iniciativas anticorrupção começaram a se proliferar no cenário internacional. O Brasil é signatário de outros dois acordos internacionais, organizados pela Organização dos Estados Americanos (OEA) e pela Organização das Nações Unidas (ONU). A Convenção Interamericana contra a Corrupção, negociada em Caracas em 1996, foi o primeiro internacional a tratar tanto de medidas preventivas quanto de medidas coercitivas contra a corrupção. A Convenção foi aprovada pelo Decreto Legislativo n. 152, de 25 de junho de 2002, e promulgada pelo Decreto Presidencial n. 4.410, de 7 de outubro de 2002. Ratificada sem restrições pelo Brasil, a Convenção lista doze medidas preventivas, que visam dificultar a ocultação do pagamento e do recebimento de suborno e estimular o controle e denúncia de práticas corruptas:
"1. Normas de conduta para o desempenho correto, honrado e adequado das funções públicas. Estas normas deverão ter por finalidade prevenir conflitos de interesses, assegurar a guarda e uso adequado dos recursos confiados aos funcionários públicos no desempenho de suas funções e estabelecer medidas e sistemas para exigir dos funcionários públicos que informem as autoridades competentes dos atos de corrupção nas funções públicas de que tenham conhecimento. Tais medidas ajudarão a preservar a confiança na integridade dos funcionários públicos e na gestão pública. 2. Mecanismos para tornar efetivo o cumprimento dessas normas de conduta. 3. Instruções ao pessoal dos órgãos públicos a fim de garantir o adequado entendimento de suas responsabilidades e das normas éticas que regem as suas atividades. 4. Sistemas para a declaração das receitas, ativos e passivos por parte das pessoas que desempenhem funções públicas em determinados cargos estabelecidos em lei e, quando for o caso, para a divulgação dessas declarações. 5. Sistemas de recrutamento de funcionários públicos e de aquisição de bens e serviços por parte do Estado de forma a assegurar sua transparência, equidade e eficiência. 6. Sistemas para arrecadação e controle da renda do Estado que impeçam a prática da corrupção. 7. Leis que vedem tratamento tributário favorável a qualquer pessoa física ou jurídica em relação a despesas efetuadas com violação dos dispositivos legais dos Estados Partes contra a corrupção. 8. Sistemas para proteger funcionários públicos e cidadãos particulares que denunciarem e boa-fé atos de corrupção, inclusive a proteção de sua identidade, sem prejuízo da Constituição do Estado e dos princípios fundamentais de seu ordenamento jurídico interno.

2 ­ Esforços globais anticorrupção e seus reflexos no Brasil

37

9. Órgãos de controle superior, a fim de desenvolver mecanismos modernos para prevenir, detectar, punir e erradicar as práticas corruptas. 10. Medidas que impeçam o suborno de funcionários públicos nacionais e estrangeiros, tais como mecanismos para garantir que as sociedades mercantis e outros tipos de associações mantenham registros que, com razoável nível de detalhe, reflitam com exatidão a aquisição e alienação de ativos e mantenham controles contábeis internos que permitam aos funcionários da empresa detectarem a ocorrência de atos de corrupção. 11. Mecanismos para estimular a participação da sociedade civil e de organizações não governamentais nos esforços para prevenir a corrupção. 12. O estudo de novas medidas de prevenção, que levem em conta a relação entre uma remuneração equitativa e a probidade no serviço público."

A Convenção da ONU, ratificada pelo Decreto Legislativo n. 348, de 18 de maio de 2005, e promulgada pelo Decreto Presidencial n. 5.687, de 31 de janeiro de 2006, é o maior instrumento vinculante internacional de combate à corrupção de funcionários estrangeiros. A Convenção da ONU trata da criminalização da corrupção, cooperação internacional e medidas preventivas. Apesar de já terem sido abordados nos acordos anteriores, estes dispositivos abordam os temas de forma mais detalhada. Além destas regras, a Convenção da ONU regula a recuperação de ativos, tida como um dos seus princípios fundamentais ­ pouco adianta a dissuasão efetiva imposta pelo FCPA se os recursos desviados permanecem no exterior ou acabam nos cofres de outros governos: o importante é que o país prejudicado efetivamente recupere seus recursos desviados. Na contabilidade motivadora dos jogadores corruptos, a manutenção das vantagens financeiras obtidas é um dos objetivos principais, mesmo quando os criminosos são detectados e punidos. A lógica é simples: se a vantagem econômica auferida é ainda mantida, o caráter dissuasório da ação repressiva estatal é enfraquecido. Alterar essa variável é peça chave do combate à corrupção. O desafio é maior ainda sob a da globalização financeira. Para a recuperação de ativos, os signatários se comprometeram a adotar medidas necessárias para exigir das instituições financeiras que funcionam em seu território que verifiquem a identidade dos clientes e dos destinatários de depósitos de valores altos, além de manter firme controle da movimentação em contas solicitadas ou mantidas em nome de pessoas que desempenham ou tenham desempenhado funções públicas e de seus familiares e colaboradores próximos. Caso uma operação suspeita seja detectada, a instituição financeira tem o dever de informar as autoridades, fornecendo os dados de todos envolvidos. Em maio de 2011, o acordo da ONU já possuía adesão de quase todos os países do planeta, conforme ilustra o mapa a seguir.

38

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

Países-membros Signatários Países que não assinaram ou ratificaram a Convenção da ONU

Antes mesmo da entrada em vigor da Convenção da ONU, o Brasil já vinha aperfeiçoando seus mecanismos de anticorrupção, especialmente no que tange à lavagem de dinheiro: a Lei nº 9.613/9836 definiu o crime de lavagem de dinheiro e estabeleceu medidas preventivas e repressivas para combatê-lo. O referido diploma legal criou o Departamento de Combate a Ilícitos Cambiais e Financeiros no Banco Central e o Conselho de Controle de Atividades Financeiras (COAF), vinculado ao Ministério da Fazenda, o qual é responsável por receber, examinar e dar encaminhamento às denúncias sobre práticas suspeitas de lavagem de dinheiro. Nos termos da Lei nº 9.613/98, o COAF foi incumbido de disciplinar e aplicar penas administrativas, bem como coordenar e propor mecanismos de cooperação e de troca de informações que viabilizem ações rápidas no combate à ocultação ou dissimulação de bens, direitos e valores.

7. Demais iniciativas internacionais
Além das Convenções da OCDE, da OEA e da ONU, outros organismos internacionais adotaram medidas para combate à corrupção que têm reflexos indiretos sobre o Brasil. De fato, há muito as organizações internacionais têm se deparado com o tema ­ por exemplo, em 1993, a Organização Mundial do

Para tanto, o Banco Mundial tem estudado formas de atuação que lhe permitiriam atacar as causas da corrupção e desarticular seus efeitos.40 O Banco Mundial passou a divulgar gratuitamente informações relativas aos seus projetos, além de promover prestação de contas ao público (conforme determinam as políticas Open Data e Access to Information Policy). Uma das principais ferramentas de sanção usadas pelo Banco Mundial é o banimento de empresas corruptas, impedindo-as de participar de projetos financiados. Um dos exemplos recentes desta medida foi a medida aplicada à editora inglesa Macmillan. Em abril de 2010, a empresa foi banida de projetos financiados pelo Banco Mundial por seis anos. A eficácia do banimento de empresas pelo Banco Mundial não era suficiente para punir e prevenir atos de corrupção, pois existem diversos outros bancos similares de fomento ao desenvolvimento que ainda poderiam contratar empresas banidas. Os demais bancos de fomento continuavam expostos a riscos financeiros (pelo desvio dos recursos investidos) e às suas reputações.41 Nesse contexto, os cinco principais bancos multilaterais de fomento ao desenvolvimento assinaram o Agreement for Mutual Enforcement of Debarment Decisions, por meio do qual o Banco Mundial, o African Development Bank Group, o Asian Development Bank, o European Bank for Reconstruction and Development e o Inter-American Development Bank Group, concordaram em banir conjuntamente as empresas condenadas por um dos bancos por prática de atos de corrupção. Até fevereiro de 2010, 28 empresas haviam sido banidas em mais de um banco em decorrência de sanções aplicadas por outro banco.

8. Considerações finais
O combate à corrupção pressupõe uma coordenação elevada entre as nações, de modo que atribuir as mudanças somente aos interesses específicos de um país seria míope. Porém, a mera proliferação de acordos internacionais não é garantia de eficácia no combate à corrupção, mesmo porque a maioria das condutas ocorre dentro dos limites do Estado Nacional. Embora a assinatura de acordos internacionais revele um compromisso a respeito,42 vários países, paraísos fiscais ou não, continuam a oferecer refúgio aos capitais oriundos de práticas corruptas, mesmo após a adesão a tais acordos internacionais. A tabela abaixo mostra a variação do Índice de Percepção da Corrupção em vários países e nada indica que o maior ou menor comprometimento internacional guarde alguma relação com a melhora ou piora da situação. O resultado prático de acordos internacionais sobre o tema depende, assim, do que as pessoas dentro dos países farão com os instrumentos disponíveis. Se não forem utilizados, pouco ajudarão no combate à corrupção. Se forem

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

1. Introduction

T

he World Bank Group and the Inter-American Development Bank Group (IDB) are international financial institutions dedicated to reducing poverty by promoting economic growth. Recognizing that development assistance is a precious resource, both multi-lateral development banks (MDBs) have developed a robust system of administrative sanctions to ensure funds are used for the intended purpose of promoting development and reducing poverty, and are not jeopardized by corruption. Corruption is one of the greatest obstacles to economic and social development. If left unchecked, it is certain to undermine development projects and, ultimately, it can erode confidence among donors and beneficiaries, which can lead to skepticism about the effectiveness of investments in international development. To address this problem, the World Bank and the IDB have both developed internal sanctions mechanisms to debar companies and individuals found to have engaged in fraud and corruption on their funded projects.7 These MDBs have also agreed to honor certain of each other's debarments, greatly enhancing their ability to jointly crack down on fraud and corruption.8 The systems in place at both MDBs have the ultimate goal of curbing fraud and corruption, but the procedures are also designed to ensure that the sanctions processes are transparent and fair, and that they offer firms and individuals involved in these proceedings an opportunity to respond to the allegations against them. The following article details the system of administrative sanctions in place at the World Bank and the IDB. The purpose of this article is to describe the current institutional frameworks being used to sanction parties who are engaged in prohibited practices as defined by the sanction procedures adopted by the World Bank and the IDB.9

2. The World Bank's Sanctions System
The administrative sanctions process at the World Bank is rooted in the legal framework set out in the Articles of Agreement, the treaty which established the World Bank.10 These Articles require that the World Bank ensure its funds are used for their intended purpose. In furtherance of this obligation, the World
7. Other MDBs including the African Development Bank (AfDB), the Asian Development Bank (AsDB), and the European Bank for Reconstruction and Development (EBRD) also have mechanisms in place to debar individuals and companies involved in corrupt practices, but the details of those sanction systems are outside the scope of this article. 8. Agreement For Mutual Enforcement of Debarment Decisions, available at http://www.adb.org/ documents/integrity/cross-debarment-agreement.pdf. 9. Both the IDB and the World Bank Systems have their scope of sanctioning limited to individuals and entities that are not part of governments or employees of the respective banks. 10. IBRD Articles of Agreement, available at http://go.worldbank.org/WAUZA5KF90.

3 ­ Sanctions at the World Bank and the Inter-American Development Bank: ...

47

Bank incorporates by reference its Procurement and Consultant Guidelines11 into loan agreements between the World Bank and its borrower countries. Loan agreements12 also incorporate by reference the World Bank's Anti-Corruption Guidelines, which outline the obligations of borrowers and recipients "to prevent and combat fraud and corruption" in projects financed by the World Bank.13 The definition of what constitutes a "sanctionable" practice under the World Bank's sanctions system has changed over the years. In 1999, the Procurement and Consultant Guidelines referred only to corruption and fraud, with collusion. In 2004, the World Bank added a separate definition of collusive practices and added coercive practices, such as threatening others, to the list of unacceptable behaviors. And in 2006, the World Bank Guidelines added obstructive practices, encompassing actions that impede an investigation, such as destroying evidence, not allowing the World Bank to exercise its audit rights or threatening witnesses. The World Bank and other MDBs have adopted standardized definitions relating to fraud, corruption, collusion and coercive practices.14 The Bank and the IDB also share a definition of what constitutes "obstructive practices." Together, these five prohibited actions are what the Bank15 and the IDB16 refer to as "sanctionable practices." The steps in the World Bank's sanctions process are laid out in the Bank's Sanctions Board Statute and Sanctions Procedures.17 The World Bank learns about possible sanctionable conduct from any of a variety of sources, such as its own staff, the local government, or other bidders. The World Bank's Integrity

"Loan agreements" are understood to include financing agreements between the World Bank and Borrower for the provision of loans, credits, or grants financed by the World Bank (including IBRD and IDA) from its resources or from trust funds financed by other donors and administered by the World Bank. 13. TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

Vice Presidency (INT) investigates allegations through various channels, which may include interviewing witnesses, interviewing the firm or individual who committed the alleged offense, gathering documents, and visiting the project site.18 If INT finds evidence of sanctionable misconduct by a firm or individual, INT presents the case to a Sanctions Evaluation and Suspension Officer (EO), which constitutes the first tier of the Sanctions Process. INT submits to the EO a Statement of Accusations and Evidence (SAE), which summarizes the accusations and attaches the relevant evidence, both exculpatory and inculpatory. There are four EOs, one for the International Bank for Reconstruction and Development and International Development Association (who focuses on this work exclusively) and three part-time EOs for the Multilateral Investment Guarantee Agency, International Finance Corporation, and Bank Guarantee Projects (who work part-time on sanctions in addition to their other responsibilities). The EO evaluates the SAE and the evidence presented by INT and determines whether the evidence is sufficient to support a finding of sanctionable misconduct. If the evidence is sufficient, the EO issues a Notice of Sanctions Proceedings to the individual or firm (known as the Respondent), which includes all evidence presented by INT and a sanction recommended by the EO.19 At that point, the EO temporarily suspends the Respondent from eligibility to be awarded contracts for Bank projects or to participate in new activities in connection with Bank projects.20 The temporary suspension is posted on the World Bank's internal sanctions website and its extranet website known as "Client Connection," which is accessible to certain personnel in Bank member countries, but not to the general public.21 The limited distribution of information regarding temporary suspensions allows the appropriate parties to give effect to the suspension and also allows Respondents to appeal the sanction before it is made available to the general public. After delivery of the Notice of Sanctions Proceedings, the Respondent is given 30 days to send the EO an Explanation as to why the Notice should be withdrawn or the recommended sanction revised. Within 30 days of receiving
18. During the investigation phase, INT may submit a request to a Sanctions Evaluation and Suspension Officer (EO) to impose an early temporary suspension on the subject of an INT investigation. The EO may impose an early temporary suspension if the EO finds that there is already sufficient evidence that the subject has engaged in at least one sanctionable practice. The Respondent may provide rebutting evidence and petition the EO to lift the suspension. 19. "Sufficient evidence" is defined in the World Bank Sanctions Procedures as "evidence sufficient to support a reasonable belief, that it is more likely than not that the Respondent has engaged in a Sanctionable Practice." World Bank Sanctions Procedures, supra note 16 at § 1.02. 20. If the recommended sanction does not include a minimum period of debarment exceeding six months, the Respondent is not temporarily suspended. 21. The temporary suspension is not public since the Respondent has 90 days to appeal the decision. Once the sanction is final then the suspension becomes public.

3 ­ Sanctions at the World Bank and the Inter-American Development Bank: ...

49

an Explanation, the EO may decide to terminate the temporary suspension imposed on the Respondent, withdraw the Notice, or revise the recommended sanction in light of the evidence or arguments presented by the Respondent. If the Respondent does not contest the accusations or recommended sanction, the sanction recommended by the EO is imposed, and the EO's determination is posted on the World Bank's sanctions website. If the Respondent appeals the accusations made by INT and/or the sanction recommended by the EO, the case is referred to the World Bank Group Sanctions Board, which constitutes the second tier in the sanctions process.22 The Sanctions Board is an independent body within the Bank and is supported by a permanent secretariat. The Board is composed of three World Bank staff and four non-Bank staff, and chaired by one of its non-Bank staff members. The external members are appointed by the Executive Directors of the World Bank from a roster of candidates nominated by the President of the World Bank. The internal members are appointed by the President of the World Bank from among senior World Bank staff. For cases involving the Multilateral Investment Guarantee Agency, International Finance Corporation, and Bank Guarantee Projects, separate Sanctions Board members with specific expertise are appointed. The Respondent has the opportunity to contest the accusations or the sanction recommended by the EO by filing a written Response with the Sanctions Board within 90 days of receiving the Notice. The Respondent may present evidence to refute the accusations. INT has 30 days from the Sanctions Board's receipt of the Respondent's Response to submit a Reply. Either INT or the Respondent may request a hearing before the Sanctions Board. The Sanctions Board considers the accusations and evidence contained in the Notice of Sanctions Proceedings, the arguments and evidence submitted by Respondent, INT's Reply, and any other materials contained in the record prior to making a decision. The Sanctions Board reviews cases de novo and it is not bound by the EO's determination or recommended sanction. After completing its review, the Sanctions Board determines whether it is more likely than not that the Respondent engaged in sanctionable misconduct. If it finds that the Respondent has engaged in sanctionable misconduct, the Sanctions Board imposes an appropriate sanction. Decisions of the Sanctions Board are final and non-appealable. Decisions of the Sanctions Board regarding cases where the Notice was issued by the EO on or after January 1, 2011 are available on the World Bank's sanctions website.

22. The predecessor to the current sanctions system was called the Sanctions Committee.

50

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

There are five possible sanctions within the Bank's sanctions system. The baseline sanction is a 3-year debartment with conditional release.23 The other sanctions are indefinite or fixed-term debarment, conditional non-debarment, public letter of reprimand, and restitution.24 Debarment with conditional release typically requires the sanctioned party to put in place, and implement for an adequate period of time, an integrity compliance program satisfactory to the World Bank Group. In September 2010, the World Bank appointed its first Integrity Compliance Officer (ICO) to monitor integrity compliance for sanctioned Respondents and decide whether the conditions for release established by the World Bank have been met.25 Under conditional non-debarment, the Respondent is not debarred provided that it complies with certain defined conditions within a set timeframe. The determination as to whether a sanctioned party has met the established conditions is made by the ICO.26 In cases of minor misconduct or peripheral involvement, the Bank may issue a letter of reprimand to the sanctioned party. Examples of this action might include cases where an affiliate of the Respondent has been found to have some responsibility for misconduct because of an isolated lapse in supervision.27 Lastly, in appropriate cases, the sanctioned party may be required to make restitution or provide financial remedies, or to take actions to remedy the harm done by its misconduct.28 In determining the sanction, the EO and the Sanctions Board are guided by the World Bank's Sanctioning Guidelines, which are not prescriptive but provide the considerations that are relevant to the sanctioning decision.29 The Sanctioning Guidelines provide information regarding aggravating and mitigating factors to be considered. The general categories of aggravating factors are: 1) the severity of the misconduct, 2) the harm caused by the misconduct, 3) interference with the investigation, and 4) past history of adjudicated misconduct.30

23. "The term `baseline' sanction means the sanction that would normally be imposed for a sanctionable practice before giving effect to any aggravating or mitigating factors." 51

The general categories of mitigating factors are 1) minor role in the misconduct, 2) voluntary corrective action, and 3) cooperation with the investigation.31

3. The IDB Sanctioning System
The current sanctions system in place at the Inter-American Development Bank is the end result of a strategy adopted in the late nineties. The system has evolved as the IDB has learned from its own experience and that of other institutions, including the World Bank. As it is the case with the World Bank, the IDB's Articles of Agreement specify that the Bank's resources should only be used for their intended purpose.32 Based on this fiduciary duty, in 1988 the IDB amended Basic Procurement Policies and included the first definitions of Prohibited Practices and a procedure for the submission and adjudication of complaints related to fraud and corruption. 33 By 2001, the IDB established an Oversight Committee on Fraud and Corruption in charge of coordinating the Bank's responses to allegations of fraud and corruption and oversee investigations and in 2003, the Office of Institutional Integrity was created, and mandated to conduct these investigations. In 2006, the IDB joined the other MDBs and adopted the harmonized definitions for the following prohibited practices: corruption, fraud, coercion and collusion. Finally, in early 2011 and in response to a review of its anti-corruption framework undertaken by former US Attorney General Dick Thornburgh, the IDB strengthened its Sanctions Procedures and adopted obstruction as an additional prohibited practice. This new IDB sanctioning system applies to any of the operations of the IDB Group, that is, it applies to operations of the Bank, the Multilateral Investment Fund and the Inter-American Investment Corporation. The new institutional framework of the IDB sanctioning system can be found in the 2011 Sanctions Procedures and consists of three elements: the Office of Institutional Integrity (OII), the Case Officer (a staff member appointed by the President), and the Sanctions Committee. The Sanctions Committee consists of seven members appointed by the President; three are IDB staff members and four are external to the institution. 34 Permanent IDB staff members are appointed to the Committee for a non-renewable term of three years, while external members are appointed for a non-renewable term of five years. The
31. Id. at 4-6. 32. Agreement Establishing the Inter-American Development Bank, Article III, Section 1, available at http://www.iadb.org. 33. Basic Procurement Policies and Procedures of the IDB, 1995 (as amended in 1998), Sections 1.4 and 6.4. Definitions included bribery, extortion or coercion, fraud and collusion. 34. The President has appointed an alternate member of the Committee who is a staff member of the Corporation for purposes of considering cases involving Corporation activities.

52

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

Committee is presided over by one of its members and assisted by a Secretary; both Committee president and Secretary are appointed by the President. OII is responsible for investigating allegations of prohibited practices. These include corruption, fraud, coercion, collusion and obstruction as defined in the Sanctions Procedures. If OII believes that their investigation shows a preponderance of evidence to supports a finding of a prohibited practice, 35 then OII submits a Preliminary Notice of Administrative Action to the Case Officer, which should contain all the relevant inculpatory and exculpatory evidence. The Case Officer constitutes the first tier of the process and is responsible for reviewing the Preliminary Notice and determining whether the preponderance of the evidence supports a finding of a prohibited practice with regards to any case pertaining to the IDB Group. If the Case Officer determines that the evidence does not support a finding, s/he consults with the Chairperson of the Sanctions Committee and informs OII of his/her finding and the reasons for it. This concludes the proceedings on that matter. If the Case Officer determines that the Preliminary Notice presented by OII supports a finding of a prohibited practice by a preponderance of the evidence, s/he prepares a Notice of Administrative Action (Notice), sends it to the Respondents and notifies OII and the Sanctions Committee. The Notice states the findings by the Case Officer and his/her recommended sanction. In addition, the Case Officer may, upon recommendation of the Bank and after consulting with the Chairperson of the Committee, temporarily suspend the eligibility of the Respondent to be awarded additional contracts at the time of issuing the Notice, or before or after the Notice is issued, if failure to do so would cause imminent financial or reputational harm to the IDB or its affiliates. The Respondent may reply in order to oppose the temporary suspension within 60 days and the respondent's reply is considered by the Sanctions Committee. The Respondent may choose one of the following courses of action in response to the Notice: admit culpability, not to respond, or appeal the Notice to the Sanctions Committee within 60 days from receipt. If the Respondent does not respond, it is assumed that the Respondent accepts the allegations and the sanction recommended by the Case Officer takes effect; the same result is triggered if the Respondent admits culpability. If the respondent files a response with the Sanctions Committee, OII has 20 days to reply, after which the respondent has 20 days to file a rejoinder. The Sanctions Committee, as the second tier in the system and on the basis of the submissions received from OII and the Respondent, determines whether
35. "Preponderance of the evidence" means, as defined in the IDB Sanctions Procedures, that "it is more likely than not that the Respondent has engaged in a Prohibited Practice."

3 ­ Sanctions at the World Bank and the Inter-American Development Bank: ...

53

a preponderance of evidence supports a finding that the Respondent engaged in prohibited practices. According to the procedures of the Sanctions Committee, Respondents are not entitled to a hearing, but the Sanctions Committee can decide on the basis of the written record and at its discretion whether to hold a hearing on the matter. The Sanctions Committee considers cases through panels of three members (two external, one internal) designated by the chairperson of the Committee, unless there are exceptional circumstances that merit consideration of a case by the full Committee. Decisions of the full Committee are by the favorable vote of at least five members, including the majority of the external members and the majority of the internal members. Decisions of a panel are by majority and deemed to be decisions of the Sanctions Committee. The quorum of the Sanctions Committee is constituted by a majority of internal members and a majority of external members. In the case of panels, the presence of all three members is necessary to constitute a forum. If the Sanctions Committee is satisfied with the evidence, it can impose a wide range of sanctions depending on the seriousness of the offense. A party may be reprimanded, debarred for a stated period of time or debarred permanently, thus, becoming ineligible for future contracts. A party may avoid being debarred on condition that it complies with certain preventive or remedial measures before a specified deadline or be debarred with certain conditions, upon fulfillment of which the party is released. This list is not exhaustive and other sanctions may be recommended by the Case Officer or imposed by the Sanctions Committee, for instance, restitution of funds or reimbursement of costs of the investigation or of the proceedings. The Case Officer and the Sanctions Committee may consider a number of mitigating and aggravating factors which affect the choice of sanctions. The Procedures list, for instance, the egregiousness and severity of the actions, past conduct, the magnitude of the losses caused by the respondent's actions, the quality of the evidence, cooperation with the investigation, remedial action taken by the respondent, and so on. The list is not exhaustive and the Case Officer and the Sanctions Committee may consider any other factors which they deem relevant beyond those listed in the Procedures. The deliberations of the Sanctions Committee are confidential and closed to representatives of OII or the respondent. The decision of the Sanctions Committee is final and it is delivered to the Case Officer, OII, the Respondent and the Board of Directors of the Bank. The sanction, other than a private reprimand, the prohibited practice and information concerning the identity of a sanctioned party may be disclosed to other international organizations, national authorities and the public. In fact, this information is posted on the website of the IDB.

54

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

The parties subject to sanctions may be individuals or entities, including any entity that owns directly or indirectly a sanctioned party, is owned or controlled by a sanctioned party, or is under common ownership and control with a sanctioned party. The sanction may extend to officers, employees, affiliates or agents of an entity subject to sanctions. Finally, if the Case Officer or the Chairperson of the Sanctions Committee believes that the laws of any country may have been violated, they can recommend to the President of the Bank that such matter be referred to the appropriate governmental authorities.

4. Conclusion
The World Bank and the IDB sanctions systems operate with the same end goal, to ensure that their funds are used for their intended purpose and ensure that the administrative procedures are transparent and fair. The World Bank's sanctions system is a two-tier process involving two independent bodies, the EOs and the Sanctions Board, giving Respondents notice of the allegations and two opportunities to respond. The system also includes external members and internal staff to ensure the objectivity and fairness of the process. To ensure the integrity of the sanctions system, the IDB's system is also comprised of two autonomous decision-making entities that provide two separate opportunities to review the case brought up by the Office of Institutional Integrity: the Case Officer and the Sanctions Committee. And like the World Bank's sanctions system, the IDB's Sanctions Committee consists of a combination of external members and internal staff members to ensure accountability. The sanctions systems at the World Bank and the IDB have begun to make significant headway in sanctioning those engaged in fraud and corruption. To date, the World Bank has publicly sanctioned over 500 firms and individuals, while IDB has sanctioned over 125 firms and individuals.36 This concerted effort gives both MDBs a strong mechanism to hold accountable firms engaging in sanctionable practices in development projects and creates a powerful incentive for companies to clean up their operations.

36. The list of currently debarred firms and individuals is available at World Bank Listing of Ineligible Firms, WORLD BANK, http://www.worldbank.org/sanctions (follow "Debarred Firms/Individuals" hyperlink) (last visited Feb. 1, 2012).

Transnational corruption in Brazil: the relevance of the U.S. experience with the Foreign Corrupt Practices Act

B R U C E Z A G A R I S2

Vicarious liability. 2.4. Hypotheticals. 3. U.S.-Brazil Enforcement Cooperation and Prosecutions by U.S. States on Corruption Charges

1. Introduction

A

s Brazil embarks upon its own efforts to prevent and prosecute transnational corruption, the experience of the United States government and private section with the Foreign Corrupt Practices Act (FCPA) has significance. The FCPA is significant because of the fact that the U.S. and Brazil are large markets for each other's trade and investment. In addition, the U.S. has traditionally been a place for Brazilian capital, including flight capital. There are already a number of cases brought in the U.S. in both federal and state courts arising out of allegations of Brazilian corruption. In addition, the proactive extraterritorial jurisdiction, the accounting provisions, and the use of deferred prosecution agreements all are unique elements of the FCPA that Brazil may want to consider.

1. This article is adopted from Chapter 4 (Transnational Corruption) of Bruce Zagaris, International White Collar Crime: Cases And Materials (2010, Cambridge Univ. Press). 2. Partner, Berliner Corcoran & Rowe, LLP, Washington D.C.; Editor of the International Enforcement Law Reporter.

55

56

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

In 1977, Congress enacted the Foreign Corrupt Practices Act (FCPA). For many years, as a result of the FCPA, the U.S. was the only country with laws actively prohibiting its citizens, nationals, businesses, and, in some circumstances, foreign companies that participate in its capital markets from bribing foreign officials. Since the passage of the FCPA, a number of international initiatives and conventions have resulted in widespread laws prohibiting bribery and improper inducement of foreign officials in connection with business transactions. Beginning in 1995, a number of international conventions addressing transnational corruption have entered into force. As a result, the hard law requirements preventing and criminalizing participation in transnational corruption have greatly multiplied. As a result of these conventions, international organizations and civil society groups have joined to require corporate governance initiatives focusing on anti-bribery and anti-corruption measures. In recent years corruption scandals have also contributed to the downfall of governments in Ecuador, Brazil, 3 Italy, Trinidad & Tobago,4 and India. Long-entrenched ruling parties have been weakened, including Japan's Liberal Democratic Party and Mexico's Institutional Revolutionary Party. In the United States, two decades after the Watergate scandals prompted new rules concerning political contributions and the enactment of the Foreign Corrupt Practices Act (FCPA), campaign finance reform has reemerged as a major political issue.5 The number and variety of countries facing corruption scandals highlights the complexity and prominence of corruption as a global issue. When pervasive and uncontrolled, corruption thwarts economic development and undermines political legitimacy. Less pervasive corruption result in wasted resources, increased inequity in resource distribution, diminished political competition, and greater distrust of government. Establishing and exploiting opportunities for bribery at high levels of government also increases the cost of government, distorts the allocation of government spending, and can dangerously lower the quality of infrastructure. Even relatively petty or routine corruption can deprive a government of revenues, distort economic decision making, and impose negative externalities such as increased pollution on society.6

Bruce Zagaris, Trinidad Energy Minister Resigns Due to Bribery Charges, 22 Int'l Enforcement L. Rep. 102 (Mar. 2006). 5. Kimberly Ann Elliott, Corruption as an International Policy Problem: Overview and Recommendations, in Corruption and the Global Economy (Kimberly Ann Elliott, ed.) 175 (1997). R. 2. U.S. Law and the Foreign Corrupt Practices Act
The FCPA has two principal mechanisms addressing inducements to foreign officials concerning business activities. One is a prohibition on payments to foreign officials applicable to U.S. nationals, US. businesses, publicly held companies, many foreign companies with links to the U.S., and in certain circumstances, almost anyone, provided the U.S. has jurisdiction. The second mechanism imposes accounting and record-keeping requirements on the domestic and foreign operations of publicly held companies. The FCPA, which was initially enacted in 1977, was amended in 1988 and 1998. While the purpose and language of the Act seem uncomplicated, its scope and means of application present complex questions for practitioners.

2.1. The Anti-Bribery Provisions of the FCPA
§ 78DD-1. Prohibited Foreign Trade Practices by Issues (A) Prohibition It shall be unlawful for any issuer which has a class of securities registered pursuant to section 78l of this title or which is required to file reports under section 78o(d) of this title, or for any officer, director, employee, or agent of such issuer or any stockholder thereof acting on behalf of such issuer, to make use of the mails or any means or instrumentality of interstate commerce corruptly in furtherance of an offer, payment, promise to pay, or authorization of the payment of any money, or offer, gift, promise to give, or authorization of the giving of anything of value to (1) any foreign official for purposes of ­ (A) (i) influencing any act or decision of such foreign official in his official capacity (ii) inducing such foreign official to do or omit to do any act in violation of the lawful duty of such official, or (iii) securing any improper advantage; or (B) inducing such foreign official to use his influence with a foreign government or instrumentality thereof to affect or influence any act or decision of such government or instrumentality, in order to assist such issuer in obtaining or retaining business for or with, or directing business to any person; (2) any foreign political party or official thereof or any candidate for foreign political office for purposes of ­ (A) (i) influencing any act or decision of such party, official, or candidate in its or his official capacity, (ii) inducing such party, official, or candidate to do or omit to do an act in violation of the lawful duty of such party, official, or candidate, or (iii) securing any improper advantage; or

58

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

(B) inducing such foreign official, political party, party official, or candidate to use influence with a foreign government or instrumentality thereof to affect or influence any act or decision of such government or instrumentality, in order to assist such issuer in obtaining or retaining business or with, or directing business to any person; or (3) any person, while knowing that all or a portion of such money or thing of value will be offered, given, or promised, directly or indirectly, to any foreign official, to any foreign political party or official thereof, or to any candidate for foreign political office, for purposes...s (b) Exception for routine governmental action Subsections (a) and (g) of this section shall not apply to any facilitating or expediting payment to a foreign official, political party, or party official the purpose of which is to expedite or to secure the performance of a routine governmental action by a foreign official, political party or party official. (c) Affirmative defenses It shall be an affirmative defense to actions under subsection (a) or (g) of this section that ­ (1) the payment, gift, offer, or promise of anything of value that was made, was lawful under the written laws and regulations of the foreign official's, political party's, party official's, or candidate's country; or (2) the payment, gift, offer, or promise of anything of value that was made, was a reasonable and bona fide expenditure, such as travel and lodging expenses, incurred by or on behalf of a foreign official, party, party official, or candidate and was directly related to: (A) the promotion, demonstration, or explanation of products or services; or (B) the execution or performance of a contract with a foreign government or agency thereof. Notes and Questions: 1. What Are the Essential Elements of the Anti-bribery Provisions of the FCPA? ­ A payment, offer or promise of ­ Anything of value ­ To any ­ foreign official ­ any foreign political party or party official, ­ any candidate for foreign political office, or

4 ­ Transnational corruption in Brazil: the relevance of the U.S. experience...

59

­ any other person while knowing that all or part of the payment or promise to pay will be passed on to one of the foregoing; ­ With corrupt intent; ­ For the purpose ­ influencing an official act or decision of the person ­ inducing that person to do or omit to do any act in violation of his or her lawful duty; ­ inducing that person to use his influence with a foreign government ­ to affect or influence any government act or decision; or ­ to assist in obtaining or retaining business for or with, or directing business to any person. 2. Jurisdiction The jurisdiction of the FCPA's anti-bribery provisions is quite extensive. The provisions apply to "domestic concerns" and "issuers," and to any person, including foreign individuals and entities, acting in furtherance of the improper inducement of a foreign public official while in U.S. territory. Application of the U.S. territorial principle requires some connection to U.S. territory for the prohibited activity to be subject to U.S. laws. For instance, any matter involving U.S. interstate or foreign commerce gives rise to U.S. jurisdiction. In 1998, Congress extended the FCPA to include the nationality principle as a basis for jurisdiction. As a result, jurisdiction can be based solely on the status of an individual as a U.S. national or when an entity is established under U.S. laws or has its principal place or business in the U.S. Hence, the U.S. can assert the FCPA on the basis of the principles of nationality or territoriality, or both. The anti-bribery provisions address a separate category of inducers: issuers, domestic concerns, and any individual or entity acting within the territory of the U.S. in furtherance of a prohibited inducement. 3. Payment, Offer or Promise Mr. X, a U.S. businessman, visits Assistant Deputy Minister (ADM) from Country Y with which X's company has had a contract for five years. The contract is expiring. Mr. X makes a visit to Country Y, bringing the ADM some company products, namely a corporate pen, a corporate t-shirt, and a corporate mouse pad. Mr. X tells the ADM that he wants X's company to win the contract again, when it is relet. In fact, because Mr. X's children and ADM's children have become well acquainted, he promises, if he is able to visit Country Y again in connection with the ceremony of the announcement of the new contract, he will take both his own and ADM's children on a skiing trip they have discussed

60

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

for some months. Is that promise proscribed by the FCPA? Why? Are Mr. X's Bringing the ADM products of the company proscribed by the FCPA? Why? 4. Anything of Value The FCPA does not limit what constitutes "anything of value." Value can include scholarships for family members, upgrades to first-class or business airfare, trips to resorts, employing a family member for an internship, and allowing an official to designate to whom charitable contributions are directed, especially if an official has a connection to the charity. Indeed, value may depend on the circumstances. In some impoverished countries a token gift may be enough to influence decision-making. It is important to observe that the FCPA has no de minimis exception. 5. Foreign Official The prohibitions against making or offering inducements to foreign officials apply to all branches of government as well as to all units of governments, including the civil service and political functions in countries where the functions are not one. It makes no difference whether the public official is paid or unpaid. The pivotal question is whether the inducement is in any way connected with the individual's power to exert influence in his official capacity. Hypothetical: Ms. A, an agent offers an inducement to the assistant treasurer of Party A, which has a fighting chance to win the national election, in exchange for consideration on some energy concession contracts. Is there any problem under the FCPA? Does it make any difference if Ms. A makes an inducement for Party A through a person not officially connected, such as a cousin or friend of the assistant treasurer or of a campaign worker without any title? The FCPA's prohibition of inducing foreign officials extends to political parities, party officials, or any candidate for political office. The anti-bribery provisions include "instrumentalities" of foreign governments, such as parastatals or state-owned enterprises. Hence, a foreign official can include someone who is employed by a commercial enterprise that is governmentowned or operated. The inclusion of an enterprise may be how the government characterizes the enterprise and whether it prohibits and prosecutes bribery of the employees of state-owned enterprises as public corruption. The degree of control exercised by the government over the enterprise may be important. Are inducements to officials of international organizations covered? The U.S. accords such organizations and their officials immunity under the International Organizations Immunities Act.7 If international organizations and their officials have immunity, are they covered by the FCPA?
7. 22 U.S.C. § 288.

4 ­ Transnational corruption in Brazil: the relevance of the U.S. experience...

61

6. Corrupt Intent The FCPA punishes conduct only when a payment or offer of payment is made to induce the intended beneficiary to misuse his or her official position. This requirement is analogous to U.S. domestic bribery law, which requires that the inducement be offered in exchange or return for some official action, inaction, or violation of some official duty to be corrupt. The determination of culpability arises from the intent of the person making the inducement, as opposed to the official's action, inaction, or capacity. The bribe need not be paid for a person to violate the FCPA. In addition, a person making an inducement can violate the FCPA even if the recipient or intended beneficiary did not at the relevant time have the actual authority to make or influence the official decision. 7. Obtain or Retain Business A company that induces a foreign official to engage in conduct to assist in obtaining, retaining, or directing business to the company violates the FCPA. Hence, the briber must intend to induce the official to act on the inducer's behalf to assist the individual or entity in obtaining or retaining business. From a practical standpoint, prosecutors have difficulty submitting evidence to a jury to ascertain whether an inducement had a business purpose, years after the fact. The term "assist" in the anti-bribery provisions is quite broad. Actions can assist a particular goal by facilitating achievement of the goal. For instance, payments or gifts to a government official to circumvent quotas, bypass licensing requirements, or reduce or forgive proposed taxes or penalties, would all constitute business purposes. 8. Exceptions and Affirmative Defenses The anti-bribery provisions have one category of exceptions and two categories of affirmative defenses. Each is linked to circumstances in which the inducements are made, creating in effect a safe harbor. a. Facilitating payments The anti-bribery provisions allow "facilitating," "expediting," or "grease" payments. They are limited to payments made "to expedite or to secure the performance of a routine governmental action by a foreign official, political party, or party official."8 Facilitating payments are made to obtain or accelerate performance of a non-discretionary act that an official is already obligated to perform. Examples are the issuance of a permit that is automatic or only a matter of time.
8. 15 U.S.C. §§ 78dd-1(b), -2(b), -3(b).

62

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

The payment only accelerates the act, such as obtaining the installation of telephone service. To be within the exception for facilitating payments the "routine governmental action" is limited to action that is ordinarily and commonly performed by a foreign official.9 Facilitation payments are often payments to obtain permits, licenses, or other official documents and to obtain services such as police protection, mail, telephone, utilities, cargo handling, and protection of perishable products. They may embrace payments for the processing of governmental papers, including visas and work orders; scheduling of inspections associated with contract performance or the transit of goods across country; and the expedition of shipments through customs. Because facilitating payments are rarely countenanced by the written law of a host country, they pose a risk in the host country. If they are not properly recorded, facilitating payments that may be allowed can still result in violations of the accounting and record-keeping provisions of the FCPA. In addition, legitimate facilitating payments may set the stage for a relationship with government officials where the payments eventually violate the FCPA. b. Bona Fide Business Expenses The anti-bribery provisions allow, as an affirmative defense, reasonable and bona fide expenditures.10 Such expenditures must be directly related to the promotion, demonstration, or explanation of products or services or to the execution or performance of a contract with a foreign government or agency. A company must determine whether a government official or agency can lawfully receive payment or reimbursement for expenses before such a payment can quality as a bona fide business expense. Many countries limit what can be paid and, if so, how such payments are made. Such payments may require prior approvals. Trips for promotion, demonstration or explanation of products sometimes violate the anti-bribery provisions when they involve unnecessary diversions to resorts, or travel upgrades to first class. Such payments must be necessary business expenditures and must be reasonable under the circumstances. An expense that is out of proportion or unrelated to a legitimate business purpose can serve as a basis for concern. Proper corporate governance ensures that offers to pay or reimburse reasonable expenses are carefully documented and vetted as part of an entity's compliance program. The business compliance unit should determine whether the
9. Id., §§ 78dd-1(f)(3)(A), -2(h)(4)(A), -3)(f)(4)(A). 10. Id., §§ 78dd-1(c)(2), -2(c)(2), -3(c)(2).

4 ­ Transnational corruption in Brazil: the relevance of the U.S. experience...

63

expenditures are reasonable in terms of purpose and amount, are made in good faith, and relate directly to (1) the promotion, demonstration, or explanation or products or services or (2) the execution or performance of a contract with a foreign government or agency. 9. Local Law A business can raise an affirmative defense under the anti-bribery provisions for payments or offers that are lawful under the written laws and regulations of the country of the foreign official, political party, party official, or candidate.11 Most governments do not as an official act allow payments or offers to violate a lawful duty. A person cannot assert a recognized custom or practice within a particular country as an affirmative defense. Affirmative defenses are ordinarily limited to situations where contributions to political parties or candidates for public office are at issue. 10. Opinion Procedure The Department of Justice has an FCPA Opinion Procedure that allows an issuer or a domestic concern to request an opinion concerning proposed business conduct.12 Under the opinion procedure the recipient has a rebuttable presumption that the conduct that is the subject of the opinion does not violate the anti-bribery provisions. A written opinion binds only the Justice Department and the parties to a request ­ it is non-binding for other parties. While the SEC does not have an equivalent procedure, it does not take civil enforcement action against a party that has obtained a favorable opinion from the Justice Department.13 Because the opinion process can be lengthy and costly, Justice Department officials recommend that prospective requestors meet informally with them before they make a formal request. These informal meetings can help determine whether a company should formally request an opinion and, if so, how to narrow the request and what information to provide. The information provided in an opinion request is exempt from disclosure under the Freedom of Information Act. However, the Justice Department has the right to make a public release describing the requestor, identifying the country, summarizing the proposed conduct, and announcing the Justice Department's action. The Justice Department posts the opinion procedure releases on its Web site, which is a source of useful guidance on structuring
