2.2. The Accounting and Record-Keeping Provisions of the FCPA
§ 78M. Periodical and Other Reports (a) Reports by issuer of security; contents Every issuer of a security registered pursuant to section 78l of this title shall file with the Commission, in accordance with such rules and regulations as the Commission may prescribe as necessary or appropriate for the proper protection of investors and to insure fair dealing in the security ­ (1) such information and documents (and such copies thereof) as the Commission shall require to keep reasonably current the information and documents required to be included in or filed with an application or registration statement filed pursuant to section 78l of this title, except that the Commission may not require the filing of any material contract wholly executed before July 1, 1962. (2) such annual reports (and such copies thereof), certified if required by the rules and regulations of the Commission by independent public accountants, and such quarterly reports (and such copies thereof), as the Commission may prescribe. Ever issuer of a security registered on a national securities exchange shall also file a duplicate original of such information, documents, and reports with the exchange. (b) Form of report; books, records, and internal accounting; directives (2) Every issuer which has a class of securities registered pursuant to section 78l of this title and every issuer which is required to file reports pursuant to section 78o(d) of this title shall (A) make and keep books, records, and accounts, which, in reasonable detail, accurately and fairly reflect the transactions and dispositions of the assets of the issuer; and (B) devise and maintain a system of internal accounting controls sufficient to provide reasonable assurances that ­ (I) transactions are executed in accordance with management's general or specific authorization; (II) transactions are recorded as necessary (i) to permit preparation of financial statements in conformity with generally accepted

14. Department of Justice, FCPA Opinion Procedure Releases, http://www.usdoj.gov/criminal/ fraud/fcpa/opiindx.htm.

4 ­ Transnational corruption in Brazil: the relevance of the U.S. experience...

65

accounting principles or any other criteria applicable to such statements, and (ii) to maintain accountability for assets; (III) access to assets is permitted only in accordance with management's general or specific authorization; and (iv) the recorded accountability for assets is compared with the existing assets at reasonable intervals and appropriate action is taken with respect to any differences. (5) No person shall knowingly circumvent or knowingly fail to implement a system of internal accounting controls or knowingly falsify any book, record, or account described in paragraph (2). Notes and Questions: 1. Scope The accounting and record-keeping provisions, which are more limited than the anti-bribery provisions, only apply to issuers. Officers, directors, employees, and stockholders or agents of the issuer acting on its behalf are subject to the terms of the accounting and record-keeping provisions. Individuals and entities may also be subject to the accounting and record-keeping provisions if they are accomplices to a violation of those provisions. Because the accounting and record-keeping provisions require issuers to maintain accurate records of their transactions and of the disposition of their assets, these provisions apply without regard to whether foreign conduct, foreign officials, or improper inducements are involved. The provisions apply to an issuer's domestic and foreign operations, including domestic reporting and disclosure practices as well as practices involving foreign payments. They create an affirmative duty on the part of issuers and their officers, directors, employees, and agents or stockholders acting on behalf of the issuer. The accounting and record-keeping provisions do not apply to foreign subsidiaries if the U.S. issuer holds an interest of 50 percent or less in the foreign entity. However, the issuer still must "proceed in good faith to use its influence to the extent reasonable under the circumstances to cause [the affiliate] to devise and maintain a system of internal accounting controls" consistent with the requirements of the accounting and record-keeping provisions.15 Reasonable in context depends mainly on the practices governing the affiliate in the country where it is located. An issuer showing good faith efforts to use its influence will be "conclusively presumed" to have fulfilled its statutory obligation.16

15. 15 U.S.C. § 78m(b)(6). 16. Id.

66

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

The 1983 case of SEC v. World-Wide Coin Investment, Ltd. is one of the few cases that litigated the accounting provisions of the FCPA, providing the only example of judicial interpretation of the FCPA prior to its 1988 amending.17 Most other cases on the accounting provisions were resolved before trial by consent decree or other settlement.18 2. The Record-Keeping Provisions Under the record-keeping provisions an issuer must "make and keep books, records, and accounts which, in reasonable detail, accurately and fairly reflect the transactions and dispositions of the asserts of the issuer."19 An issuer's records must show transactions in conformity with accepted accounting standards and should be designed to prevent off-the-books transactions such as kickbacks and bribes. "Reasonable detail" is "such level of detail and degree of assurance as would satisfy prudent officials in the conduct of their own affairs."20 An important feature of the provisions is that they apply to all payments, not just to sums that would be material in the traditional financial context. Traditionally, except for disclosures as to certain aspects of an issuer's activities, materiality was the overriding determining factor as to what required disclosure and as to what constituted a violation. a. Falsification of Books and Records An issuer's management must ensure that its books and records are accurate so that the entity can prepare financial statements in conformity with accepted procedures. The SEC has issued two rules to ensure that the accounting books of original entry, ledgers, and other accounting data are maintained to the extent reasonably necessary to support the financial statements and to allow independent auditors to apply accepted audit procedures. Rule 13b2-1 forbids the falsification of books and records that must be kept under the record-keeping provisions.21 It applies to "any person" and is not limited to officers and directors of an issuer. Rule 13b2-1 has no materiality requirement. Additionally, books and records are defined broadly to include "accounts, correspondence, memoranda, tapes, discs, papers, books, and other documents or transcribed information of any type, whether expressed in ordinary or machine language."22
17. SEC v. World-Wide Coin Investments, Ltd., 567 F.Supp. 724 (N.D. Ga. 1983). 18. Kathleen A. Lacey and Barbara Crutchfield George, Expansion off SEC Authority into Internal Corporate Governance: The Accounting Provisions of the Foreign Corrupt Practices Act, 7 J. Transnat'l L. & Pol'y 119, 121-22 (1998). 19. Id., § 78m(b)(2)(A). 20. Id., § 78m(b)(7). 21. 17 C.F.R. § 240.13b2-1. 22. 15 U.S.C. § 78c(a)(37).

4 ­ Transnational corruption in Brazil: the relevance of the U.S. experience...

67

Manipulating books or records to mask transactions by obliquely characterizing or actually falsifying them, can lead to serious legal exposure for an issuer and those individuals involved. For instance, concealing a transaction may subject the issuer and other responsible persons to enforcement action for violating Rule 13b2-1. In recent years, the SEC has emphasized that it has "zero tolerance" for the falsification of records relating to an improper payment. b. Misrepresentations to Auditors Rule 13b2-2 prohibits any officer or director from making materially false or misleading statements or omitting to state any material facts in the preparation of filings required by the Exchange Act. While the rule applies only to officers and directors, it applies to internal auditors as well as to outside auditors, and covers "causing another person to make a material misstatement or make or cause to be made a materially false or misleading statement."23 Following enactment of the Sarbanes-Oxley Act of 2002, the SEC enlarged Rule 13b2-2. As a result, officers and directors of an issuer, or anyone acting on their behalf are prohibited from "taking any action to fraudulently influence, coerce, manipulate, or mislead any independent public or certified accountant engaged in the performance of an audit of the financial statements of that issuer for the purpose of rendered such financial statements materially misleading."24 3. The Internal Accounting Controls Provisions The accounting provisions of the FCPA seek to prevent the improper use of an issuer's assets. They require issuers to develop and maintain internal accounting controls that provide reasonable assurance that transactions are executed in accordance with management's general or specific authorization; that transactions are recorded as necessary to allow the preparation of financial statements in conformity with generally accepted accounting principles or any other criteria applicable to such statements, and to maintain accountability for assets; that access to company assets is allowed only in accordance with management's general or specific authorization; and that records are compared with existing assets at reasonable intervals and appropriate action is taken with respect to any differences. The SEC has construed "reasonable assurance" of management control over an issuer's assets as "such level of detail and degree of assurance as would satisfy prudent officials in the conduct of their own affairs."25 The "prudent man" standard is analogous to management's expectations concerning their oversight
23. 17 C.F.R. § 240.13b2-2. 24. 15 U.S.C. §7242; 17 C.F.R. § 240.13b2-2(b). 25. Id., §78m(b)(7).

68

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

obligations under U.S. law, and does not require any specific type internal accounting controls. The standard for compliance is whether a system, taken as a whole, reasonably fulfils the statute's goals. The SEC has identified elements of an adequate system of internal controls, which it looks for when considering specific systems. Among other things, the SEC says that: the board of directors must oversee the establishment and maintenance of strong internal accounting controls; the board must identify a method of analyzing the system of accounting controls that is effective within the context of each entity; and management must have reasonable assurance that the system of internal accounting is functioning as designed. An effective system of internal accounting controls has a range of review and approval guidelines designed to detect and deter questionable payments. The planning, implementation, and monitoring of an issuer's compliance program should be closely connected, if not intertwined, with its system of internal accounting controls. In World-Wide Coin, the Court's greatest concern was that the company's independent auditors had alerted it to serious weaknesses in its internal controls that constituted potential FCPA violations, including no adequate segregation of duties, no documentation to support transactions, and accounting records that were inadequate and improperly filed. Although notified of these deficiencies and possible FCPA violations by the auditor's opinion letter, World-Wide Coin management took no corrective action. The Court later found World-Wide Coin in violation of Section 13(b)(2) of the FCPA and ordered a full fraud accounting and disgorgement of wrongfully received benefits by defendant Hale.26

2.3. Vicarious Liability
An individual or entity can suffer vicarious liability for the conduct of a third party when the third party is acting for or on behalf of the individual or entity. Actions by an agent, consultant, or representative can subject an individual or entity to liability. Despite a third party's not being subject to the FCPA, an individual or entity can become subject to vicarious liability if the individual or entity authorizes, directs, or in some way ratifies activity that is prohibited by the FCPA. The knowledge requirement for establishing vicarious liability is important and varies according to whether the anti-bribery or the accounting or record-keeping provisions are involved and whether criminal or civil charges are brought. One basis of liability occurs when express provisions exist for vicarious liability as part of the statutory mandate. Both the FCPA criminal and civil anti-bribery provisions establish vicarious liability. Implicitly, the accounting
26. World-Wide Coin, 567 F. Supp. at 751-52, 760; Lacey and George, supra, at 141.

4 ­ Transnational corruption in Brazil: the relevance of the U.S. experience...

69

and record-keeping civil provisions establish vicarious liability. A second basis for vicarious liability is the general one in all U.S. statutory provisions for accomplices and aiders and abettors. 1. The Anti-Bribery Provisions Offers or payments are expressly prohibited to "any person, while knowing that all or a portion of such money or things of value will be offered, given, or promised, directly or indirectly, to any foreign official, to any foreign political party or official thereof, or to any candidate for foreign political office."27 These provisions apply to anyone in the U.S. or abroad who acts on behalf of an individual or entity subject to the terms of anti-bribery provisions, embracing consultants, distributors, joint venture partners, foreign subsidiaries or affiliates, contractors, or subcontractors. An offeror can be vicariously liable under the anti-bribery provisions with respect to improper payments made by or through a third party to obtain or return business when anything of value is offered or paid to a third party where the offeror knows that all or a portion of such value is or will be offered, given, or promised, directly or indirectly, to a foreign official. Vicarious liability also exists where a third party is authorized to offer or pay anything of value to a foreign official. The only difference in terms of vicarious liability between criminal and civil enforcement actions is that the standard of proof is a preponderance of evidence in a civil context and beyond a reasonable double in a criminal context. a. The Knowledge Requirement The anti-bribery provisions prohibit promising or giving benefits to a third party when the offeror knows that the benefits will be passed on by the third party to a foreign official. An individual or entity is responsible for the conduct of a third party when it "knows" that the money or thing of value given to the third party would be used, directly or indirectly, to make an improper payment. If an individual or entity "is aware of a high probability of the existence of" activity prohibited by the anti-bribery provisions but does not have actual knowledge of the circumstance, the individual or entity is still deemed to "know" of the existence of the circumstance.28 The FCPA deems an individual or entity to have the requisite knowledge of any activity of a third party if the individual or entity (1) "is aware that such person is engaging in such conduct, that such circumstance exists, or that such result is substantially certain to occur" or (2)
27. 15 U.S.C. §§ 78dd-1(a)(3), -2(a)(3), -3(a)(3). 28. Id., §§ 78dd-1(f)(2)(B); -2(h)(3)(B); -3(f)(3)(B).

70

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

"has a firm belief that such circumstance exists or that such result is substantially certain to occur." In a trial knowledge can be inferred under the anti-bribery provisions when facts show that the act occurs with conscious disregard of or willful blindness to the evident purpose of the offer or payment. Prosecutors try to establish the existence of such knowledge when they can show circumstances that should raise suspicions or red flags. In such cases knowledge on the part of an individual or entity will most likely be established ­ for instance, making a payment to someone who did not appear to perform a service or making a payment grossly disproportionate to the value of the services to be delivered. A payor telling a recipient that he will never need to know how the services for which he is paying are rendered raises another red flag. In 1988, amendments to the FCPA removed the "reason to know" standard, which, albeit never applied by the Justice Department, was perceived as possibly including situations where an individual or entity negligently disregarded the risk that a sales agent might use payments made to bribe a foreign official by an individual or entity subject to the anti-bribery provisions. However, there is little practical difference between the current "knowledge" standard and the old "reason to know" standard. Knowledge is imputed under the anti-bribery provisions to an individual or entity with information indicating a "high probability" that prohibited conduct may result. Knowledge can also be established if an individual or entity consciously disregards or deliberately ignores circumstances that should reasonably have alerted the individual or entity to a high probability of violence. Hence, if an individual or entity becomes aware of questionable circumstances concerning the activities of a third party with whom a relationship may exist, diligence must be exercised in undertaking an inquiry, at the risk that an individual or entity could be found to have consciously disregarded information that could have served as notice of the likelihood of a violation. b. Authorization In addition to prohibiting an inducement to a foreign official, the antibribery provisions also prohibit the "authorization" of an improper inducement to be made by another.29 Such prohibitions embrace, among others, sales representatives, consultants, and foreign subsidiaries. While the anti-bribery provisions do not define the standard for authorization, legislative history shows that authorization can be either explicit or implicit. Authorization appears to mean approval or direction to carry out the
29. 15 U.S.C. §§ 78dd-1(a), -2(a), -3(a).

4 ­ Transnational corruption in Brazil: the relevance of the U.S. experience...

71

conduct. Because implicit authorization is proscribed, when an individual or entity becomes aware of possible improper payments to third parties, he/it should establish a record to show that such conduct was not authorized, and should document explicit objection to such improper conduct if possible. Authorization may include knowing acquiescence or tacit approval by individuals or entities that could have prevented the conduct leading to the making of an improper inducement. c. Control If a foreign corporation is not an issuer subject to the FCPA, the anti-bribery provisions do not generally apply to foreign corporations, including controlled foreign affiliates of U.S. entities that are subject to the FCPA. If a foreign subsidiary makes an improper inducement, it is not in itself a violation of the anti-bribery provisions unless an act in furtherance of the improper inducement occurs within the U.S. territory. Hence, officers, directors, employees, and agents of foreign affiliates are not subject to the anti-bribery provisions if these individuals or entities are neither domestic concerns nor issuers. However, an issuer of a domestic concern can be vicariously liable for the conduct of its foreign subsidiary if the issuer directs, authorizes or knowingly acquiesces to the foreign subsidiary's prohibited conduct in some way. 2. The Accounting and Record-Keeping Provisions Issuers may be held strictly liable in a civil enforcement case for the actions of controlled subsidiaries or foreign affiliations for violations of the accounting and record-keeping provisions. The SEC need not prove that an issuer knew or even suspected wrongful conduct on the part of its controlled subsidiary or affiliate to establish civil liability. The 1988 FCPA amendments narrowed the knowledge requirement under the accounting and record-keeping procedures. Criminal liability can be established if an individual or entity subject to the accounting and record-keeping provisions knowingly circumvents or fails to implement a system of internal accounting controls or knowingly falsifies any book, record, or account.30 An individual or entity can be criminally liable for acts of third parties if an individual or entity had knowledge that the third party has circumvented the internal controls or falsified books and records. Deliberate ignorance or knowing disregard can establish the requisite knowledge, especially if an individual or entity becomes aware of questionable circumstances.

30. Id., § 78m(b)(4)-(5).

72

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

3. Vicarious Liability for Accomplices Individuals and entities may also be secondarily criminally liable under the federal conspiracy and aiding and abetting statutes for violations of the antibribery or the accounting and record-keeping provision. The prosecutor must show that individual's or entity's knowledge coupled with either a conspiratorial agreement 31 or actions that aid or abet 32 a violation in connection with prohibited conduct on the part of a third party.

2.4. Hypotheticals
1. A U.S. company wants to sell products to a foreign state enterprise. However, properly demonstrating the production process and after-sale services requires bringing the key decision-makers to the U.S. plant. The U.S. company does not have a plant abroad. The government officials do not have a budget for travel and it is considering products from a foreign competitor who have wined and dined government officials in its country. What, if anything, can a U.S. company do? A U.S. company can arrange for foreign government officials to visit to demonstrate its product. The FCPA creates an affirmative defense to anti-bribery liability for payments that are "reasonable and bona fide expenditure(s), such as travel and lodging expenses, incurred by or on behalf of a foreign official" that are "directly related" to (A) "the promotion, demonstration or explanation of products or services;" or (B) "the execution or performance of a contract with a foreign government or agency thereof."33 The defense clarifies that bona fide expenses connected with promotional activities are lawful. Because the tentative visit will take the group to Atlanta and they expressed great interest in visiting Disney World, one of the company marketing people suggests routing the group through Orlando and paying their way to Orlando. Is this a violation of the FCPA? It does seem to go beyond a bona fide expense and may be deemed as an improper payment. What if the company schedules two meetings over breakfast and lunch in Orlando to discuss the operation of its products? This would also seem to violate the bona fide requirement unless there is an overriding business reason to meet in Orlando. What if the company wants to take the officials to lunch and/or dinner in Atlanta to explain the products and services? This should be proper. If the company learns that the government officials like to have the company of "loose women" when they go abroad, can the company arrange some
31. 18 U.S.C. § 371. 32. Id. at § 2. 33. U.S.C. §§ 78dd-1 (c)(2); 78dd-2(c)(2).

4 ­ Transnational corruption in Brazil: the relevance of the U.S. experience...

73

prostitutes? Absolutely not ­ these expenses clearly would not be bona fide. If a company official volunteers that his niece or niece's friend loves foreigners, especially from the foreign officials country, can the company official arrange for the niece or niece's friend to accompany the foreign official(s)? This arrangement should be appropriate, especially if no arrangement is made for sex or any favors of this kind. It would clearly be a violation of the FCPA if the company or anyone associated with the company paid the niece or niece's friend any money to accompany the foreign official. Proper company policy may want to avoid even the appearance of impropriety and advise against the arrangement. The company produces pens and t-shirts and a variety of consumer (e.g., food and beverages) and entertainment products, which it is trying to sell the foreign government. When the foreign government officials visit, the marketing department has suggested they should be given pens, t-shirts, food and beverages, CDs and videos of the famous and current movies and albums produced by the U.S. company. Is this a violation of the FCPA? A gift of those products that relate to the proposed sales, especially if they promote, demonstrate or explain the products or services, is proper. The company should carefully consider and document that the products and/or services meet the statutory requirements. 2. A U.S. company that would like to do business in Hungary has arranged an appointment with some high-level Hungarian officials to discuss sales of some new products. The U.S. company is told that it is customary to bring gifts to such meetings, and that it will be difficult to do business without gifts. The general counsel consults you about purchasing a gift. Is it appropriate to bring some nominal gift? This is a gray area. Congress has clarified that if a payment or gift is "corruptly made, in order to obtain an official act or omission," then "it cannot be bona fide, good-faith payment, and this defense would not be available."34 Hence, this exception is in a certain sense not a true affirmative defense since it does not apply where the basic elements of the anti-bribery prohibition have been fulfilled. As noted above, because the FCPA has no "de minimus" rule, if the U.S. company brings a gift that has any value, such that it may be considered to influence Hungarian officials to make a decision about the U.S. company's products, then it may be a violation of the FCPA. To be on the safe side, the U.S. company would have to bring a gift that is so nominal that it would be questionable what, if any value, it would have. The safest course of action legally

34. See H.R. Conf. Rep. N º 100-576 at 922 (1988).

74

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

would be to not bring a gift unless the company already has products they make for demonstration and marketing. What would happen if the U.S. company brings the gift and shortly thereafter wins a new no-bid contract worth $1 million a year for the next five years. You are approached by a Czech company that believes it has lost the contract due to the gifts, which it heard about through personal connections subsequent to the contract award. What if, any rights, might the Czech company have? 3. When a U.S. company receives information that it is customary to bring some gifts for meetings with government officials, is there any way to verify the information and also determine whether such conduct is permitted under local law? Yes. U.S. or foreign counsel can try to ascertain what the local law on procurement and bribery state. Increasingly, the OECD and similar international organizations interested in anti-corruption maintain Web sites covering anti-bribery laws. Another useful reference is Transparency International (TI). For instance, in the case of Hungary, counsel can refer to the TI Web site to contact the organization's local chapter, which was at the Chamber of Commerce. Within 24 hours, we received an answer that Hungary has no custom requiring visitors to bring gifts to government officials. Local counsel is also a good resource. Hypotheticals 4 through 7 concern XYZ Widget Co., a Delaware corporation, which has targeted the Kingdom of Ebenezer as a new export markets. The Kingdom is starting its transition to a modern market economy, but the ruling family still control the government. Widgets are critical to the infrastructure projects that the Kingdom is considering. 4. XYZ has sent Mr. J., a marketing representative, to the Kingdom and has given the representative an overall budget. Mr. J arrives in the kingdom and seeks to open XYZ's office. He finds that all of his computer and communications equipment, as well as corporate samples and literature ­ worth over $20,000 ­ are being held at the airport by customs officials. Although he specifically checked and ascertained that no duties are applied to such corporate equipment, the customs official informs him that he must make a cash payment of $50 to receive the equipment. Would it matter if the cash payment were $500 or $1,000? What should Mr. J. do? ­ pay the money and record the expense as "miscellaneous"? ­ pay the money and record the expense as "facilitating payment"? ­ intervene with the host government? It seems that Mr. J should inquire further about the reason for the refusal to release the equipment. If the customs official is overwhelmed with responsibilities and $50 is needed to prioritize the processing of the equipment, then $50

4 ­ Transnational corruption in Brazil: the relevance of the U.S. experience...

75

is probably an appropriate "facilitating payment" to have the customs official perform a "routine governmental action," which is one of the exceptions to an improper payment. However, it is important that the payment be accurately reflected on the books and records of XYZ. $500 or $1,000 is more difficult to justify because of the relationship to the value of the merchandise ­ in this case, over $20,000). 5. Mr. J decides to recommend the hiring of a local representative as an agent. He learns that other foreign companies use members of the ruling family who have no "official" role but market themselves as "consultants" who can provide access to the high-level procurement decision-makers within the government. One of Mr. J's contacts recommends Mr. A, the son of the Director of Procurement for Ebenezer's Ministry of Public Works. What should Mr. J do about the recommendation? 6. Hiring Mr. A, the son of the Director of Procurement for a Ministry with which XYZ Co. is likely to do business would be a red flag and should be avoided, if possible, or scrutinized very carefully. Hiring a family member or a relative of a high-level official or official who will make procurement decisions is an indication that payments may actually go to the decision-maker or may influence improperly his decision because of his direct or indirect benefit of the payments. (See the discussion above of due diligence program.) Mr. J may want to perform more investigation and check with any references from the U.S. Government in Ebenezer, obtain a World Trader Data Report, and check with any local (e.g., Chamber of Commerce) and trade associations (e.g., American-Ebenezer Chamber of Commerce) in Ebenezer. Before hiring any agent, Mr. J may want to develop a checklist of duties and obligations along with compliance obligations for the prospective agent and interview personally any likely candidates for the job. Mr. J may want to visit the offices and meet the staff of likely candidates to ascertain whether they have adequate resources to perform the job. Mr. J may want to hire an investigative firm in Ebenezer to ascertain the background and reputation of any final candidate(s), and follow other guidelines of the due diligence program. 7. In negotiating the commission rate, the proposed local agent asks for a commission of 15 percent on all sales. Mr. J learns that commissions in XYZ's widget sector in this geographical area range from 7.5 percent to 10 percent. Should Mr. J agree to pay 15 percent? Mr. J should confront the proposed local agent with the information about the normal commission range. Unless the proposed local agent can provide an explanation detailing how market difficulties justify the higher rate, Mr. J should refuse to pay it. Mr. J should also note that payment of almost double the normal commission may indicate a bad economic arrangement for XYZ Co.,

76

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

and consider the possibly that the proposed local agent may be contemplating some unusual activity ­ such as bribery ­ to perform the job. Mr. J should document his negotiations with the agent regarding the commission rate, the amount XYZ ultimately pays the agent, and why he believes the amount is reasonable and consistent with the market in Ebenezer. 8. In negotiating the agreement with the proposed local agent, the local agent requests all commissions in cash. What should Mr. J do? Cash payments are a red flag and should be avoided. (See the discussion of the due diligence program). Mr. J should ascertain the reason for preference for cash payments and explain that any payments will be accurately recorded on the books and records of XYZ and with the requirements of the U.S. and Ebenezer laws, especially their respective income tax laws. Mr. J should also inquire about the laws and practices of Ebenezer with as many of the above-referenced sources as possible. What if the proposed local agent requests all payments to a numbered account in an offshore bank in Antigua? Such a request would be a red flag because companies should avoid paying consultants outside of the country where services are rendered, unless local counsel has provided an opinion that such conduct is legal in the jurisdiction in question. Additionally, the request to pay commissions into a numbered account in an offshore bank in Antigua is suspect because since 1999 the U.S. and U.K. governments have warned their banks about doing business with Antiguan financial institutions due to insufficient supervision. Mr. J should ascertain the reason for preference for payments into a numbered account in Antigua and explain that any payments will be accurately recorded on the books and records of XYZ and with the requirements of the U.S. and Ebenezer laws, especially their respective income tax laws.

3. U.S. ­ Brazil Enforcement Cooperation and Prosecutions by U.S. States on Corruption Charges
When either the U.S. or Brazil governments want to cooperate on corruption-related cases, they have a rich variety of mechanisms. They can use modern Mutual Assistance in Criminal Matters Treaty, an extradition treaty, Memoranda of Understandings on both securities and commodities future trading. The two countries have signed a tax information exchange agreement that awaits Brazilian ratification before it takes effect. In addition, there exists a robust number of law enforcement officials cooperating in each other's country over a variety of offenses, including drug trafficking, organized crime, money laundering, and white collar crime. An important consideration in anti-corruption enforcement is that state prosecutors are quite robust in conceptualizing their mandates and their jurisdiction

4 ­ Transnational corruption in Brazil: the relevance of the U.S. experience...

77

remit. For instance, on March 9, 2007, Manhattan District Attorney Robert Morgenthau announced the indictment of Paulo Maluf, former governor and mayor of São Paulo, Brazil, along with four co-conspirators. Prosecutors accuse Maluf, 75, of masterminding a scheme to steal and launder more than $11.6 million from a Brazilian public works project.35 The indictment charges Paulo Maluf, currently a federal deputy in the lower house of Brazil's National Congress, with participating in an over-invoicing and kickback conspiracy that generated millions of dollars in criminal proceeds. The indictment alleges that during the period from January 1993 through August 2004 the stolen funds were illegally transferred to a bank account in New York secretly controlled by Maluf, and then wired to another account under Maluf's control in Jersey through the Durant International Corp. The indictment alleges that Durant is controlled through "offshore shell entities and offshore trusts from the British Virgin Islands." According to the indictment, Maluf used kickback funds to pay for personal expenses in the U.S. and Brazil, and to finance political campaigns in Brazil. 36 The indictment alleges that the Malufs obtained the kickbacks through cash payments and the transmission of funds through doleiros to accounts they controlled in New York. Some of the money was paid through wire transfers sent to an account at Safra National Bank in Manhattan, maintained under the code name "Chanani." According to Daniel Castleman, chief of investigations in the Manhattan district attorney's office, Jersey authorities have frozen about $26 million. Authorities in Brazil and New York are trying to repatriate the money to Brazil. Although Brazil may not extradite Maluf because the Brazilian Constitution prohibits extradition of citizens, Mr. Morganthau has said that his office will try to arrest Maluf if he leaves Brazil ­ effectively confining him to the country.37 Morganthau noted that his office cooperated with law enforcement agencies in Brazil and Jersey, using court orders to share evidence and working to trace and freeze assets. According to Morgenthau the public works project, budgeted at $200 million, wound up costing $600 million, with the kickbacks amounting to 20 percent of the cost.38 Since 2004, the Manhattan District Attorney's office has investigated a number of illegal money transmitter cases involving the tri-border region of South America comprised of Brazil, Argentina and Paraguay. The latest indictments

Id. 38. D.A.'s News Release, supra; Karen Freifeld, Maluf, Brazilian Politician, Indicted in New York, Bloomberg, Mar. 8, 2007.

support U.S. efforts to prosecute public corruption and related money laundering. The U.S., especially financial institutions in New York and Miami, are receptacles of money from Brazil. However, U.S. and Brazilian law enforcement agencies are increasingly using the Mutual Assistance in Criminal Matters Treaty and a number of other law enforcement agreements, as well as the growing experience of Brazilian officials in the Asset Recovery and International Cooperation Unit of the Brazilian Ministry of Justice, to investigate and prosecute transnational public corruption cases.39

1. Introduction

T

he U.S. Foreign Corrupt Practices Act ("FCPA"), when adopted in 1977, was the first example of a national effort to regulate foreign, or transnational, bribery. Today, it is but one of dozens of such laws adopted by countries around the globe, including Brazil.
1. Partner, Steptoe & Johnson, LLP; head of FCPA and Anti-Corruption practice; Board of Directors and Executive Committee member, Transparency International-USA. 2. Former Associate attorney, Steptoe & Johnson, LLP, in FCPA and Anti-Corruption practice group; past Vice President, Inter-American Bar Association Committee XIX on Corruption and Money Laundering.

79

80

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

Yet despite this harmonization, the FCPA remains the statute with the most extensive extraterritorial impact, active enforcement, and profound compliance implications. This article will focus on the FCPA's current enforcement, and identified future trends, with a particular emphasis on its application to Brazilian entities and to business activities in and involving Brazil. The FCPA as drafted and enforced applies to a wide range of business dealings and range of entities involved in inbound and outbound business in Brazil, both U.S. and foreign. This includes business dealings of covered firms and individuals with state-owned enterprises ("SOEs") and other governmental bodies in Brazil. The statute also applies to a variety of sources of foreign investment in Brazil ­ U.S. companies, U.S. hedge funds and private equity funds, and issuers of shares in the United States; as such, its requirements also are relevant to Brazilian companies seeking to attract such investment. As Brazilian business becomes increasingly engaged internationally, the impact of the FCPA and the compliance expectations it generates will have even greater impact on Brazilian business than is the case today. After providing a brief summary of the FCPA, Part I will review trends in FCPA enforcement, including significant recent cases involving Brazil (such as Nature's Sunshine and Universal Leaf ) that illustrate how other U.S. laws are being deployed in FCPA cases in the service of aggressive enforcement. Part II will discuss the broad meaning courts have given to the "obtaining, retaining or directing business" element of the FCPA anti-bribery offense, as including operational activities of companies, not just procurement, public contracts, or key licenses or concessions. The associated impact on the types of business activities, as well as the types of third-party relationships that raise FCPA risks, is explored. Part III will discuss several recent U.S. court decisions that seek to clarify the test for what type of SOE is an "instrumentality" of a government, making its officials "foreign officials" for FCPA purposes. These decisions provide some guidance for determining what types of SOEs are covered by the FCPA. As a result, they are relevant to FCPA-regulated concerns that deal with SOEs, whether in Brazil or in third countries.

2. The FCPA and Its Enforcement
2.1. Brief Summary of the FCPA's Provisions
As explained in Chapter 4, the FCPA includes anti-bribery provisions and, for issuers of securities on a U.S. exchange, accounting requirements. The

5 ­ The U.S. Foreign Corrupt Practices Act: past, present, and future

81

elements and requirements of these provisions are discussed in Chapter 4, and will only be recited in summary form here for convenience. The anti-bribery provisions of the FCPA criminalize the bribery by covered persons of foreign government officials, officials of public international organizations, political party officials, political parties, and candidates for political office to obtain or retain business or an improper advantage, whether carried out directly by a covered person or indirectly through third parties. 15 U.S.C. §§ 78dd-1(a), 78dd-2(a), 78dd-3(a). The specific elements of the anti-bribery prohibitions, as well as the provisions' affirmative defenses for promotion of goods and services and for execution and performance of a government contract, and the exception for facilitating payments, are discussed in detail in Chapter 4. It is important to emphasize, however, that the anti-bribery provisions ­ which are primarily penal in nature ­ apply both to companies and individuals. They also apply not only to payments made directly to foreign officials by an individual or a company's employees, but also to payments and other transfers of value made to third parties (including joint venture partners, agents, representatives, brokers, and others, including professional advisors) while "knowing" that some or all of the value would be passed to a foreign official. One of the most significant features of the FCPA is that "knowing" is defined broadly to include awareness of a "high probability" of a pass-through payment, offer, or promise.3 As a result, companies can be liable for the conduct of agents and others acting on their behalf, if they make a payment to the agent or other third party while being aware of a high probability it will result in a pass-through payment. In effect, if there are signs of a likely payment by a third party to a foreign official or other prohibited recipient ­ or what are called "red flags" ­ which are not appropriately addressed, this may increase the risk of a violation by the company. This outcome is sometimes called "vicarious liability" because the company is being held responsible for the actions of a third party.4 Many FCPA cases in recent years have involved dealings with third parties. Oversight of the selection and performance of third parties is one of the most important areas of FCPA compliance. Notably, a recent case involving Frederic Bourke, a private investor in an oil investment opportunity in Azerbaijan, explored the contours of the knowledge standard.5
3. 15 U.S.C. §§ 78dd-1(f)(2)(B), 78dd-2(h)(3)(B), 78dd-3(f)(3)(B). 4. Companies also have vicarious liability for the acts of their employees. The test for such liability is generally whether the employee was acting within the scope of his or her authority and whether the actions were at least in part for the benefit of the corporation. Under U.S. laws, even the actions of a relatively low-level employee may trigger FCPA liability. 5. The U.S. Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit upheld the instruction the trial court gave to the jury that Mr. Bourke could be convicted based upon "conscious avoidance", rather than actual knowledge of bribery. United States v. Bourke, case No. 09-4704 (2d. Circ.) (Slp Op. of Dec. 14, 2011 at 13) (citing trial court's jury instruction that "[k]nowledge may be proven in this manner if,

82

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

The FCPA's jurisdictional reach has expanded significantly since 1998. That year saw the adoption of alternative nationality jurisdiction for "U.S. persons­U.S. citizens and permanent residents, and all forms of business organized under U.S. law; the addition of a new anti-bribery prohibition focusing on "any person" whose acts further bribery with the requisite territorial connection to the United States; and the elimination of any need for a foreign person who is an officer, director, employee, agent or shareholder of a covered entity to be otherwise subject to U.S. jurisdiction in order to be liable for criminal penalties.6 The result has been fewer and fewer cases where jurisdiction is a barrier to prosecution. While the primary focus of this chapter is the anti-bribery provisions ­ often described as the core of the FCPA ­ we also will explore the significance of the accounting requirements. The accounting requirements mandate that "issuers"­SEC reporting companies ­ maintain complete and accurate books and records, as well as a system of internal controls over payments, for themselves and majority-owned and controlled affiliates.7 These requirements do not have a materiality threshold, and no scienter or wrongful state of mind is necessary for enforcement action to be brought by the Securities and Exchange Commission ("SEC"), leading them to be described as "strict liability" standards.8 They also apply to all transactions of an issuer, wherever they occur, and whether they involve the issuer or its controlled affiliates. As a result, they can be used by the SEC in cases where bribery cannot be proved by a preponderance of the evidence, or where there is no jurisdiction under the anti-bribery provisions. The U.S. Department of Justice ("DoJ") and SEC both have enforcement jurisdiction over the FCPA within their respective spheres. The SEC's jurisdiction is limited to issuers and persons acting on behalf of or connected to issuers (officers, directors, employees, shareholders, and agents) and is civil and administrative in nature, while the DoJ has civil and criminal jurisdiction over all persons subject to the statute. The two agencies typically cooperate closely where the jurisdiction of both is implicated but may each individually sanction the same conduct. Indeed, one reason for the high level of penalties today is the SEC's insistence on the disgorgement of profits as well as fines and penalties, coupled
but only if, the person suspects the fact, realized it's high probability, but refrained from obtaining the final confirmation because he wanted to be able to deny knowledge.''). 6. Criminal enforcement of the accounting provisions, which has occurred with increasing frequency in recent years, especially against foreign companies, does require scienter­willful falsification of books and records, or willful circumvention of controls. See, e.g., United States v. Siemens AG, Case No. 08cr367 (D.D.C.) (Information filed Dec. 12, 2008).

with the DoJ's use of penalty formulae that consider the benefit received from improper conduct in determining the amount of fines to be assessed.

2.2. Investigations
Several recent developments have significantly increased the flow of information to U.S. law enforcement authorities in FCPA cases and enhanced their ability to investigate and prosecute FCPA cases. These include enhanced cooperation with foreign law enforcement authorities arising from international treaties, industry-wide sweep investigations, the use of aggressive investigative techniques traditionally reserved for violent crime, and the adoption of a bounty provision intended to promote whistleblowing to the SEC. For some time, U.S. authorities have been deepening their ties with foreign law enforcement and enhancing the sharing of evidence. The OECD Working Group on Bribery, in which both the United States and Brazil participate in their capacity as parties to the OECD Anti-Bribery Convention,9 provides a cooperative forum. The Organization of American States ("OAS"), and the process for monitoring implementation of the Inter-American Convention against Corruption (to which Brazil, the United States, and 31 other countries in the Americas are party), has provided another vehicle for strengthening relations between anti-corruption authorities in the Americas. With respect to Brazil, the DoJ works under the Treaty on Mutual Legal Assistance in Criminal Matters with Brazil.10 The SEC also has a bilateral Memorandum of Understanding with the Comissão de Valores Mobiliários ("CVM") of Brazil, which is a signatory to the IOSCO Multilateral Memorandum of Understanding Concerning Consultation and the Exchange of Information as well.11 In addition, enforcement authorities have centralized their enforcement capacity and industry-wide sweeps are becoming more common. These have occurred in the customs and import area (as seen in the Panalpina series of cases in 2010), and have been ongoing in the medical devices and pharmaceutical industries, as well as the sovereign wealth and hedge fund industries and film studios.12 With the creation of a dedicated FCPA unit in the Federal Bureau
9. OECD Convention on Combating Bribery of Foreign Public Officials in International Business Transactions, 37 I.L.M. 1 (entered into force Feb. 15, 1999) (the "OECD Anti-Bribery Convention"). 10. U.S. Treaty Doc. 105-42 (1998), 105th Cong., 2d Sess. 11. MoU Between SEC and CMV (July 1, 1988), available at http://www.sec.gov/about/offices/ oia/oia_bilateral/brazil.pdf; MoU Concerning Consultation and the Exchange of Information (May 2002), available at http://www.iosco.org/library/index.cfm?section=pubdocs&year=2002&public DocID=126. 12. of Investigation ("FBI") to support the DoJ's investigations, and a specialized FCPA Unit at the SEC, these sweeps are expected to continue.13 Further, in what has been a controversial move, the DoJ recently carried out an undercover sting operation in the gun sale industry. While the prosecution itself has led to mixed results,14 it has allowed the DoJ to raise the specter that FCPA investigators would be making use of law enforcement techniques ­ such as wire taps and undercover operations ­ traditionally used to fight organized crime or violent crime.15 On the SEC side, in May 2011, the SEC adopted a whistleblower bounty rule to implement provisions of the Dodd-Frank financial reform legislation. Under this rule, individuals may be eligible to receive a bounty if they provide information that leads to or furthers an SEC enforcement action in which penalties over US$1 million are collected.16 The SEC is required by law to grant a bounty of between 10% and 30% to eligible whistleblowers. While both the SEC and DoJ have had tip-lines or hotlines for some time, these provisions are widely expected to increase the number of tips provided to the SEC in FCPA cases. The SEC estimates that the bounty rule potentially could lead to approximately 30,000 tips each year relating to violations of all U.S. securities laws including the FCPA; shortly after Congress adopted the bounty provisions, there were reports that the SEC was receiving at least one FCPA tip per day.17

2.3. Who is Being Prosecuted 2.3.1. Increased Prosecution of Individuals
As noted earlier, both individuals and companies over whom there is jurisdiction can be charged for FCPA violations. Prosecutors do not have to pursue an employer in order to prosecute its employees, but may pursue one, the other, or both, depending on what they view as being warranted in the individual case.

85

In recent years, while prosecution of companies continues at a strong pace, the authorities have made prosecution of individuals a higher enforcement priority than previously for purposes of deterrence. In 2010, 12 individuals pleaded guilty, with some 40 more awaiting trial. In 2009, as DoJ indicted 22 individuals in the "SHOT show" sting, more individual prosecutions (28) were brought than corporate prosecutions (18), and the U.S. brought more cases against individuals (three) to trial in FCPA cases than in the preceding five years combined. The growing number of cases against individuals is the result of several factors. One factor is the growing number of cases against foreign nationals, including in high-profile cases where the United States seeks extradition.18 While it appears that the United States has yet to prosecute a Brazilian national for violation of the FCPA, several Brazilian citizens were identified by the authorities as participating in a criminal conspiracy involving Universal Leaf Tabacos Ltda.19 Even where extradition is not available, however, the SEC can obtain a default judgment against an absentee defendant.20 Cases against foreign nationals are increasing as the U.S. asserts expansive extraterritorial jurisdictional theories, as discussed below. The increasing proportion of cases against individuals also reflects the dynamics in FCPA investigations and prosecutions. Virtually all FCPA cases against companies are brought as settled enforcement actions, binding companies to ongoing cooperation that can give the government access to extensive documents and information, and create significant risks for employees and third parties. In addition, in very recent years where the proceeds of bribery have ended up in, or passed through, the United States, the DoJ has been pursuing enforcement action against foreign officials, further raising the tally of cases against individuals.21

2.3.2. Prosecution of Business Entities, Including Foreign Entities In 2010, nearly half of all corporate FCPA prosecutions involved foreign entities, whether as issuers of U.S. securities, as subsidiaries of issuers, or as separate businesses based abroad. Of the 21 corporations charged in 2010, the highest number yet ­ 10 ­ were foreign companies.22 (And seven out of the top

eight highest penalty payers in 2010 were foreign companies.) Companies based outside the United States that issue shares on a U.S. exchange, such as American Depositary Receipts ("ADRs"), are being targeted for enforcement. Since the first FCPA prosecutions against foreign issuers ABB Ltd. and Statoil ASA in 2004 and 2006, many of the largest-ever FCPA prosecutions (in terms of penalties) have been against foreign issuers, including Siemens AG ($800 million), Daimler AG ($185 million), Technip S.A. ($338 million), ENI S.p.A. ($365 million), JGC ($219 million), BAE ($400 million), and Alcatel-Lucent ($137 million).

2.3.3. Use of Expansive Jurisdictional Theories As suggested above, U.S. authorities are employing increasingly expansive jurisdictional theories as a tool to increase enforcement against foreign companies. These theories have come in several forms. The two most noteworthy recent theories are an "agency" theory used to reach foreign firms that do business with issuers, and a "correspondent account" theory used to reach companies that carry out transactions between non-U.S. accounts in U.S. dollars. The 2010 civil SEC settlement by the Swiss customs brokerage firm Panalpina represents the first time the SEC has charged a foreign corporation that is not an "issuer" of securities in the United States or a subsidiary of an issuer ­ but instead an independent third party acting as an agent for U.S. issuers ­ with civil violations of the FCPA.23 This development has the potential to significantly expand the universe of companies subject to FCPA enforcement. In the SEC's "agent" theory, all companies, including non-U.S. companies doing business outside the United States with no affiliation to a U.S. issuer through ownership, but that carry out business tasks for U.S. or foreign issuers of securities on a U.S. exchange, may be targets of the SEC's enforcement efforts. For example, a Brazilian firm that provides services to a U.S. or even a non-U.S. issuer could potentially be reached by this theory. In addition, for some time, the mere transmission of electronic communications, such as emails and faxes, to and from persons in the United States has been regularly cited as jurisdictional grounds for conspiracy and FCPA antibribery charges, including in the Universal Leaf case.24 Yet U.S. enforcement authorities recently have taken this approach one step further, claiming, as in the Alcatel-Lucent case, that the transmission of funds involving U.S. banking facilities also triggers jurisdiction, even when the transmission occurs merely through a U.S. dollar clearing account and the transmitting company is not necessarily aware of that fact.
23. SEC v. Panalpina, Inc., Case No. 10cv4334 (S.D. Tx.) (Complaint of Nov. 4, 2010) § 6. 24. United States v. Universal Leaf Tabacos Ltda., Case No. 10cr225 (E.D. Va.) (Information) § 26.

5 ­ The U.S. Foreign Corrupt Practices Act: past, present, and future

87

In 2010, for the first time, the DoJ asserted jurisdiction to bring an FCPA charge solely on the basis of a wire transfer between two non-U.S. accounts that passed through a clearing account in the United States. This "correspondent account" jurisdictional theory appears in the criminal charges the DoJ filed against Technip S.A. of France relating to a grand corruption scheme involving the Bonny Island Project in Nigeria and other companies including KBR/ Halliburton, Snamprogetti of Italy, Technip of France, and JGC Corporation of Japan. The DoJ charged Technip with violation of the anti-bribery provisions based solely upon the allegation that "defendant TECHNIP caused... corrupt U.S. dollar payments to be wire transferred from... bank account in Amsterdam, The Netherlands, via correspondent bank accounts in New York, New York, to bank accounts... in Switzerland for use in part to bribe Nigerian government officials."25 Then, in 2011, the DoJ further multiplied the extraterritorial reach of this jurisdictional theory by bringing conspiracy and aiding and abetting charges against a Japanese firm that took what appear to be exclusively foreign acts outside the United States as part of the payment scheme.26 The JGC Corp. case shows that merely assisting other parties with foreign wire transfers in U.S. dollars can trigger a claim of jurisdiction by U.S. authorities, and also that raising jurisdictional defenses could even lead U.S. authorities to argue for greater penalties.27 The "correspondent account" jurisdiction theory itself is not new to FCPA enforcement. In the Alcatel-Lucent case, for example, the DoJ asserted jurisdiction over a conspiracy to violate the anti-bribery provisions by a French subsidiary of Alcatel. In that case, however, the DoJ did not rely exclusively on correspondent account jurisdiction, and also described a series of wire transfer payments in U.S. dollars that originated from U.S. bank accounts.28 In Siemens, the first major FCPA case where the theory appeared, there also were other grounds for U.S. jurisdiction. As the Technip case illustrates, the DoJ is now willing to bring FCPA charges based solely upon a U.S. dollar bank transfer between two accounts outside the United States. The increased use of this

theory also has consequences for business relating to Brazil. In the Brazilian context, it means that FCPA jurisdiction could be asserted based solely on a U.S. dollar transfer between accounts in Brazil, from Brazil to an offshore or other foreign jurisdiction, from an offshore or other foreign jurisdiction to Brazil, or between offshore or foreign jurisdictions.

2.4. Beyond Bribery ­ Use of Other Authorities in FCPA Cases
Not only have U.S. authorities been bringing vastly more FCPA cases than in the past, they also have been resorting to a wider range of U.S. laws and legal theories to prosecute conduct associated with FCPA violations. For example, many of the cases discussed above involve charges of violation of the federal conspiracy statute, as opposed to direct violations of the FCPA. The conspiracy statute can be used to prosecute conduct that pre-dates the five-year statute of limitations period for direct FCPA violations. Other arrows in the enforcement quiver have included U.S. anti-money laundering laws (which are invoked in prosecutions of officials on the demand side as well as prosecutions of individuals and companies on the supply side of bribery),29 U.S. laws criminalizing false statements to the U.S. government (which were the basis for the charges against the U.K. firm, BAE, leading to the third-largest penalty in an FCPA case ­ US$400 million), and the U.S. Travel Act (which can be used to reach conduct linked to violations of commercial bribery laws at the state level30). In several recent cases involving Brazil, however, U.S. authorities have broken new ground in alleging violations of additional laws, beyond the typical set of charges used in FCPA cases. The two starkest examples are the use of anti-trust charges in the Bridgestone case, and the charge of "control person" liability under the securities laws in the Nature's Sunshine case.

2.4.1. "Control person" liability under the U.S. securities laws In July 2009, the SEC brought securities fraud charges against Nature's Sunshine Products, Inc. ("NSP") for violations of Rule 10b-5 under the Exchange Act (an anti-fraud rule), and for the first time in an FCPA case, successfully brought "control person" charges ­ against the NSP Chief Executive Officer, Douglas Faggioli, and its former Chief Financial Officer, Craig D. Huff. The parties agreed to pay civil penalties of US$600,000, US$250,000, and US$250,000 respectively.31
29. See n. 20 supra. 30. See, e.g., United States v. Control Components, Inc., Case No. 09cr162 (C.D. Ca.) (Information charging conspiracy citing as its object, at § 29, violation of the FCPA anti-bribery provisions and the Travel Act based upon the commercial bribery statute of California). 31. SEC v. Nature's Sunshine Products, Inc., Faggioli and Huff, Case No. 09cv672 (D. Ut.) (Compl. Filed July 31, 2009). Prior efforts to prosecute control person charges, in the case of SEC v. Murphy, Case No. 02cv2908 (S.D. Tx.) did not lead to a resolution, as the case was stayed.

5 ­ The U.S. Foreign Corrupt Practices Act: past, present, and future

89

According to the SEC, the wholly-owned Brazilian subsidiary of NSP, Nature's Sunshine Produtos Naturais Ltda. ("NSP Brazil") made improper cash payments to customs officials in Brazil in 2000 and 2001 to import unregistered products into Brazil; purchased fictitious documentation to make the cash payments appear legitimate; and improperly failed to disclose the payments in securities disclosures. At least some of the payments were made indirectly, through customs brokers who received over US$1 million in cash that NSP Brazil typically booked as "importation advances". NSP Brazil reportedly made the payments after Brazil had reclassified certain nutritional products as medicines, subjecting the products to a registration requirement that NSP Brazil was not able to meet. As a result of its inability to import unregistered products into Brazil, the revenues of NSP Brazil had fallen almost tenfold. Before these restrictions, Brazil had become one of the largest markets for NSP. The "control person" charge is particularly noteworthy because it can be used to impose liability on supervisory and management personnel, even where they are not aware of or furthering bribery. The SEC charges against Messrs. Faggioli and Huff under Section 20(a) rested solely on the allegation that the officers were effectively "in control" of the Brazilian employees who paid the bribes. The SEC alleged that Messrs. Faggioli and Huff "had supervisory responsibilities for the senior management and policies" of the company including the "export and sale" of its products. In this regard, they allegedly "failed to adequately supervise" their subordinates who were responsible for ensuring the accurate recording of the "state of registration of products sold in Brazil" and for ensuring a system of controls to provide assurances that "registration of NSP products sold in Brazil was adequately monitored."32 Thus the executives were, in effect, charged with failing to supervise the subsidiary's compliance with the accounting provisions of the FCPA. If this charge is invoked in further cases, it could create a significant expansion in liability where corporate officers are highly involved in the operations of a company.

2.4.2. Anti-corruption and anti-trust In September 2011, Bridgestone Corporation ("Bridgestone") ­ a company based in Japan with a subsidiary based in the United States ­ agreed to plead guilty and to pay a US$28 million criminal fine for its role in conspiracies to rig bids and to make corrupt payments to foreign government officials in Latin America related to the sale of marine hose and other industrial products manufactured by the company and sold throughout the world. In December 2008,

the DoJ had brought a similar combination of charges against an executive of the company.33 That prosecution secured a guilty plea, after DoJ had already obtained guilty pleas from eight other individuals to anti-trust charges. 34 Thus what began as an anti-trust case apparently became a combined anti-trust/ FCPA case, 35 and the DoJ charged Bridgestone with conspiracy to violate not only the FCPA, but also the Sherman Anti-Trust Act. This apparent linkage of FCPA and anti-trust realms through the charges in a single case is notable for several reasons. It signifies how foreign bribery and transnational anti-competitive collusion can be part of the same extraterritorial scheme. 36 In this case, the company colluded with competitors to rig prices at artificially high levels, and used its sales agents to bribe officials of SOEs in Latin America ­ including Brazil, Argentina, Ecuador, Mexico, and Venezuela ­ to secure SEO purchases of the company's product. One part of the conspiracy had the goal of inflating the price, and the other of securing purchases from SOEs. The interplay between anti-bribery and anti-competition also can be seen in several other recent FCPA cases. In August 2010, the DoJ brought an enforcement action against foreign subsidiaries of Alliance One International, Inc., and Universal Leaf Tabacos Ltda., Universal Corporation's Brazilian subsidiary based in Santa Cruz do Sul.37 (One of the Alliance One subsidiaries also had operations in Brazil.) The primary allegations in the case are that each of the three subsidiaries retained sales agents, and collaborated through those agents to apportion tobacco sales to the Thailand Tobacco Monopoly among themselves, coordinated their sales prices, and paid kickbacks to officials of the Thailand Tobacco Monopoly in order to ensure that each company would share in the Thai tobacco market. (The Alliance affiliate also was accused of similar conduct in Kyrgyzstan.) This growing linkage between the two areas (anti-corruption and anti-trust) also suggests a need for an integrated approach to developing compliance program modules that focus on sales practices.

See also DoJ Deputy Assistant Attorney General (1999) (observing that DoJ "believe[s] there are many potential overlaps between FCPA violations and international antitrust violations", and noting that the "Antitrust Division has occasionally discovered evidence of FCPA payments in the course of international cartel investigations"). 36. 2.5. New Plateaus for Penalties, Multijurisdictional Enforcement, and Collateral Consequences
FCPA enforcement has garnered attention in recent years for dramatic monetary penalties, as well as imprisonment terms for individuals, which have become commonplace. As foreign countries implement international treaty obligations to adopt enhanced anti-corruption regimes, FCPA problems also are increasingly playing out in a context of multijurisdictional enforcement, as well as leading to collateral consequences, such as debarment from contracting with national governments and participation in projects financed by international financial institutions ("IFIs") such as The World Bank, the Inter-American Development Bank, and others. With respect to outcomes in FCPA cases, it is now common for FCPA settlements with companies to include penalties above US$10 million, and even in some cases above US$100 million. While the form of a negotiated resolution can vary from a plea, to a civil settlement, to a deferred prosecution agreement ("DPA"), to a non-prosecution agreement ("NPA"), all forms of resolution typically carry significant requirements regarding future conduct of the party settling the case relating to compliance, cooperation, remediation, and sometimes others. These requirements can also provide authorities with a basis for seeking higher penalties for any future violations. While corporate resolutions also continue to include obligations for the development of a compliance program, it is not as automatic as it was a few years earlier that every significant resolution will include a compliance monitor. In addition, as authorities continue to bring a historically larger number of cases against individuals, the data on sentencing for FCPA violations is growing. Multiyear prison sentences are common, and the authority to impose penalties based upon the amount of the gain has led to a massive penalty in the recent KBR/Halliburton/ENI/Technip/JGC case against a former attorney for a multinational oil venture in Nigeria, where the defendant (a U.K. national) agreed to forfeit US$149 million. While the Siemens case set a record for the largest penalty in a case against a single company, it also highlights another trend: multijurisdictional enforcement. Even before U.S. authorities concluded an FCPA settlement with Siemens, authorities in Italy and Germany already had resolved enforcement actions against individuals and the company and investigations in numerous other jurisdictions also have been launched. The 2007 and 2008 settlements with German authorities ­ the largest ever in a foreign bribery case ­ included 1.25 million in administrative penalties for a violation of law, and 594.75 million as disgorgement of the revenues linked to business tainted by the bribery. Siemens also was required to pay $100 million to settled a World Bank prosecution, and has

92

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

faced other investigations in Europe (Switzerland, Lichtenstein, Austria, Greece, Hungary, Norway, Poland, and Serbia), in Asia (China, Indonesia, Malaysia, Vietnam, Russia) as well as in Argentina and Nigeria. Foreign enforcement in subsequent years has continued to grow. While the first prosecution under the U.K. Bribery Act involved domestic corruption, U.K. authorities have begun to bring more cases under prior laws, and are expected to do so even more often under the Bribery Act, which became effective in July 2011. Latin American jurisdictions also have brought some significant cases, such as in Costa Rica, where the former President of Costa Rica and the Secretary General of the OAS was convicted of accepting more than US$800,000 in payments from the Alcatel organization during his Presidency. He was sentenced to five years in prison. 38 (This prosecution is part of the same corruption scheme that led to FCPA charges in the Alcatel-Lucent case discussed above.)

3. The Expanding Types of Business Dealings Featured in FCPA Cases, and the Expanding Cast of Third Parties Who Raise FCPA Issues
In the first 25 years of FCPA enforcement, the FCPA traditionally had been viewed as being focused primarily, if not exclusively, on bribery related to procurement and business awards. Its goal was to eradicate the classic "quid pro quo" arrangement, where bribes were used to buy contracts and purchase orders. This type of example is seen in many cases, including the 1998 and 1999 cases of International Material Solutions and Control Systems Specialists/ Crites where payments were used to win awards of business from the Brazilian Air Force. In recent years, these types of procurement activities continue to be a focus area for enforcement, including in Brazil.39 As FCPA enforcement has expanded over the past decade, however, so too has the type of conduct that it reaches. Rather than focusing only on large payments to get business ­ so-called "grand corruption" ­ authorities are now closely scrutinizing smaller, but still significant, payments, such as gifts, sponsorships, and entertainment. In the last few years, several FCPA cases have been brought focusing exclusively on recurring instances of these types of payments, even in the absence of large cash bribes.40
38. ABC News, Former Costa Rican president jailed for corruption (Apr. 28, 2011), available at http:// www.abc.net.au/news/2011-04-28/former-costa-rican-president-jailed-for-corruption/2695972. 39. See, e.g., SEC v. Tyco International Ltd., Case No. 06cv2942 (S.D.N.Y.) (Complaint of Apr. 13, 2006) §§ 48-52 (describing payments by acquired Brazilian subsidiary to secure business including municipal water and wastewater contracts). 40. See, e.g., SEC v. Even more significantly, enforcement authorities now clearly apply the FCPA to all manner of operational aspects of the business, wholly apart from winning it in the first place.

3.1. Operational Activities Caught in the FCPA's Web
The Pandora's Box was opened in the landmark litigation over the scope of the "obtaining or retaining business" element of the anti-bribery prohibition in the Kay case. After payments to reduce customs duties were deemed potentially within the scope of this element in the Kay case,41 this so-called "business purpose" test has been interpreted broadly to include almost any bribe that improves a company's profitability. The range of operational activities caught is very broad, including collection of overdue payments from government customers, customs and immigration, tax, and regulatory issues including environmental regulation. For example, in an August 2010 SEC enforcement action a former executive of Pride International, Inc. was accused of bribing a mid-level accounts payable employee at Venezuela's state-owned oil company, Petroleos de Venezuela, S.A. ("PDVSA"), in order to get that employee to release a payment that Pride International was entitled to receive under its contract with PDVSA.42 In the customs area, the case against Nature's Sunshine Products, Inc. and Messrs. Faggioli and Huff (discussed supra) concerned payments to Brazilian customs authorities. These payments were not alleged to help the company secure buyers for its products. Rather, they were to ensure that the products could be imported into the market. The Panalpina case brought in 2010 similarly included allegations of bribery to customs authorities in Brazil.43 The application of the FCPA anti-bribery provisions to tax matters is seen in several recent cases, including the 2008 Willbros case involving payments to tax authorities in Nigeria.44 In addition, in 2006, Sitel Corp., a teleservices firm focused on customer care that was previously publicly traded, voluntarily disclosed accounting irregularities and the possibility of FCPA violations in its

Brazilian operations 45 which, according to unconfirmed reports, may have been related to the payment of municipal taxes. While it is not clear whether that investigation will lead to enforcement action, it is clear that U.S. authorities will pursue improper payments to tax officials as bribes under the FCPA. Payments in the operational context also can trigger accounting violations, regardless of whether they are viewed as bribes. For example, in 2001, the SEC brought an administrative action against Baker Hughes, Inc. relating to payments to secure reductions in tax assessments in Indonesia as well as payments to secure approval of a corporate filing with the Commercial Registry of Rio de Janeiro, Brazil.46 In addition, the 2008 DoJ non-prosecution agreement with Westinghouse Air Brake Technologies Corp. ("WABTEC") cited payments related to several types of operational activities. These included payments to obtain scheduling of pre-shipping product inspections, to have certificates of product delivery scheduled, and to curb excise tax audits.47 One result of this new focus on operational issues has been "pattern and practice" cases ­ cases involving payments that, while not large individually, are recurring, and through their recurrence can involve significant amounts.48

3.2. The Expanded Cast of Third Parties that Can Raise FCPA Risks
As the FCPA reaches into virtually all areas of business operations that interface with the government, it also covers dealings with third parties in these areas. In the early years of the FCPA, compliance attention focused largely on engagement of sales agents and sales representatives ­ the types of third parties who can help companies win business. Many more types of third parties now garner enforcement attention. Indeed, there is no limit or restriction within the FCPA on the type of third party that can create vicarious liability for a company. In practice, U.S. authorities have adopted the broad formulation of third party seen in the 2010 OECD Good Practice Guidance on Internal Controls, Ethics, and Compliance: "agents and other intermediaries, consultants, representatives, distributors, contractors and suppliers, consortia, and joint venture partners (hereinafter "business partners")". Accordingly, as seen in the Nature's Sunshine case and the Panalpina case, customs brokers (and related third parties such as freight forwarders, expediters, etc.) are clearly an area of third party risk for companies engaged in international trade. Tax advisors, lawyers, and consultants
45. See Sitel Corp. 10-K (Sept. 14, 2006). 46. In re: Baker Hughes, Inc., Admin. Proc. No. 3-10572 (Sept. 12, 2001). 47. DoJ Nonprosecution Agreement with WABTEC (Feb. 8, 2008) §§ 4, 9-13. 48. See SEC Press Rel., supra n. 11 (describing customs brokerage and freight forwarding industry sweep in Panalpina case).
