also can raise issues. Distributors and suppliers, on accounts related to SOEs, also can present FCPA risks. Three other types of third parties merit particular mention here: lobbyists, joint venture partners, and companies targeted for mergers or acquisitions. Lobbyists. Lobbyists are a type of third party that generates significant FCPA risk, including in Brazil, as seen in the case of Tyco (2006). After Tyco acquired Earth Tech Brasil Ltda. in 1998, the subsidiary made payments to officials in Brazil to secure contracts for construction and operation of municipal water and wastewater treatment systems. As the SEC noted, some lobbyists submitted false inflated invoices to the company, and "[a]t times, the payments were made by lobbyists that Earth Tech Brazil retained with full knowledge that all or a portion of the money that Earth Tech Brazil paid to the lobbyists would be given to various Brazilian officials for the purpose of obtaining work for Earth Tech Brazil."49 While the Tyco case involved the use of a lobbyist to win business, FCPA risks also can arise from the engagement of a lobbyist to assist with regulatory or legislative issues as well, particularly when the lobbyist lacks qualifications in relevant areas, and other compliance safeguards are not used.50 Joint venture partners. As noted in the OECD Good Practice Guidance, joint venture partners are another type of third party that can raise FCPA risk. This is seen in the cases brought against Halliburton/KBR, ENI S.p.A., Technip S.A., and JGC Corp. relating to their joint venture for the Bonny Island project in Nigeria, as well as the case involving Frederic Bourke and others. In the Halliburton/KBR matter, all four venture partners settled criminal and, except for non-issuer JGC Corp., civil, FCPA cases. The combined penalties are the highest aggregate penalties in any case ­ nearly US$1.8 billion. (The case also led to criminal charges against the venture's dealings with U.K. lawyer, Jeffrey Tesler, who, as noted above, has been criminally charged and extradited to the United States, along with other individuals.) Mergers and acquisitions. M&A transactions also are a relatively recent area of FCPA focus. Cases such as Latin Node, ABB, Titan, and others, have highlighted the risks that buyers face when acquiring a company that may have engaged in bribery in the past or may have ongoing activities that implicate the FCPA.51

Pre-acquisition due diligence has become one tool used extensively by buyers to try to apprise themselves of the risks attendant on a particular acquisition so that they can take appropriate steps to address them.

4. What is a State Enterprise: Weighing Multiple Factors
The FCPA statute defines "foreign official" broadly, including officers and employees of government "instrumentalities" in addition to departments and agencies. U.S. FCPA enforcement authorities have long construed "instrumentalities" to include SOEs. For example, in the CCI case the alleged bribes included payments to officials of Petrobras, and the charge of conspiracy to violate the FCPA in the Bridgestone case arose from payments made to un-named SOEs in Brazil and other countries in Latin America. But the term "instrumentalities" is not defined in the statute, giving rise to questions about its scope. The term's meaning has been the subject of litigation in several recent cases. Several courts have ruled on the matter, and no court has held that SOEs are categorically excluded from the statute. Rather, federal district courts in California (in the Carson and Lindsey cases) and Florida (in the Esquenazi case) have applied a multi-factor test in evaluating whether an enterprise qualifies as an SOE whose officials are foreign officials under the FCPA. Drawing from guidance under the OECD Anti-Bribery Convention, the court in the Lindsey case identified the following "non-exclusive list" of factors that can be used on a case-by-case basis to determine whether an entity is an "instrumentality" for FCPA purposes: (1) The entity provides a service to the citizens of the jurisdiction; (2) The key officers and directors of the entity are, or are appointed by, government officials; (3) The entity is financed, at least in large measure, through governmental appropriations or through revenues obtained as a result of government-mandated taxes, licenses, fees, or royalties; (4) The entity is vested with and exercises exclusive or controlling power to administer its designated functions; and (5) The entity is widely perceived and understood to be performing official (i.e., governmental) functions.52 Based upon these factors, the court determined that the Mexican federal electric utility (CFE) could qualify as an instrumentality for FCPA purposes.

Rel. 2004-02 (DoJ opinion on compliance controls instituted by affiliates of ABB Ltd. prior to completion of their spinoff by ABB Ltd., and as part of their resolution of criminal FCPA charges). 52. Order Denying Motion to Dismiss at 9, United States v. Noriega, Case No. 10cr1031 (C.D. Cal. Apr. 20, 2011).

The following month, in May 2011, the jury convicted the defendants. (In the Esquenazi case in Florida, the court used virtually identical language in its jury instructions, and the jury convicted the two former executives who stood trial in August 2010.). The California court in the Carson case identified similar factors.53 The multi-factor tests outlined above likely will face appellate scrutiny at some time. For now, these decisions are the only judicial guidance for companies to use to determine which SOEs are covered by the FCPA. The test can be unpredictable, however, because the courts are placing the final decision on whether a given SOE is an instrumentality in the hands of juries. Accordingly, prudent companies will continue to treat entities as SOEs in cases where the status is not clear-cut.

5. Conclusion ­ What the Future May Bring, and the Importance of Effective Compliance
Virtually every trend in FCPA enforcement and interpretation in recent years has served to broaden its reach. As discussed above, jurisdictional theories, coverage of operational activities and third parties involved in them, use of ancillary statutes to bring additional charges, coverage of SOEs ­ all have been used in the service of bringing more and more FCPA cases. Courts thus far have tended largely to uphold the Government's broad interpretations of the statute; it remains to be seen whether this will continue to be the trend. Expanded enforcement has mobilized the U.S. business community to seek amendments to the FCPA.54 Largely on the basis of this proposal, one chamber of the U.S. Congress has held a hearing on potential amendments to the FCPA. The proposed amendments that have received the most attention include adding a statutory definition of "instrumentality", limiting liability in M&A situations, and clarifying credit that companies may receive for effective compliance programs. Finally, there has been some pressure on the facilitating payments exception to the FCPA anti-bribery provisions, as organizations such as the OECD call for eradication of facilitating payments. Civil society has countered these proposals with its own critique; at this writing, the prospects for passage of legislation that would significantly narrow the FCPA in the near
53. These factors were: (1) The foreign state's characterization of the entity and its employees; (2) The foreign state's degree of control over the entity; (3) The purpose of the entity's activities; (4) The entity's obligations and privileges under the foreign state's law, including whether the entity exercises exclusive or controlling power to administer its designated functions; (4) The circumstances surrounding the entity's creation; and (5) The foreign state's extent of ownership of the entity, including the level of financial support by the state. Order Denying Motion to Dismiss at 5, United States v. Carson, Case No. 09cr77 (C.D. Cal. May 18, 2011). As of this writing, the parties were litigating over jury instructions in the case. 54. TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

term seem dim. As of late 2012, U.S. enforcement authorities were preparing to issue guidance on the FCPA, which was expected to cover there issues as well as others, and which will be closely watched by companies looking to develop or update their Compliance programs. Internationally, with the advent of the U.K. Bribery Act and other statutes that cover commercial as well as public sector bribery, and contain no exception for facilitating payments, pressures run the other way­to expand rather than contract the scope of the FCPA. At the same time, the adoption of other statutes with transnational effect, make it increasingly likely that conduct will fall under the jurisdiction of multiple authorities. Parallel and successive prosecutions, while not yet frequent, are becoming more common. With such a broad reach, given the international developments and pressures, and with additional legislation in Brazil potentially on the horizon, the need for companies doing business in Brazil ­ and Brazilian companies who issue shares on U.S. exchanges or do business with issuers ­ to develop an effective anti-corruption compliance program is greater than ever. If the compliance program is deemed as "effective", the company stands a greater chance if there is a problem of persuading enforcement authorities to refrain from bringing an enforcement action, to use alternative methods of resolving a matter short of prosecution (NPAs or DPAs), to use reduced or lesser charges, or to seek a lighter sentence or penalty.55 FCPA enforcement actions, the OECD Good Practice Guidance, and other initiatives, provide guidance on the scope and components of anti-corruption compliance programs. In general, such programs should be risk-based, and should cover the range of issues faced by a company given where and how it carries out its international business. Dealings with third parties, the incidence of M&A transactions, and the nature and extent of governmental interactions will all shape the contours of a program. Boards of directors are increasingly viewed as carrying responsibilities for the adoption and maintenance of effective programs. Compliance programs require substantial resources. They are not static, but need to be updated and tailored to future developments. The effort to establish and maintain a program, however, can be well worth the investment, as even a relatively small FCPA problem can cost millions of dollars in investigative costs, even before considering fines and penalties, collateral sanctions, and reputational damage.

1. Introduction3

W

hen measured by direct dollar losses, public corruption is not the most serious form of white collar crime, but when measured by its overall negative impact on society, it very well may be. Corruption is uniquely harmful because it involves the distortion and betrayal of the political system.4 Historically, countries that have

On March 27, 2008, Attorney General Michael Mukasey emphasized the Department of Justice's commitment to fighting public corruption because of its uniquely harmful impact, explaining that "[public] corruption has `a cost beyond dollars and cents ­ it undermines the whole idea of government."" 100

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

been unable to control official corruption have suffered severe consequences in terms of economic development and national morale. Former FBI Director Robert Mueller declared that "[p]ublic corruption is a betrayal of the public's trust. Unchecked, it threatens our government and our way of life."5 Therefore, it is critical to the health of the republic that law enforcement stays one step ahead of corrupt officials, thereby preventing the corrosion of politics and the obstruction of governmental functions. Given the dual-sovereignty structure of government in the United States, public corruption violates both federal and state law. Nevertheless, federal prosecutors and investigators have traditionally been the main players on both stages: at the state level, because local law enforcement is typically not good at self-policing; and at the federal level, because there is no one else to do the job. Historically, the "feds" have been relatively successful in their anti-corruption work. This can probably be attributed to two factors. First, federal investigators and career federal prosecutors are generally well insulated against political influence. Second, their professional careers are typically advanced by successfully bringing down "big fish" ­ the bigger, the better ­ rather than by looking the other way. Four statutes are most commonly employed to combat corruption. They are (1) Mail (and Wire) Fraud, 18 U.S.C. §1341 (and §1343); (2) Bribery of Public Officials, 18 U.S.C. §201; (3) Theft or Bribery Concerning Programs Receiving Federal Funds, 18 U.S.C. §666; and (4) the "Hobbs Act," 18 U.S.C. §1951, which ­ among other things ­ prohibits extortion under color of official right. Each of these statutes will be explored in some depth in this chapter. The mail and wire fraud statutes have recently been the subject of considerable controversy, culminating in the U.S. Supreme Court case of Skilling v. United States, 130 S. Ct. 2896 (2010). In Skilling, the Court restricted the use of these statutes in the honest services (public corruption) context to cover only bribery and kickback schemes, excluding the "amorphous category of cases" where the public official merely engaged in undisclosed self-dealing. Bribery of Public Officials criminalizes both bribery and the promising of gratuities to public officials "for or because of any official act performed or to be performed by such public official." Courts have struggled with delineating the boundaries of this clause, called the "nexus requirement." Other interpretive challenges under the statute include identifying who is a "public official" and what constitutes an "official act."

101

Compared to §201, §666 is a relatively straightforward provision criminalizing the theft of more than $5,000 from any organization or state, local, or tribal government that receives more than $10,000 in federal funds in any one-year period. It also outlaws bribes involving $5,000 or more of officials who oversee receipt of more than $10,000 in federal funds in a single calendar year. Section 666 has sparked considerable litigation testing the limits of the relationship between the federal funding and the criminal activity. The Hobbs Act comes into play when a public official obtains property from another person in return for an official act. It is jurisdictionally based on interference with interstate commerce, though this requirement has been minimized over many years of judicial interpretation. A critical question under the Hobbs Act is whether the public official must demand (or even request) payment to come within its reach, or whether mere acceptance of property is good enough. All of these issues, and a few more, are examined below.

2. Honest Services Fraud, 18 U.S.C. §1346
2.1. History of Mail and Wire Fraud
Honest services fraud, 18 U.S.C. §1346, is a particular type of mail and wire fraud, which are codified at 18 U.S.C. §§1341 and 1343, respectively. Courts have interpreted mail and wire fraud to have the same substantive elements with the exception of the jurisdictional requirement.6 To prove a violation of either statute, the government must establish: (1) the existence of a scheme or artifice to defraud; (2) intent to defraud; and (3) either the use of the mails or private interstate carrier (mail fraud), or the use of an interstate wire communication (wire fraud), for the purpose of executing the scheme. Typically, mail or wire fraud also require that the victim of the fraud be deprived of money or property. However, through 18 U.S.C. §1346, "the term `scheme or artifice to defraud' [in §§1341 and 1343] includes a scheme or artifice to deprive another of the intangible right of honest services."7 In 1872, Congress used its power to regulate interstate commerce to pass the first version of §1341. The original mail fraud statute sought to protect the post office from abuse by criminal schemes to defraud individuals where use of the mails furthered the relevant scheme.8 Congress continued to amend
6. E.g., United States v. LaMacchia, 871 F. Supp. 535, 541 (D. Mass. 1994). 7. 18 U.S.C. §1346 (2006). 8. Note, Nicholas J. Wagoner, Honest-Services Fraud: The Supreme Court Defuses the Government's weapon of Mass Discretion in Skilling v. United States, 51 S. Tex. L. Rev. 1087, 1093 (2010). Congress was particularly concerned about the innocent victims of mail fraud: "[A]ll through the country thousands of innocent and unsophisticated people, knowing nothing about the ways of... city thieves and robbers, [were] continually fleeced and robbed [through a variety of frauds targeting the largely

102

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

the statute for the next twenty years, firmly cementing that use of the mails is purely a jurisdictional element and rejecting the requirement that the defendant specifically intend to abuse the postal system. 9 Moreover, through a 1994 amendment, Congress modified the U.S. Postal service element to encompass situations in which a defendant "deposits or causes to be deposited any matter or thing whatever to be sent or delivered by a private commercial interstate carrier."10 This amendment has enabled federal prosecutors to attach a mail fraud count to almost every fraudulent scheme they uncover, significantly expanding the federalization of public corruption offenses. By the mid-twentieth century, telephone and radio had gained popularity as the quickest modes of communication. To keep abreast of criminal activity, Congress enacted 18 U.S.C. §1343, establishing wire fraud. Wire fraud mirrors its sister mail-fraud statute in every manner except its jurisdictional element. Instead of using the mail system to defraud, wire fraud prohibits one from "transmit[ing] or caus[ing] to be transmitted by means of wire, radio, or television communication in interstate or foreign commerce, any writings, signs, signals, pictures, or sounds" for the purpose of executing a fraudulent scheme.11 Today, this includes the internet, cell phones, and other modern technologies. Unlike the mailing under §1341, the wire communication must actually have traveled in interstate or foreign commerce, although the defendant need not be cognizant of this fact.12 In 1909, Congress expanded the types of schemes covered by §1341. The new language of the statute prohibited a scheme to defraud "or for obtaining money or property by means of false or fraudulent pretenses, representations, or promises." The use of the disjunctive word "or" allowed prosecutors to interpret the first clause independent of the phrase "money or property," eventually leading to the creation of the intangible rights theory.13 The Fifth Circuit's decision in Shushan v. United States, 117 F.2d 110 (1941), is credited as the first acknowledged intangible rights case. In Shushan, the court upheld the conviction of a public official who accepted bribes in exchange for influencing the award of a city contract in the bribe-givers' favor. It found that the bribe was a "scheme to defraud the public" and "[n]o trustee has more sacred duties
uninformed rural population], and the mails [were] made use of for the purpose of aiding them in their nefarious designs." Id. at 1092-93 (quoting Cong. Globe, 41st Cong., 3d Sess. 35 (1870). 9. Note, Charion L. Vaughn, Power Corrupts: Honest Services Fraud and Fiduciary Duties, 50 Washburn L.J. 713, 714 (2011). 10. 18 U.S.C. §1341, 1994 Amendments. Pub. L. 103-322, §250006(1). 11. 18 U.S.C. §1343 (2006). 12. United States v. Blassingame, 427 F.2d 329, 330 (2d Cir. 1970). 13. Comment, Nicholas J. Wagoner, Honest-Services Fraud: The Supreme Court Defuses the Government's Weapon of Mass Discretion in Skilling v. United States, 51 S. Tex. L. Rev. 1087, 1094-95 (2010).

6 ­ Prosecution of Public Corruption in the United States

103

than a public official and any scheme to obtain an advantage by corrupti[on] ... must ... be considered a scheme to defraud." 14 Later, the Supreme Court would aptly described the difference between traditional mail or wire fraud and the increasingly popular intangible rights theory, stating:
Unlike fraud in which the victim's loss of money or property supplied the defendant's gain, with one the mirror image of the other, the honest-services theory targeted corruption that lacked similar symmetry. While the offender profited, the betrayed party suffered no deprivation of money or property; instead, a third party, who had not been deceived, provided the enrichment. For example, if a city mayor (the offender) accepted a bribe from a third party in exchange for awarding that party a city contract, yet the contract terms were the same as nay that could have been negotiated at arm's length, the city (the betrayed party) would suffer no tangible loss. Even if the scheme occasioned a money or property gain for the betrayed party, courts reasoned, actionable harm lay in the denial of that party's right to the offender's "honest services."15

For more than forty years, federal prosecutors used the intangible rights theory of mail and wire fraud to combat deceptive schemes that did not involve deprivations of money or property.16 In fact, honest services fraud became the federal government's primary and most potent weapon against public corruption because of the sweeping definition of "scheme to defraud." Prosecutions went forth based on varying types of conduct, including bribery, kickbacks, failures to disclose self-interests, and conflicts of interest. These intangible rights cases involved not only public officials who defrauded citizens of honest government services, but also private employees who defrauded their employers by breaching their fiduciary duties.17 As a federal prosecutor once boasted:
To federal prosecutors of white collar crime, the mail fraud statute is our Stradivarius, our Colt 45, our Louisville Slugger, our Cuisinart ­ and our true love. We may flirt with RICO, show off with 10b-5, and call the conspiracy law "darling," but we always come home to the virtues of 18 U.S.C. §1341, with its simplicity, adaptability, and comfortable familiarity.18

Ct. 2896, 2926 (2010). 16. J.B. Perrine & Patricia M. Kipnis, Navigating the Honest Services Fraud Statute After Skilling v. United States, 72 Ala. Law. 294, 295 (2011). 17. Note, Charion L. Vaughn, Power Corrupts: Honest Services Fraud and Fiduciary Duties, 50 Washburn L.J. 713, 715 (2011). 18. Jed S. Rakoff, The Federal Mail Fraud State (Part I), 18 Duq. L. Rev. 771, 771 (1980).

2.2. McNally v. United States and 18 U.S.C. §1346
Throughout the period of expansion of the intangible rights theory, many observers expressed due process concerns based on the argument that the doctrine's vagueness resulted in its failure to provide sufficiently clear notice of what constituted an offense. In McNally v. United States, 483 U.S. 350 (1987), the U.S. Supreme Court responded to these concerns and momentarily stopped the intangible rights doctrine in its tracks. The Court held that §1341's "scheme to defraud" only covered schemes intended to defraud others of money or property, not their intangible right to honest services or "the right of the citizenry to good government."19 The Court stated:
After 1909 ... the mail fraud statute criminalized schemes or artifices "to defraud" or "for obtaining money or property by means of false or fraudulent pretenses, representation, or promises ... ." Because the two phrases identifying the proscribed schemes appear in the disjunctive, it is arguable that they are to be construed independently and that the money-or-property requirement of the latter phrase does not limit schemes to defraud to those aimed at causing deprivation of money or property. This is the approach that has been taken by each of the Courts of Appeals that has addressed the issue: schemes to defraud include those designed to deprive individuals, the people, or the government of intangible rights, such as the right to have public officials perform their duties honestly. **** ... The Court has often stated that when there are two rational readings of a criminal statute, one harsher than the other, we are to choose the harsher only when Congress has spoken in clear and definite language... . Rather than construe the statute in a manner that leaves its outer boundaries ambiguous and involves the Federal Government in setting standards of disclosure and good government for local and state officials, we read §1341 as limited in scope to the protection of property rights. If Congress desires to go further, it must speak more clearly than it has.20

Congress swiftly responded to the Court's request to "speak more clearly" by passing 18 U.S.C. §1346 the following year. It engraved into federal law the principle that a scheme or artifice to defraud includes depriving another of the intangible right of honest services. Once again, federal prosecutors wielded this powerful weapon in combating public corruption.
19. 483 U.S. 350, 356 (1987). 20. McNally v. United States, 483 U.S. 350, 355-61 (1987) (citations omitted).

6 ­ Prosecution of Public Corruption in the United States

105

The problem was not solved, however. Disagreements among courts quickly arose concerning what constituted "honest services." For example, the Eleventh Circuit Court of Appeals interpreted the phrase to include situations in which a public official violates his fiduciary duty to the public by "secretly mak[ing] his decision based on his own personal interests ­ as when an official accepts a bribe or personally benefits from an undisclosed conflict of interest."21 Other courts were not as friendly to the doctrine. Some required the showing of a violation of state law as a predicate for §1346 charge; others required proof that the defendant specifically intended that his victim suffer economic harm; and a third group sustained the charges only if the evidence supported a finding that the defendant had acted in pursuit of personal gain. Despite these interpretive differences, however, the various courts of appeals uniformly declined to invalidate the statute as unconstitutionally vague.22 In his dissent to the denial of certiorari in Sorich v United States, 129 S. Ct. 1308 (2009), Justice Antonin Scalia foreshadowed the possibility that the Supreme Court might once again step in to trim the sails of honest services fraud. In reviewing the "staggeringly broad swath of behavior" covered by this statute,23 Justice Scalia addressed the fundamental due process, federalism, and separation of powers issues that undermined the constitutional legitimacy of §1346:
If the "honest services" theory ­ broadly stated, that officeholders and employees owe a duty to act only in the best interests of their constituents and employers ­ is taken seriously and carried to its logical conclusion, presumably the statute also renders criminal state legislator's decision to vote for a bill because he expects his office to obtain a restaurant table without a reservation; a public employee's recommendation of his incompetent friend for a public contract; and any self-dealing by a corporate officer. Indeed, it would seemingly cover a salaried employee's phoning in sick to go to a ball game. In many cases, moreover, the maximum penalty for violating this statute will be added to the maximum penalty for violating 18 U.S.C. §666, a federal bribery statute, since the latter
21. United States v. Walker, 490 F.3d 1282, 1297 (11th Cir. 2007) (quoting United States v. LopezLukis, 102 F.3d 1164, 1169 (11th Cir. 1997)). 22. Skilling v. United States, 130 S. Ct. 2896, 2928 & n.37 (2010). 23. Sorich v. United States, 129 S. Ct. 1308, 1309 (2009) (denial of certiorari). Justice Scalia provided this varied overview of how the statute has been applied: Courts have upheld convictions of a local housing official who failed to disclose a conflict of interest; a businessman who attempted to pay a state legislator to exercise "informal and behind-the-scenes influence on legislation"; students who schemed with their professors to turn in plagiarized work; lawyers who made side-payments to turn insurance adjusters in exchange for the expedited processing of their clients' pending claims; and, in the decision we are asked to review here, city employees who engaged in political-patronage hiring for local civil-service jobs. Id. (citations omitted).

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

requires the additional factor of the employer's receipt of federal funds, while violation of the "honest services" provision requires use of mail or wire services, §§1341, 1343. Quite a potent federal prosecutorial tool. **** In the background of the interpretive venture remain the two concerns voiced by this Court in McNally. First, the prospect of federal prosecutors' (or federal courts') creating ethics codes and setting disclosure requirements for local and state officials. Is it the role of the Federal Government to define the fiduciary duties that a town alderman or school board trustee owes to his constituents? ... Second and relatedly, this Court has long recognized the "basic principle that a criminal statute must give fair warning of the conduct that it makes a crime." **** It may be true that petitioners here, like the defendants in other "honest services" cases, have acted improperly. But "bad men, like good men, are entitled to be tried and sentenced in accordance with law."24

2.3. Skilling v. United States
Finally, in 2010, the U.S. Supreme Court clarified the post-McNally honest services fraud chaos, cutting a compromise between flatly invalidating the statute for being unconstitutionally vague and permitting prosecutors to wield nearly unlimited discretion to use it against marginally dishonest conduct. In Skilling v. United States, 130 S. Ct. 2896 (2010), the Court upheld the constitutionality of §1346, but limited its wide scope by requiring the government to show that the offender's dishonest conduct was the result of his participation in either a bribery or kickback scheme.25 In its argument before the Court, the government had urged that §1346 be interpreted to include "undisclosed self-dealing by a public official or private employee." It argued McNally itself constituted the nondisclosure of a conflicting financial interest and that pre-McNally case law consistently included undisclosed self-dealing within the purview of §1346. The Court flatly rejected both contentions. It responded that McNally involved a classic kickback scheme and that Courts of Appeals had never reached a solid consensus on the selfdealing issue. Additionally, the Court adhered to the principle that "ambiguity concerning the ambit of criminal statues should be resolved in favor of lenity."26 Concluding, the Court once again told Congress to speak more clearly if it wished to expand §1346. However, it warned Congress that if it should
24. Id. at 1309-11 (citations omitted). 25. 130 S. Ct. 2896, 2931-32 (2010). 26. Id. at 2932.

6 ­ Prosecution of Public Corruption in the United States

107

attempt to include undisclosed self-dealing within honest services, it must "employ standards of sufficient definiteness and specificity to overcome due process concerns."27

2.4. In the Wake of Skilling v. United States: Honest Services Fraud Today
Congress may once again respond to the Court's challenge to expand honest services fraud. Shortly after the Skilling case was handed down, Senator Patrick Leahy (D-VT) introduced Senate Bill 3854, the Honest Services Restoration Act, to amend §1346 by expanding the definition of "scheme or artifice" to include undisclosed self-dealing by public officials and private officers and directors of corporations. While arguing before the Senate Judiciary Committee to pass this "necessary" new legislation, Assistant Attorney General Lanny Breur suggested that Congress could avoid vagueness problems by requiring the government to prove (1) "knowing concealment of an undisclosed interest and (2) a specific intent to defraud."28 Former Representative Anthony Weiner introduced a comparable bill in the House of Representatives. Neither bill was voted on during the 111th congressional session.29 In the meantime, courts are also struggling with applying Skilling to pending convictions and with establishing clear lines for the charging of offenders. In the recent and highly publicized case of United States v. Blagojevich, former Illinois governor Rod R. Blagojevich was convicted of, inter alia, trying to sell President Barrak Obama's vacated Senate seat to the highest bidder. The charges included honest services wire fraud, so a key element in the likely appeal will involve determining whether the prosecution successfully proved a bribery or kickback scheme meeting the Skilling requirements.30 The difficult question will be figuring out what exactly these requirements are. The Skilling opinion referenced the federal bribery statutes, 18 U.S.C. §§201 & 666, and the health care fraud kickback statute, 41 U.S.C. § 52(2). However, the Court left open how much of the interpretation and case law surrounding those statutes should be imported into the honest services context. Ultimately, if the Court pulls in all of the case law requirements, it will eviscerate the independent potency of honest services fraud.

3. Bribery and Gratuities, 18 U.S.C. §201
In 1962, Congress enacted a group of federal criminal conflict of interest statutes "to strengthen the criminal laws relating to bribery, graft, and conflicts of interest."31 These statutes included prohibitions on bribery and illegal gratuities. Together, these prohibitions form part of Congress' "intricate web of regulations, both administrative and criminal, governing the acceptance of gifts and other self-enriching actions by public officials."32 Section 201 of Title 18 of the United States Code defines the offenses of bribery and illegal gratuity.33 Sections 201(a)(1)-(3) sets forth the relevant definitions of "public official," "person who has been selected to be a public official," and "official act." To prove bribery under 18 U.S.C. §201(b), the government must establish that: (1) a thing of value; (2) was given, offered, or promised to (or, in the case of a recipient, demanded, sought, received, or accepted by); (3) a present or future public official; (4) with corrupt intent to influence (or be influenced); (5) an "official act." To prove an illegal gratuity under 18 U.S.C. §201(c), the government must establish that: (1) a thing of value; (2) was given, offered, or promised to (or, in the case of a recipient, demanded, sought, received, or accepted by); (3) a present, past, or future public official; (4) "for or because of;" (5) "any official act performed or to be performed by such public official." The critical differences between bribery and illegal gratuities have been articulated as follows:
The distinguishing feature of each crime is its intent element. Bribery requires intent "to influence" an official act or "to be influenced" in an official act, while illegal gratuity requires only that the gratuity be given or accepted "for or because of" an official act. In other words, for bribery there must be a quid pro quo ­ a specific intent to give or receive something of value in exchange for an official act. An illegal gratuity, on the other hand, may constitute merely a reward for some future act that the public official will take (and may already have determined to take), or for a past act that he has already taken. The punishments prescribed for the two offenses reflect their relative seriousness: Bribery may be punished by up to 15 years' imprisonment, a fine of $250,000 ($500,000 for organizations) or triple the value of the bribe, whichever is greater, and disqualification from holding government office. Violation of the illegal gratuity statute, on the other hand, may be punished by up to two years' imprisonment and a fine of $250,000 ($500,000 for organizations).34
31. Amy F. Dunathan & Nilam A. Sanghvi, Federal Criminal Conflict of Interest, 38 A. Crim. L. Rev. 733, 735 & n.11 (2001). 32. United States. V. Sun-Diamond Growers of Cal., 526 U.S. 397, 409 (1999). 33. 18 U.S.C. §201 (2006). 34. United States v. Sun-Diamond Growers of Cal., 526 U.S. 397, 404-05 (1999).

6 ­ Prosecution of Public Corruption in the United States

109

The illegal gratuity's requirement that it be "for or because of" an official act is referred to as the "nexus requirement."35 Because the nexus requirement does not include bribery's elements of corrupt intent or specific quid pro quo, American juries are often instructed that an illegal gratuity is a lesser-includedoffense of bribery. In addition to the lesser intent requirement, there are two other important differences between these two offenses. First, although both cover present and future public officials, only illegal gratuities applies to past public officials. Second, illegal gratuity offenses only reach attempts to influence "official acts," while bribery applies both to "official acts" and to any violation of a public official's lawful duty.36

3.1. The Nexus Requirement: United States v. Sun-Diamond Growers of California
Before the U.S. Supreme Court's decision in United States v. Sun-Diamond Growers of California, 526 U.S. 397 (1999), lower courts interpreted the nexus requirement for illegal gratuities in two divergent ways. Some courts held that the requisite link between a gratuity and an official act could be met simply by the recipient's status as a public official. Other courts required more of an identifiable link between the gratuity and a specified official act, treating an illegal gratuity violation more like a bribery offense.37 The unanimous Sun-Diamond Court clearly confirmed that the illegal gratuity statute was confined to the more narrow approach. The Court held that "to establish a violation of 18 U.S.C. §201(c)(1)(A), the Government must prove a link between a thing of value conferred upon a public official and a specific `official act' for or because of which it was given."38 The Court flatly rejected the view that a gratuity prosecution could be based solely on the giving of a thing of value an official because of his position "to build a reservoir of goodwill that might ultimately affect one or more of a multitude of unspecified acts, now and in the future."39 As a result of this interpretation, the use of the illegal gratuity statute is relegated to instances where corrupt intent cannot be established for bribery, but where the government can establish a requisite link between the gratuity and a specific official act. This interpretation, however, leaves many instances of public corruption unpunished because gifts to public officials by those whom
35. Michael L. Seigel, White Collar Crim: Law, Procedure, Practice and Theory, forthcoming Chapter 10: Public Corruption (Aspen Publishers) (2011). 36. A. David Pardo, Jonathan Hill, & Patrick Alberts, Public Corruption, 44 Am. Crim. L. Rev. 855, 858 (2007). 37. Id. at 865. 38. United States v. Sun-Diamond Growers of Cal., 526 U.S. 397, 414 (1999). 39. Id. at 405.

110

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

they are suppose to regulate cannot be prosecuted unless a provable official act can be connected to the gift. In effect, this permits lobbyists (and others) to put politicians on a "general retainer."

3.2. Mens Rea (Intent)
As discussed above, bribery and illegal gratuities differ most importantly with respect to the element of intent. To prove bribery under §201(b), the government must establish a corrupt intent to influence, or be influenced in, an official act. Understandably, courts have had a difficult time discerning just how the corrupt intent requirement adds anything of significance to the crime's definition. Faced with this dilemma, and in light of the Supreme Court's Sun Diamond decision, most courts have decided that acting corruptly means having in mind a specific quid pro quo. In other words, the courts have collapsed the intent and quid quo pro elements into one. Consider, for example, United States v. Alfisi, 308 F.3d 144, 149 (2d Cir. 2002). In that case, the defendant was convicted of bribery and paying unlawful gratuities to a U.S. Department of Agriculture produce inspector. On appeal, the Second Circuit wrestled with clarifying the difference between the intent element of bribery and the lesser-included-offense of illegal gratuity.40 It concluded:
The "corrupt" intent necessary to a bribery conviction is in the nature of a quid pro quo requirement; that is, there must be "a specific intent to give ... something of value in exchange for an official act." Putting it only slightly differently, bribery involves the giving of value to procure a specific official action from a public official. The element of a quid pro quo or a direct exchange is absent from the offense of paying an unlawful gratuity. To commit that offense, it is enough that the payment be a reward for a past official act or made in the hope of obtaining general good will in the payee's performance of official acts off in the future. ... In defining "corruptly," the district court stated that the term entailed a "specific intent to influence Inspector Cashin's official acts of performing inspections and certifying the condition and grade of fruits and vegetables." The payment of an unlawful gratuity was described as the giving of money "for or because of official acts performed or to be performed by William Cashin." More specifically, the district court told the jury that to prove the payment of an unlawful gratuity, the government must show a link between the payment and some official act, but not corrupt intent as earlier defined. ****
40. Id. at 146.

6 ­ Prosecution of Public Corruption in the United States

111

The jury evidently found the instructions somewhat ambiguous with regard to the difference between bribery and paying unlawful gratuities and asked the court for clarification. In response, the court described bribery as the giving of money to a public official "for or because of an official act [and] with a corrupt intention specifically to influence the outcome of the official act." This instruction clearly set out the quid pro quo requirement, and, accordingly, any ambiguity in the original jury instructions was cured. The events here suggest that it might be well to add similar language in the future for such jury instructions.41

Furthermore, the corrupt intent element of bribery need not change the public official's course of action or require him to divert from faithfully performing his job. The intent element is satisfied not by an impact on the final outcome of the official act. Rather, corrupt intent simply requires that the intent of the payee is to "purchase a particular decision" regardless if that decision aligns with the public official's legal duty.42 "[I]t does not matter whether the government official would have to change his or her conduct to satisfy the payee's expectations."43 Courts have found the necessary corrupt intent in a variety of situations, including a public housing official who sought $500 cash payments from clients in exchange for low-income housing vouchers; a Social Security Administration law clerk who demanded payment from a disabled child's parent in exchange for valid documents; and a Small Business Administration official who exchanged a promise of future employment for maintaining a company's program eligibility.44

3.3. Public Official
Section 201 restricts giving a bribe or illegal gratuity to a "public official," a "person who has been selected to be a public official," or a "witness." A "public official" is defined as a
[m]ember of Congress, Delegate, or Resident Commissioner, either before or after such official has qualified, or an officer or employee or person acting for or on behalf of the United States or any department, agency or branch of Government thereof, including the District of Columbia, in any official function, under or by authority of any such department, agency, or branch of Government, or a juror.45

41. Id. at 149-51. 42. Id. at 151-52. 43. Unites States v. Quinn, 359 F.3d 666, 675 (4th Cir. 2004). 44. Amy F. Dunathan & Nilam A. Sanghvi, Federal Criminal Conflict of Interest, 38 A. Crim. L. Rev. 733, 743 (2001). 45. 18 U.S.C. §201(a)(1) (2006).

112

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

Additionally, §201(a)(2) defines a "person who has been selected to be a public official" as "any person who has been nominated or appointed to be a public official, or has been officially informed that such person will be so nominated or appointed." Lastly, a "witness" is defined as a person testifying at "a trial, hearing, or other proceeding, before any court, any committee of either House or both Houses of Congress, or any agency, commission, or officer authorized by the laws of the United States to hear evidence or take testimony." Federal employees or those contractually bound to the United States government are straightforward examples of "public officials" as included in the definition under §201. However, the more troublesome demarcation concerns individuals who have no formal employment contract or agreement with the federal government. In Dixson v. United States, 465 U.S. 482, 492-93 (1984), the Supreme Court clarified that the term "public official," particularly because of the phrase "person acting for or on behalf of the United States," encompasses an individual who possesses "some degree of official responsibility for carrying out a federal program or policy."46 The defendants in Dixson were hired by the local municipality to supply low income housing to qualified individuals using federal block grants provided to the municipality by the federal Department of Housing and Urban Development (HUD).47 In addition to administering contracts with persons seeking housing assistance, they also contracted with entities that rehabilitated housing to make it habitable. Defendants were convicted under §201 for accepting bribes from certain rehabilitation companies. On appeal, they argued that, as sub-grantees rather than direct recipients of federal funds, they were not "public officials" because they never entered "into any agreement with the United States or any subdivision of the federal government." The government countered that the lower court had correctly decided that defendants "were acting as federal agents in the sense of having discretion in administering the expenditure of federal funds."48 The U.S. Supreme Court agreed with the government and lower court's analysis. However, the Court added that:
By finding petitioner to be public officials within the meaning of section 201(a), we do not mean to suggest that the mere presence of some federal assistance brings a local organization and its employees within the jurisdiction of the federal bribery statute or even that all employees of local organizations responsible for administering federal grant programs are public officials within the meaning of section 201(a)...
46. Dixson v. United States, 465 U.S. 482, 499 (1984). 47. Id. at 484. 48. Id. at 490-91.

6 ­ Prosecution of Public Corruption in the United States

113

We recognized that the manner in which the HCDA block grant program combines local administration with federal funding initially creates some confusion as to whether local authorities administering HCDA grants should be considered public officials under the federal bribery statute. However, when one examines the structure of the program and sees that the HCDA vests in local administrators like petitioners Hinton and Dixson the power to allocate federal fiscal resources for the purpose of achieving congressionally-established goals, the confusion evaporates and it becomes clear that these local officials hold precisely the sort of positions of national public trust that Congress intended to cover with the "acting for or on behalf of" language in the bribery statute. The federal government has a strong and legitimate interest in prosecuting petitioners for their misuse of government funds...49

Although some potential for confusion still exists, the Eleventh Circuit Court of Appeals seems to have located the line that the Supreme Court was trying to draw in the Dixson case. In United States v. Evans, 344 F.3d 1131 (11th Cir. 2003), that court found that the critical distinction is between parties who have discretion to redistribute federal funds, which makes them public officials for purposes of the statute, and those who are mere "final recipients" of such funds.

3.4. Official Act
An "official act" is required to trigger §201's liability for both bribes and illegal gratuities. Section 201(a)(1)(3) defines "official act" as "any decision or action on any question, matter, cause, suit, proceeding or controversy, which may at any time be pending, or which may by law be brought before any public official, in such official capacity, or in such official's place of trust or profit."50 To prove an official act, the prosecution need not show that the conduct was an enumerated responsibility of a public official. Rather, it is sufficient if the government can establish that the conduct was "clearly established by settled practice" to be part of the public official's position.51 In a recent case in which a Virginia congressman faced bribery charges, the district court addressed the nuances of how a public official can use his position to exert influence in a manner that would constitute an official act, even though his actions were not specific conduct authorized by his congressional position.52 It stated:

49. 50. 51. 52.

Id. at 499-501. 18 U.S.C. §201(a)(3) (2006). United States v. Birdsall, 233 U.S. 223, 231 (1914). United States v. Jefferson, 634 F. Supp. 2d 595, 602 (E.D. Va. 2009).

114

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

First, analysis of what constitutes an "official act" must begin with ­ and indeed, must be anchored in ­ the plain language of the statute defining that term... . [T] he plain language of §201(b)(2)(A) and §201 (a)(3), taken together and applied to a specific case like this one, requires the government to adduce proof with respect to the "official act" element that defendant solicited a thing of value in exchange for being influenced in this performance of (i) a decision or action (ii) on a question, matter, cause, suit, proceeding or controversy (iii) which could at any time have been pending, or which could by law have been brought before him, in his official capacity, or in his place of trust or profit. **** Next, it is important to address what constitutes a "question, matter, cause, suit, proceeding or controversy," and what must be shown for that "question, matter," etc., to be one "which may at any time be pending, or which may by law be brought before ... the charged official ... in such official's official capacity, or in such official's place of trust or profit." §201(a)(3). In this respect, it has long been settled that these phrases do not require proof that the alleged "official act" was taken pursuant to responsibilities explicitly assigned by law; rather, as the Supreme Court held long ago, these phrases are intended to describe those activities that have been "clearly established by settled practice" as part of a public official's position... . Thus, where, as here, the charged public official is a congressman, the universe of "official acts" described by §201(a)(3) is not limited to so-called "legislative acts" such as voting on or introducing a piece of legislation; rather, §201(a)(3) has been read "sufficiently broadly to encompass all of the acts normally thought to constitute a congressman's legitimate use of his office." Moreover, it is also settled that the charged public official need not have authority to make a final decision or take binding action on the "question, matter," etc., at issue. Rather, §201(a)(3) "cover[s] any situation in which the advice or recommendation of a [public official] would be influential, irrespective of the [official]'s specific authority (or lack of same) to make a binding decision." ... [I]t is sufficient for the government to adduce proof, including expert testimony or evidence of defendant's admissions and conduct, that is was customary, as a matter of clearly established settled practice, for members of Congress in defendant's position to exert influence ­ by advice, recommendation, or otherwise ­ on the issues in question. Instructive in demonstrating how the government might meet this burden are Carson and Biaggi, two Second Circuit cases addressing exertion of influence by a congressman or his staff on resolution of questions, matters, causes, suits, proceedings or controversies not by law within a congressman's purview to resolve. First, in Carson, the Second Circuit rejected could not support a jury verdict for solicitation of bribes in exchange for "official acts." ... Similarly, in Biaggi the

6 ­ Prosecution of Public Corruption in the United States

115

Second Circuit rejected a congressman's arguments that his actions in lobbying various municipal and federal entities on behalf of individuals who made payments "were not `official act[s]' within the meaning of §201 because they were not legislative acts and because they were directed principally toward municipal, not federal, agencies." Rather, the Second Circuit held that "[a] congressman's own invocation of his position and of congressional interest in his intercession with others on behalf of a constituent" constituted an official act... .53

Other acts found to be "official acts" include (1) attempting to persuade a deputy attorney general to quash a federal criminal prosecution; (2) lobbying various municipal and federal entities; (3) funding housing projects; (4) failing to report licensing violations of massage parlors;54 and (5) "us[ing] ... governmental computer systems to create fraudulent documents for the benefit of the employee or a third party for compensation, even when the [defendant's] scope of authority does not formally encompass the act."55 One crucial caveat to the "official act" clause is the expanded scope under the bribery provisions. Under §201, bribery prohibits not only receiving a "thing of value" in exchange for a specific official act, but also being induced to do or omit to do any act in violation of an "official duty." The illegal gratuity offense does not include this latter reference to violating an official duty. Therefore, bribery (the more serious crime) encompasses a much broader scope of proscribed behavior than the lesser included offense of illegal gratuity. Courts justify the increased jurisdiction of bribery through its countervailing requirement of a specific quid pro quo.56

3.5. Thing of Value
For an act to trigger liability as a bribe or illegal gratuity under §201(b) or (c)(1), an individual must provide the recipient with a "thing of value."57 Courts have broadly interpreted this term; any item qualifies as long as the recipient subjectively believes it has value.58 This includes not only monetary and tangible gifts, but also intangible benefits, regardless if the object has little or no commercial worth. Courts have recognized many items as "things of value,"

53. Id. at 600-04. 54. Amy F. Dunathan & Nilam A. Sanghvi, Federal Criminal Conflict of Interest, 38 Am. Crim. L. Rev. 733, 736-37 (2001). 55. United States v. Parker, 133 F.3d 322, 326 (5th Cir. 1998). 56. Valdes v. United States, 475 F.3d 1319, 1333 (D.C. Cir. 2007). 57. 18 U.S.C. §§201(b), (c) (2006). 58. A. David Pardo, Jonathan Hill & Patrick Alberts, Public Corruption, 44 Am. Crim. L. Rev. 855, 859 (2007) (citing United States v. Crozier, 987 F.2d 893, 901 (2d Cir. 1993) ("[A]ny payment that [recipient] defendant subjectively believes has value ... constitutes a `thing of value.'")).

116

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

including promises of future employment, vacations, illegal immigration documents, shares of stock, and unsecured loans.59 The thing of value may be given to the public official either before or after the official act occurs. Bribes tend to be paid in advance, although "it is only logical that in certain situations the bribe will not actually be conveyed until the act is done."60 Likewise, gratuities are usually paid after the official act is accomplished; however, they may "be conveyed before the occurrence of the act (so long as the payor believes the official has already committed himself to the action)."61

4. Federal Program Bribery, Theft, or Fraud, 18 U.S.C. §666
The federal program bribery provision, codified at 18 U.S.C. §666(a)(2), broadly proscribes bribing or attempting to bribe a member of an organization or local government, or any agency thereof, if that entity receives federal funding. The statute has two key limitations: the bribe must exceed $5,000 and the recipient of the bribe must be an agent of an entity that receives more than $10,000 in benefits from the federal government.62 There are additional requirements, most importantly that the prosecution must prove corrupt intent by the actor giving or receiving the bribe, respectively. Key disputes arise under the statute as to what constitutes "benefits" from the federal government and whether gratuities are included in the prohibition against "influenc[ing] or reward[ing]" agents.63 Congress enacted §666 to "protect the integrity of the vast sums of money distributed through Federal programs from theft, fraud, and undue influence by bribery."64 The two prior federal statutes directed at threats to federal funds were deemed ineffective at safeguarding the proper use of disbursed federal aid: the federal theft statute only prevented direct theft of unadulterated federal funds and the federal bribery statute originally only reached federal officials, leaving no recourse against state and local actors. Congress passed 18 U.S.C. §666 to fill these regulatory gaps. However, it was not intended to become a general catch-all provision that would criminalizes all state and local malfeasance, as this would intrude on the sovereignty of the States.65
59. A. David Pardo, Jonathan Hill & Patrick Alberts, Public Corruption, 44 Am. Crim. L. Rev. 855, 859 (2007). 60. United States v. Jennings, 160 F.3d 1006, 1014 (4th Cir. 1998)(quoting United States v. Campbell, 684 F.2d 141, 148 (D.C. Cir. 1982). 61. Jennings, 160 F.3d 1014. 62. 18 U.S.C. §666(a)(2) (2006). 63. See generally, Anthony A. Joseph, Public Corruption: The Government's Expansive View in Pursuit of Local and State Officials, 38 Cumb. L. Rev. 567, 571-74 (2008). 64. Sabri v. United States, 541 U.S. 600, 606 (2004). 65. Philip M. Schreiber, Accepting a New Genre of Federal Criminal Laws?: How the Supreme Court's Sabri Decision Increases Avenues of Congressional Authority Without Providing Adequate Safeguards for States and Individuals, 12 Widener L. Rev. 585, 594 (2006).

6 ­ Prosecution of Public Corruption in the United States

117

4.1. Federal Jurisdiction: Sabri v. United States
Contrary to the typical federal criminal statute that is authorized under Congress's constitutional power to regulate interstate commerce, the federal program bribery provision was enacted pursuant to Congress's Spending Powers enumerated in Article I, §8, cl. 1, and its corresponding authority derived from the Necessary and Proper Clause, Art. I, §8, cl. 18, in the U.S. Consitution. As a result, federal program bribery does not require an impact on interstate commerce. Moreover, there is not even a requirement that the prosecution prove a direct connection between the federal funds received by the local entity and the bribe given to the entity's agent. In other words, there is no statutory element requiring the government to establish that federal funds were affected by the bribe. Rather, simple proof that the bribe exceeded $5,000 and that the entity received more than $10,000 in federal benefits in one year suffices to create the requisite federal interest.66 The U.S. Supreme Court explained the rationale for this interpretation in Sabri v. United States, 541 U.S. 600 (2004):
Congress does not have to sit by and accept the risk of operations thwarted by local and state improbity. Section 666(a)(2) addresses the problem at the sources of bribes, by rational means, to safeguard the integrity of the state, local, and tribal recipients of federal dollars. It is true, just as Sabri says, that not every bribe or kickback offered or paid to agents of governments covered by §666(b) will be traceably skimmed from specific federal payments, or show up in the guise of a quid pro quo for some dereliction in spending a federal grant. But this possibility portends no enforcement beyond the scope of federal interest, for the reason that corruption does not have to be that limited to affect the federal interest. Money is fungible, bribed officials are untrustworthy stewards of federal funds, and corrupt contractors do not deliver dollar-for-dollar value. Liquidity is not a financial term for nothing; money can be drained off here because a federal grant is pouring in there. And officials are not any the less threatening to the objects behind federal spending just because they may accept general retainers. It is certainly enough that the statutes condition the offense in a threshold amount of federal dollars defining the federal interest, such as that provided here.67

66. Sabri, 541 U.S. at 605. 67. Id. at 605-06 (emphasis added).

118

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

4.2. "Benefits in Excess of $10,000"
A large source of contention in connection with §666 centers on what constitutes "benefits" under the statute. The government must prove that the agency or governmental entity to which the defendant was tied received federal "benefits in excess of $10,000 under a Federal program." In Fischer v. United States, 529 U.S. 667 (2000), the Supreme Court crafted the standard for deciding when an organization participating in a federal assistance program is a recipient of federal "benefits." The Court held that "an examination must be undertaken of the program's structure, operation, and purpose. The inquiry should examine the conditions under which the organization receives the federal payments. The answer could depend... on whether the recipient's own operations are one of the reasons for maintaining the program." In Fischer, the Court found that Medicare payments to local and state hospitals met this standard, as the "payments [were] made not simply to reimburse for treatment of qualifying patients but to assist the hospital in making available and maintaining a certain level and quality of medical care, all in the interest of both the hospital and the greater community." Thus, Fischer's kickbacks to an officer of a Medicare-recipient hospital qualified as criminalized activity under §666. 68 However, the Fischer Court explicitly held that not all federal funds disbursed through an assistance program will result in liability for fraud under §666(b). "Any receipt of federal funds can, at some level of generality, be characterized as a benefit. The statute does not employ this broad, almost limitless use of the term. Doing so would turn almost every act of fraud or bribery into a federal offense, upsetting the proper federal balance."69 Following this reasoning, one court held that an employee of a private technical school, which did not receive federal funds directly, could not be prosecuted under the theory that the institution participated in federal student loan programs. This indirect benefit to the attending students would not satisfy the "benefits in excess of $10,000" to the school as required by the statute.70

4.3. Corrupt Intent and Gratuities
It is unclear whether proof of a purely gratuitous transfer, such as an illegal gratuity as defined under 18 U.S.C. §201, would trigger liability under §666. Thus far, courts have effectively evaded this question by finding defendants who claim that they merely paid gratuities guilty, in fact, of making bribes. When they face the issue head-on, courts will have to decide whether the term "reward" in §666 infers the inclusion of gratuities, on the one hand, or whether
68. 529 U.S. 667, 681 (2000). 69. Id. 70. United States v. Wyncoop, 11 F.3d 119, 120-21 (9th Cir. 1993).

6 ­ Prosecution of Public Corruption in the United States

119

the statute's corrupt intent requirement limits its applications to bribes because it implies the need for a specific quid pro quo, on the other.71

5. Extortion Under Color of Official Right, 18 U.S.C. §1951(b)(2)
As early as the 1970s, the Hobbs Act, 18 U.S.C. §1952, has criminalized politicians who extort property from another individual under color of official right.72 The pertinent part of the Hobbs Act proscribing extortion provides:
(a) Whoever in any way or degree obstructs, delays, or affects commerce or the movement of any article or commodity in commerce, by robbery or extortion or attempts or conspires so to do ... shall be fined under this title or imprisoned not more than twenty years, or both. (b) As used in this section ... (2) The term "extortion" means the obtaining of property from another, with his consent, induced by wrongful use of actual or threatened force, violence, or fear, or under color of official right.73

Over the years, this section of the Hobbs Act has played a central role in the expansion of federal jurisdiction over local and state public corruption.74 This expansion has been supported through five key interpretational developments: that (1) the purchased political influence need only be motivated based on the recipient's office; (2) the payee needs only "reasonably believe" that the government official can exercise the purchased influence; (3) no affirmative act of inducement by the public official is required; (4) only a loosened form of quid pro quo is required; and (5) a de minimis impact on interstate commerce is sufficient. These matters are examined in more detail below. First, the government official who extorts a payee does not need to be fulfilling an official duty within his official position or performing acts unrelated to his official duties that could only be completed because of his official position.75 Rather, "[s]o long as the motivation for the payment focuses on the recipient's office, the conduct falls within the ambit of 18 U.S.C. §1951."76 For example, in Unites States v. Rindone, 631 F.2d 491 (7th Cir. 1980), the defendant, a city electrical inspector, asserted that he could not be guilty "under color of official right"
71. This issue is discussed in dicta in United States v. Jennings, 160 F. 3d 1006, 1015-16 n.4 (4th Cir. 1998). 72. James P. Arguelles, Is the Hobbs Act the Next Statute Subject to Abuse in the Prosecution of Public Corruption Cases?, 46 No. 6 Crim. Law Bulletin 1 (2010). 73. 18 U.S.C. §1953(a), (b)(2) (2006). 74. James P. Arguelles, Is the Hobbs Act the Next Statute Subject to Abuse in the Prosecution of Public Corruption Cases?, 46 No. 6 Crim. Law Bulletin 1 (2010). 75. See id. at 1-2. 76. United States v. Braasch, 505 F.2d 139, 150-51 (7th Cir. 1974).

120

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

because he had no authority to issue the permits sought by electrical contractors in exchange for payments. The court responded that it was immaterial whether the extortion involved a promise to perform acts unauthorized by his official duties. It only mattered that the illegal payments were motivated by the electrical inspector's office.77 Second, the government official is criminally liable for extortion under "color of official right" if he or she exploits a reasonable belief by the payee that the official has authority to deliver the promised acts.78 In United States v. Mazzei, 521 F.2d 639 (3d Cir. 1975), the defendant was a state senator without any power over granting leases to buildings. But the court found that "the jury need not have concluded that he had actual de jure power to secure grant of the lease so long as it found that [the payee] held, and defendant exploited, a reasonable belief that the state system so operated that the power in fact of defendant's office included the effective authority to determine recipients of the state leases here involved."79 Even more dramatically, the Sixth Circuit held that "[r]easonable prospects of success are sufficient. Indeed ... we would be inclined to say that any attempt by a public official to sell his influence over others runs afoul of the Hobbs Act. Political influence is not a commodity to be bought and sold on the open market."80 Third, the U.S. Supreme Court has flatly rejected the interpretation of §1951(b)(2) that would require an affirmative act of inducement by the public official. In Evans v. United States, 504 U.S. 255 (1992), the defendant argued on appeal that the Hobbs Act necessitated a "demand" or "request" by the public official. The Supreme Court disagreed, quoting the lower court's holding that "passive acceptance of a benefit by a public official is sufficient to form the basis of a Hobbs Act violation if the official knows that he is being offered the payment in exchange for a specific requested exercise of his official power. The official need not take any specific action to induce the offering of the benefit."81 The Court derived this standard by comparing it to common law extortion and by employing common principles of statutory construction:
At common law, extortion was an offense committed by a public official who took "by colour of his office" money that was not due to him for the performance of his official duties. A demand, or request, by the public official was not an element of the offense... .
77. United States v. Rindone, 631 F.2d 491, 495 (7th Cir. 1980). 78. See James P. Arguelles, Is the Hobbs Act the Next Statute Subject to Abuse in the Prosecution of Public Corruption Cases?, 46 No. 6 Crim. Law Bulletin 2-4 (2010). 79. United States v. Mazzei, 521 F.2d 639, 643 (3d Cir. 1975). 80. United States v. Bibby, 752 F 2d 1116, 1228 (6th Cir. 1985). 81. Evans v. United States, 504 U.S. 255, 258 (1992).

6 ­ Prosecution of Public Corruption in the United States

121

There is nothing in either the statutory text or the legislative history that could fairly be described as a contrary direction from Congress to narrow the scope of the offense... . The two courts that have disagreed with the decision to apply the common-law definition have interpreted the word "induced" as requiring a wrongful use of official power that "begins with the public official, not with the gratuitous actions of another." If we had no common-law history to guide our interpretation of the statutory text, that reading would be plausible. For two reasons, however, we are convinced that it is incorrect. First, we think the word "induced" is a part of the definition of the offense by the private individual, but not the offense by the public official. In the case of the private individual, the victim's consent must be "induced by wrongful use of actual or threatened force, violence or fear." In the case of the public official, however, there is no such requirement. The statute merely requires of the public official that he obtain "property from another, with his consent, ... under color of official right." The use of the word "or" before "under color of official right" supports this reading. Second, even if the statute were parsed so that the word "induced" applied to the public officeholder, we do not believe the word "induced" necessarily indicates that the transaction must be initiated by the recipient of the bribe. Many of the cases applying the majority rule have concluded that the wrongful acceptance of a bribe establishes all the inducement that the statute requires. They conclude that the coercive element is provided by the public office itself. And even the two courts that have adopted an inducement requirement for extortion under color of official right do not require proof that the inducement took the form of a threat or demand.82

Fourth, the Hobbs act appears to have a strict quid pro quo requirement only in the context of campaign contributions.83 As the Supreme Court has explained:
Serving constituents and supporting legislation that will benefit the district and individuals and groups therein is the everyday business of a legislator. It is also true that campaigns must be run and financed. Money is constantly being solicited on behalf of candidates, who run on platforms and who claim support on the basis of their views and what they intend to do or have done. Whatever ethical considerations and appearances may indicate, to hold that legislators commit the federal crime of extortion when they act for the benefit of constituents or

82. Id. at 260-66. 83. See McCormick v. United States, 500 U.S. 257 (1991).

122

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

support legislation furthering the interests of some of their contributions, shortly before or after campaign contributions are solicited and received from those beneficiaries, is an unrealistic assessment of what Congress could have meant by making it a crime to obtain property from another, with his consent, "under color of official right." To hold otherwise would open to prosecution not only conduct that has long been thought to be well within the law but also conduct that in a very real sense is unavoidable so long as election campaigns are financed by private contributions or expenditures, as they have been from the beginning of the Nation. It would require statutory language more explicit than the Hobbs Act contains to justify a contrary conclusion. This is not to say that it is impossible for an elected official to commit extortion in the course of financing an election campaign. Political contributions are of course vulnerable if induced by the use of force, violence, or fear. The receipt of such contributions is also vulnerable under the Act as having been taken under color of official right, but only if the payments are made in return for an explicit promise or undertaking by the official to perform or not to perform an official act.84

The quid pro quo requirement is satisfied at the time the public official "receives a payment in return for his agreement to perform specific official acts; fulfillment of the quid pro quo is not an element of the offense."85 Fifth, and lastly, §1951 requires that the defendant's criminal conduct have an impact on interstate commerce. This impact, however, may be negligible. For example, in United States v. Carter, 530 F.3d 565 (7th Cir. 2008), the defendant claimed that the government failed to prove an effect on interstate commerce because his payments were from a state company to a state official. The court found that the interstate commerce requirement was fulfilled by the "depletion of assets" theory. Under this theory, if a business that customarily purchases goods through interstate commerce has its assets depleted by defendant's extortionate acts, its ability to purchase such goods is diminished. Standing alone, this satisfies the interstate commerce element.86 Furthermore, because the Hobbs Act also criminalizes attempted crimes, it is not required that there be an actual effect on interstate commerce; a showing that the conduct had the potential to impact commerce is sufficient. It should be remembered that the interstate commerce requirement is an element of Hobbs Act extortion and as such must be specifically alleged in the indictment.87

84. Id. at 272. 85. Evans v. United States, 504 U.S. 255, 268 (1992). 86. United States v. Carter, 530 F.3d 565, 572 (7th Cir. 2008). 87. Stirone v. United States, 361 U.S. 212 (1960).

6 ­ Prosecution of Public Corruption in the United States

123

6. Conclusion
The battle against public corruption is never-ending. There is no limit to the variety and ingenuity of corrupt officials' schemes to further their private interests at the expense of the public's money and trust. Three ingredients are key to achieving, if not success, at least an equilibrium in the fight against such crime. The first is a well-funded and uncorrupted contingent of competent law enforcement personnel and prosecutors to man the front lines. The second is an independent and well-functioning criminal justice system. And the third ingredient is the existence of a set of criminal statutes on the books for these players to use to investigate, prosecute, and punish those whose behavior crosses the line. To date, the United States has accomplished the task of minimizing corruption about as well as any other country in the world. But constant vigilance is required to maintain the upper hand.
