or a state attorney general in a criminal case that is based on the same original information that the whistleblower voluntarily provided to the SEC and that led the SEC to obtain monetary sanctions totaling more than $1 million. In determining whether the required $1 million threshold has been satisfied for purposes of making the award, the SEC will exclude any monetary sanctions that the whistleblower is ordered to pay, or that are ordered against any company whose liability is based on conduct that the whistleblower directed, planned, or initiated.39 The purpose of such limitations is to prevent wrongdoers from receiving a financial benefit for blowing the whistle on their own violations.40

4. Exclusions
The Final Rules exclude the following categories of individuals from eligibility for the award subject to certain exceptions discussed below: (1) principals; (2) compliance personnel; (3) accountants; and (4) lawyers who obtain violation information in connection with legal representation.

4.1. Principals
Officers, directors, trustees, or partners of a company who receive information about the alleged misconduct from another person or from the company's internal Compliance process are excluded from receiving an award.41 However, there is an exception; such individuals may serve as whistleblowers and be eligible for an award if they learn about violations in another manner, such as by observing other members of senior management engaging in a securities law violation.42 In explaining the limitations on the scope of this exclusion, the SEC has stated that "including all supervisors at any level would create too sweeping an exclusion of persons who may be in a key position to learn about misconduct." 43

4.2. Compliance Personnel
Employees whose principal responsibilities include compliance or internal audit functions, as well as individuals retained by a company to perform
39. 17 C.F.R. § 240.21F-16. 40. Adoption Release at 194 (explaining that 17 C.F.R. § 240.21F-16 "appropriately incentivizes culpable whistleblowers to report securities violations while preventing culpable whistleblowers from financially benefiting from their own misconduct or misconduct for which they are substantially responsible"). 41. 17 C.F.R. § 240.21F-4(b)(4)(iii)(A). 42. Adoption Release at 71-72; see also id. at 71 (explaining that 17 C.F.R. § 240.21F-4(b)(4)(iii)(A) "is not intended to establish a general bar against officers, directors, and other designated persons becoming whistleblowers any time they observe possible violations at a company or other entity"). 43. Id, at 71 (footnote omitted).

258

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

Compliance or internal audit functions, also are ineligible for an award.44 The Final Rules, however, do not clearly extend the exclusions to other company employees involved in accounting functions such as accounting and financial reporting personnel. Individuals retained by the company to conduct an inquiry or investigate possible law violations also are ineligible to receive an award.45

4.3. Accountants
Employees of, and other persons associated with, public accounting firms who obtain information through an engagement required under the federal securities laws, including any foreigners working directly on the audit or review engagement,46 are not eligible for an award if that information relates to a violation by the engagement client or the client's directors, officers, or other employees.47 This includes public company auditors who report information to the SEC in accordance with Section 10A of the Exchange Act since making a whistleblower submission would be contrary to the requirements for auditor reporting of potential illegal activity specified in that section of the Exchange Act.48

4.4. Attorneys Who Obtain Information in Connection with Legal Representation
The Act prohibits attorneys from using information obtained from client engagements or protected by the attorney-client privilege to make whistleblower claims unless disclosure of the information is permitted under SEC rules pertaining to the conduct of attorneys representing public companies (e.g., 17 C.F.R. § 205.3(d)(2)) or state bar ethics rules.49 The Final Rules provide that this exclusion applies to in-house attorneys who may be eligible for an award insofar as their disclosures are consistent with their ethical obligations and SEC rules.50 The exclusion for privileged information, moreover, extends to non-attorneys who learn information through confidential attorney-client communications with company counsel.51

4.5. Other Exclusions
Individuals who obtain information in a way that is determined by a U.S. court to have violated federal or state criminal laws are excluded from receiving
44. 17 C.F.R. § 240.21F-4(b)(4)(iii)(B). 45. 17 C.F.R. § 240.21F-4(b)(4)(iii)(C). 46. Adoption Release at 143. 47. 17 C.F.R. § 240.21F-4(b)(4)(iii)(D). 48. 17 C.F.R. § 240.21F-8(c)(4). 49. 17 C.F.R. § 240.21F-4(b)(4)(i), (ii); Adoption Release at 60. 50. Adoption Release at 59-60. 51. Id. at 59.

12 ­ How whistleblowing may pay under the U.S. Dodd-Frank Act: implications...

259

an award.52 In addition, foreign government officials, including employees of state-owned enterprises, are ineligible for an award.53 Finally, anyone who acquires original information from a person "[w]ith the intent to evade any provision" of the Final Rules is excluded from receiving an award.54

4.6. Exceptions to the Exclusions
Whistleblowers who fall into the principals, compliance, and accounting exceptions described above may nonetheless be eligible for a whistleblower award if (1) they reasonably believe that disclosure to the SEC is necessary to prevent the company from engaging in conduct that is likely to cause substantial injury to the company or its investors; (2) they reasonably believe that the company is engaging in conduct that will impede an investigation of misconduct; or (3) at least 120 days have elapsed since the whistleblower made an internal report to the audit committee, chief legal officer, or chief compliance officer at the company, or 120 days have elapsed since the whistleblower received the information at a time when any of such company officials knew about the information already.55 Although the SEC has said that the 120-day provision "is not intended to, and does not, create any new or special duties of disclosure on entities to report violations or possible violations of law to the [SEC]," companies may, in fact, report to the SEC more possible violations in order to reduce the incidence of whistleblower reports to the SEC and to demonstrate the seriousness with which they consider any employee reports of concerns or complaints.56

5. Treatment of Culpable Whistleblowers
Under the Final Rules, any whistleblower who is convicted of a criminal violation related to the SEC's action or a related action is ineligible for an award.57 Neither the Dodd-Frank Act nor the Final Rules, however, categorically disqualify any other culpable whistleblowers from collecting an award. In fact, the Final Rules expressly contemplate situations in which whistleblowers

engage in culpable conduct and still receive awards.58 The SEC based its decision on the "use a rogue to catch a rogue" enforcement principle, reasoning that sophisticated securities fraud schemes are difficult to detect and prosecute "without insider information and assistance from participants in the scheme or their coconspirators."59

6. Anti-Retaliation Provisions
In drafting the whistleblower protections, the Congressional Committee on Banking, Housing, and Urban Affairs explicitly acknowledged the "enormous risk of blowing the whistle in calling attention to fraud."60 The Dodd-Frank Act addresses this risk by prohibiting retaliation against employees who report to the government, or assist the government in an investigation of, conduct that the employee reasonably believes is a violation of the federal securities laws by publicly traded companies.61 It also protects employees that make disclosures that are required or protected under the Sarbanes-Oxley Act of 2002 ("SOX"), the Exchange Act, and any other law, rule, or regulation subject to the jurisdiction of the SEC.62 Under the Act, no employer may "discharge, demote, suspend, threaten, harass, directly or indirectly, or in any other manner discriminate against" any employee who provides information to the SEC, assists in an SEC investigation, or makes a required or protected disclosure.63 The statute also provides aggrieved employees with a cause of action in federal court and remedies that include reinstatement with the same seniority status that the employee would have had but for the retaliation, double back pay with interest, and compensation for litigation costs, including attorneys' fees.64 To be afforded protection from retaliation, whistleblowers must (1) possess a "reasonable belief" that the information they are providing relates to a possible federal securities law violation that "has occurred, is ongoing, or is about to occur"; and (2) report that information in accordance with the procedures outlined in the rules.65 The Final Rules provide that the anti-retaliation provisions

While the SEC has taken the position that employers cannot require their employees to waive or limit their anti-retaliation rights,68 the Dodd-Frank Act's anti-waiver provision does not appear to apply to Section 21F.69

7. Confidentiality Provision
Although the Dodd-Frank Act contains confidentiality provisions in order to protect the identity of the whistleblower, its provisions do not guarantee confidentiality.70 The Final Rules specifically provide that the SEC shall not disclose any information that could reasonably be expected to reveal the identity of the whistleblower unless disclosure to a defendant is required in connection with a federal court or administrative action, or the SEC determines that it is necessary to disclose the identity to regulatory agencies at the federal or state level, or to foreign securities authorities or foreign law enforcement authorities, in order to advance the objectives of the Exchange Act, 15 U.S.C. § 78a, and to protect investors.71 The Final Rules also require that anonymous whistleblowers be represented by an attorney who must certify that he or she has verified the whistleblower's identity.72

8. Implications for Foreign Whistleblowers
While neither the Dodd-Frank Act nor the Final Rules explicitly state that foreign nationals are included among eligible whistleblowers, they contain no language restricting participation to U.S. citizens. The only reference to foreign nationals that appears in the Final Rules, excludes foreign government officials (including employees of state-owned enterprises) from obtaining the whistleblower award.73

8.1. Application of the Anti-Retaliation Provisions to Foreign Whistleblowers
While the Dodd-Frank Act appears to apply to foreign nationals and has attracted international whistleblowers,74 it does not establish how the anti-retaliation provisions would be enforced in a situation involving foreign whistleblowers employed by foreign employers.75 The enforcement section of the anti-retaliation provisions merely states that a cause of action may be filed "in the appropriate district court of the United States," and subpoenas requiring the attendance of witnesses at a trial or hearing "may be served at any place in the United States."76 As a result, to the extent that foreign nationals seek to exercise their rights under the anti-retaliation provisions, such litigants may have to absorb the costs of litigation in U.S. courts, and may face significant obstacles in corralling witnesses. In the anti-retaliation cause of action section of SOX, Section 806, codified at 18 U.S.C. § 1514A, Congress failed to establish clear intent for extraterritorial coverage, and several courts have declined to apply the law's protections to a foreign worker at a foreign subsidiary of a U.S. company.77 In Carnero v. Boston Scientific Corp., 433 F.3d 1 (1st Cir. 2006), the Court of Appeals for the First Circuit held that Section 806 did not have extraterritorial application and therefore rejected the plaintiff's request to extend whistleblower protection to foreign employees working abroad.78 The court based its decision on the statute's silence as to its extraterritorial application, finding that it failed to

73. 17 C.F.R. § 240.21F-8(c)(2). The Final Rules further provide that anyone who obtains his or her information from foreign government officials is ineligible for the award. 17 C.F.R. § 240.21F-8(c)(6). 74. As noted in the introduction, 10% of the whistleblower tips for fiscal year 2011 came from foreign whistleblowers. See SEC, Annual Report on the Dodd-Frank Whistleblower Program: Fiscal Year 2011, App. B (Nov. 2011). 75. Exchange Act Section 21F(h)(1)(B)(i)-(ii). 77. Carnero v. Boston Scientific Corp., 433 F.3d 1, 9 (1st Cir. 2006). 78. Id. at 17.

address important issues such as enforcement, conflicts of laws, investigatory powers, venue, and certain logistical concerns.79 In Beck v. Citigroup, Inc., Case No. 2006-SOX-00003 (Aug. 1, 2006), an administrative law judge ("ALJ") cited Carnero in holding that SOX did not cover a German national employed by a subsidiary of a publicly traded U.S. company, despite the global scale of the employer and the employee's frequent contact with the United States. The employee in Beck worked primarily in Germany and engaged in whistleblowing activity in the United States; the senior executives who terminated his employment were based in the United States.80 Although the court recognized the "interrelationship of the various subsidiaries of Citigroup, Inc.," it refused to assert extraterritorial jurisdiction because the employee was not an American citizen and was employed by a foreign subsidiary.81 Some courts, however, have found that SOX's anti-retaliation cause of action applies to workers at foreign subsidiaries. In O'Mahony v. Accenture LTD, 537 F. Supp. 2d 506 (S.D.N.Y. Feb. 5, 2008), the court found that SOX protections applied to an overseas employee working for a Bermuda company's French subsidiary. The court explained that, unlike in Carnero, the plaintiff had previously worked in the United States for an American subsidiary of Accenture, and "the alleged wrongful conduct and other material acts occurred in the United States by persons located in the United States."82 As a result, the court concluded that its "exercise of jurisdiction... would not implicate extraterritorial application of American law."83 An ALJ reached a similar finding with respect to an overseas employee of a foreign subsidiary in Walters v. Deutsche Bank AG, Case No. 2008-SOX70 (Mar. 23, 2009), "focus[ing] less on the location of employment than the location of the misconduct."84 The ALJ explained that, similar to the plaintiff in O'Mahony, Walters "does not ask for the intervention of American law in a dispute between foreigners that occurred abroad... and all elements essential to establishing a prima facie violation of Section 806 allegedly occurred in the

United States."85 As a result, the ALJ concluded that the "[c]omplaint does not require extraterritorial application of American law" and that SOX's antiretaliation cause of action applies.86 While the Dodd-Frank Act provides anti-retaliation protection for any employee who is terminated by an employer as a result of, among other things, reporting information to the SEC about possible violations of the U.S. federal securities laws,87 neither the Dodd-Frank Act nor its legislative history appears to address the extent to which the right to claim retaliation applies to employees of foreign employers. Moreover, neither the Dodd-Frank Act nor the Final Rules address the extraterritorial concerns outlined by the court in Carnero. Despite these omissions, several commentators have suggested that employees of foreign employers would appear to be covered.88 It remains to be seen whether courts will apply the anti-retaliation protections to foreign whistleblowers.

8.2. Potential Barriers to Recovering the Whistleblower Award
While the Dodd-Frank Act bars a whistleblower who has been convicted of a criminal violation related to the enforcement action from obtaining an award, it is not clear whether a foreign whistleblower with unclean hands, but for whom there is no jurisdiction for criminal prosecution, is eligible for the bounty.89 "Culpability" is one of the factors for determining the amount of the award, and the SEC may consider, inter alia, the whistleblower's role in the security violations, the whistleblower's financial benefit from the violations, and whether the whistleblower is a recidivist.90 The Final Rules nonetheless provide that, if "all of the conditions are met for a whistleblower award ... . the amount will be at least 10 percent ... of the monetary sanctions[.]"91 Similar to other whistleblowers, it is not likely that a foreign whistleblower can receive an award if the information leads to a monetary penalty in a related action but not in the SEC investigation. Section 21F(a)(5) of the Exchange Act provides that a related action must be "based upon the original information ... that led to the successful enforcement of the Commission action." The Final

Rules also state that the related action must be based upon the same original information that resulted in the successful enforcement of an SEC action.92

8.3. Conflicts of Laws
The global patchwork of privacy and data protection laws can give rise to thorny conflict-of-laws disputes. Foreign whistleblowers who are current employees of foreign companies that are either subsidiaries of a U.S. securities filer or a foreign private issuer that files with the SEC, for instance, may provide the U.S. government with information that is proprietary or covered by the attorney-client privilege in the whistleblowers' jurisdiction.93 The transmission of information from a foreign country to the United States, moreover, may violate the foreign country's data privacy or data protection laws. 94 For example, South Africa's National Assembly passed legislation entitled the Protection of Information Bill in November 2011 which criminalizes, inter alia, the collection, communication, or delivery of information deemed to be classified by the government. 95 Other countries have similar national laws. Therefore, there could be substantial legal risks for foreign whistleblowers in their local jurisdictions if they transmit documents and other information to the U.S. government. 96

9. Best Practices Recommendations
By enticing whistleblowers with a share of up to 30% of any enforcement action recovery that exceeds $1 million, the Dodd-Frank Act puts federal regulators in direct competition with company efforts to self-police

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

for wrongdoing through corporate compliance programs. The following best practice recommendations may help companies improve the effectiveness of their existing compliance programs and steer whistleblowers toward internal reporting channels.

9.1. Increase Internal Reporting Channels
Tips are the number one way in which companies detect fraud violations.97 As a result, companies should provide their employees with multiple channels for reporting potential violations, such as anonymous toll-free hotlines and websites that accept anonymous allegations. These reporting mechanisms should be available around the clock and in multiple languages for companies operating internationally. While employees tend to be the most common source of fraud tips, customers, vendors, and competitors, among others, can be vital sources for identifying fraud violations.98 Thus, companies should consider publicizing their policies and programs to a broader audience than their employees, such as business partners who act on the company's behalf. Companies also should consider outsourcing the management of reporting channels to independent third parties. The use of such parties may boost employee confidence in the companies' promises of anonymity and non-retaliation, thereby increasing the likelihood of internal reporting.

9.2. Benchmark Internal Reporting Figures
Companies should consider benchmarking their internal reporting systems against those of similar companies in their industry. For instance, hotline metrics such as call volume can be benchmarked against industry statistics. Deviations from industry averages may be an indication that a company needs to improve its whistleblower channels, increase employee awareness about such channels, or instill greater employee confidence in its internal reporting mechanisms. Companies may also benefit from issuing anonymous employee surveys about internal reporting channels. Comparing results over time and between operating units can help a company better assess employee sentiment about such mechanisms. The surveys may also raise employee awareness about the available channels for reporting violations.

97. Association of Certified Fraud Examiners, Report to the Nations on Occupational Fraud and Abuse: 2010 Global Fraud Study 16 (2010) ("Tips were by far the most common detection method in our study, catching nearly three times as many frauds as any other form of detection... . Tips have been far and away the most common means of detection in every study since 2002, when we began tracking the data."). 98. Id. at 17 (reporting that "customers, vendors, competitors and acquaintances (i.e., non-company sources) provided at least 34% of fraud tips" in the 2010 study).

12 ­ How whistleblowing may pay under the U.S. Dodd-Frank Act: implications...

267

9.3. Communicate the Importance and Effectiveness of Internal Reporting
Employee awareness of a company's compliance program also is vital to encouraging internal reporting. As a result, companies should continually emphasize to all employees the importance of their compliance programs, publicize their channels for reporting employee concerns, including explain the availability of confidential and anonymous ways to submit such concerns or complaints, and clearly explain their commitment against retaliation for reporting concerns or complaints internally or to the SEC. This messaging should come from and be reinforced by senior management and directors of the organization. Periodic training about companies' compliance programs are effective ways to enhance employee understanding of the importance of internal reporting. Companies can promote their compliance programs by taking prompt action in response to reports about concerns and informing employees of such actions as well as improvements and other constructive changes made in response to internal reports, recognizing employees who have shown an exceptional commitment to corporate compliance, and providing incentives to employees who routinely participate in compliance training and workshops. Finally, companies should consider explaining the SEC's whistleblower program to their employees, including the potential for increased awards if employees report internally first, and should not create the impression that employees cannot or should not report concerns or complaints to the SEC.

10. Conclusion
The whistleblower provisions of the Dodd-Frank Act may have wide-ranging implications for U.S. securities filers in the United States and abroad. Companies subject to the Act should carefully review the potential implications and risks of the SEC's new whistleblower program and encourage employees to use reporting mechanisms offered by the company if there are compliance and ethics concerns. Companies should be vigilant in their compliance programs, training, and monitoring to identify and address potential transgressions of applicable laws or internal company standards. Ultimately, companies are best protected from potential regulatory and related-whistleblower risks when they have robust and effective compliance programs and can clearly demonstrate that they respond appropriately to concerns reported by employees and that they are attentive to identifying, addressing, and remediating compliance issues promptly and proactively.

Best practices for investigations in Brazil

PAU L J. M C N U LT Y1 THOMAS A. DOYLE2
1. Introduction. 2. The "duty to investigate" economy. 3. Ten important lessons. 3.1. The "who" questions. 3.2. Early action items. 4. Conclusion.

1. Introduction

A

fter briefly noting forces that have increased the number of formal corporate investigations, the authors (two Baker & McKenzie principals, including the former Deputy Attorney General who authored the original "McNulty Memorandum" on corporate prosecutions in the US) offer 10 practical lessons or steps to take, based on their experiences with investigations centered in Brazil.

2. The "Duty to Investigate" Economy
Corporate general counsel have justifiably shifted their concerns from the "duty to disclose" to the "duty to investigate." Regulations and enforcement increasingly focus on who a Company chooses to do business with, the preventive action it takes, and the response it makes
1. Partner at Baker & McKenzie. Chair of Baker & McKenzie's Global Corporate Compliance Steering Committee. Former U.S. Deputy Attorney General. Former Deputy Attorney General of the United States Department of Justice. 2. Partner at Baker & McKenzie. Past chair of Baker & McKenzie's North America Litigation Practice.

to red warning flags. The two previously dominant questions (which known facts must be disclosed?, and when does the Company really know them enough to require such disclosure?) have been eclipsed by the newer issues of when must the Company affirmatively investigate, and how should it acquire relevant facts. When a crisis such as a government accusation, arrest, or investigation suddenly arises, neither the regulators nor the public will allow the Company to simply say "no comment" and to insist that the full litigation process must run its course to determine the facts and consequences. Effective crisis management usually requires the Company to promise customers, employees, investors, and government officials that the Company will fully investigate matters that reflect unfavorably on the Company's integrity or on its compliance with significant laws. Courts and regulators in the US have made the adequacy, speed, and thoroughness of any such investigation important factors in deciding whether to prosecute the Company ­ and how to punish it. 3 The unavoidable result is more focus on investigations. Although partly driven by fear of the prosecutor's draconian punishments (e.g., debarment, appointment of monitors, and imposition of massive fines), the investigation is also part of the necessary cure for whatever caused the trouble. Regulators understandably believe that any serious intention to avoid future offenses begins with a serious determination of what contributed to the Company's original failure. Legally, a good investigation affects two kinds of fault or culpability. The first concerns whether the Company encouraged or tolerated the conduct. This is to separate "rogue employee" situations from cases where the employee's conduct was a predictable or expected result of some Company incentive, policy, neglect, or condoned practice.4 Perceptions of fault are also influenced by any sense that the Company knew facts that should have led the Company to pursue more facts with adequate inquiry. An important federal appellate court has recently affirmed the FCPA bribery conviction that resulted from the Justice Department's use of a "wilful blindness" theory in prosecuting an individual who either knew of bribes or consciously avoided obtaining such

3. For example, the Department of Justice Guidelines for Charging Corporations (available at http://www.justice.gov/opa/documents/corp-charging-guidelines.pdf) note the key importance of a Company producing discovered facts to the government, which can only be obtained through serious inquiry. The SEC similarly weighs discretionary factors that depend, in the first instance, on the Company having meaningful investigative information to disclose to the Agency regarding possible securities law violations. See,, e.g., In re ArthroCare Corp., Exchange Act Release No. 63,883 (Feb. 9, 2011), http://www.sec.gov/litigation/admin/2011/34-63883.pdf. A solid investigation is the start of any such cooperation with the agencies, which is the start of obtaining some favorable credit regarding prosecutorial and punishment factors. 4. Under US law, a company can be criminally liable for a rogue employee's conduct that was intended at least partly to benefit the corporation. The exposure increases if the Company knew, expected, or condoned the conduct.

13 ­ Best practices for investigations in Brazil

271

knowledge despite warning signs.5 While wilful blindness (sticking one's head in the sand to avoid knowledge) still requires serious proof (which can be entirely circumstantial), no corporation wants to risk judicial proceedings that punish on the basis of what the company could have learned if it had conducted an adequate investigation. There is a greater current sense that Companies should more thoroughly inquire to protect themselves, Companies are expected to be more aware of, and more responsive to, circumstances that raise suspicions, and they are at great peril if they fail to look beyond what they definitely know and follow the logical trail to what they could and should know. A thorough investigation thus achieves multiple healthy purposes. It helps the Company know if it missed previous warnings and why it missed them. It firms up internal controls when weaknesses are identified and cured. It is persuasive evidence to the regulators that the Company does not intend such conduct to occur. And it is the safest way to prevent a current failure from becoming future proof that the Company did not care and did not act to prevent repetition. In summary, the "duty to investigate" is another manifestation of the Board's ever-present Duty of Care. In the corruption setting, this duty has been heightened by a combination of (1) more generally available information about risk (such as Transparency International's bribery rankings for countries, public announcements of government investigations, and competitors' disclosures of their experiences), (2) greater corporate knowledge about what constitutes a red flag, reflects weak controls, or suggests patterns of bad behavior,6 and (3) a public and regulatory perception that the point at which a Company "should have known" or "should have investigated more" is lower than it once was. Collectively, these all counsel in favor of rapidly reacting to surprise exposures with a thorough and independent investigation. Government raids are obvious (and urgent) triggers for investigations, but they are not the only reason to investigate. News about the arrest or investigation of a competitor,7 rumors in the marketplace, complaints on employee hotlines, and due diligence activities in acquisitions can all become investigation
5. United States v. Kozeny, No. 09-cr-4704, 2011 WL 6184494 (2d Cir. Dec. 14, 2011). 6. Corporations "learn" individually and collectively. Experience is constantly accumulating in the investigative community about red flags and warning signs. A request for an "out-of-country payment" might have once been tolerated as a tax avoidance that did not concern the corporation, but now is a magnet for investigation. Unspecified "marketing expenses" were once accepted as a catch-all classification for all kinds of expenditures, but are now an immediate source of concern and reason for attention. Legal rules naturally follow notions of best practices, eventually holding companies to higher and higher levels of sophistication and awareness that have developed in the marketplace. A company cannot complain about what it did not know or understand when its industry is awash in realizations about corruption or weak controls. 7. Competitors often deal with the same government agencies that a company faces. News that a competitor has been raided, or has disclosed potential FCPA violations in a particular country or in dealings with a particular agency can be red flags and powerful reasons for investigating whether the same problems are happening inside the Company.

triggers. The time available is usually quite constrained, the publicity risks are usually quite high, the need for independence is great, and the consequences of the investigation are often much greater than those of an average audit. We do not seek, in this chapter, to catalog all the good advice that is available about conducting a comprehensive and thorough internal investigation, but we can offer ten important lessons that we have learned through various investigative experiences in Brazil, all in the hope that mistakes can be avoided, the truth can be discovered or determined, and the Company can recover from the difficult circumstances that prompted the inquiry.

3. Ten Important Lessons
3.1. The "Who" Questions
The Company must quickly establish the right reporting lines and put the right Investigation Team in place. 1. Define the Reporting Lines Immediately Define early (1) who the Client is, (2) who has reason to be told what is discovered during the investigation, and (3) how soon and how frequently that audience should hear reporting from investigating counsel. 8 The simplest and most preferred reporting line is to the Audit Committee of the parent corporation (reinforcing that it is the Board's investigation at the parent level), but ­ depending on the Company's size and experience ­ there is often an intermediate client in the form of the General Counsel, the Business Conduct Committee, or the Chief Ethics officer, each of whom may be charged with initially deciding when a matter rises to the attention of the Audit Committee. 9 There is no one-size-fits-all answer regarding intermediate reporting audiences, but most US companies operating in Brazil have a Brazilian subsidiary, a "Country Manager," and a "Country Counsel," and it is best to assume that none of those is the proper client or proper reporting audience for a serious corruption investigation. Although they may be important resources in the investigation ­ and for that reason they must not be alienated by any early steps

8. An investigation is of no value if the ultimate audience (including regulators or investors) do not perceive it to have been thorough and independent. The only thing worse than an expensive investigation is one that has to be done over, because the regulators doubt its independence and thoroughness. As a result, counsel that is close to potentially involved persons cannot conduct the investigation without risking a loss of credibility. 9. One of these authorities should immediately sign letters of direction instructing all employees to lend their full cooperation to the investigation and to preserve documents that might be related to the subject of the investigation.

13 ­ Best practices for investigations in Brazil

273

­ most investigations at some point need to be protected from their influence.10 Any serious investigation likely will need to comment on whether these same individuals cooperated, and on their potential responsibility for causing the problems, through either acts or failures to act. The same is true of any "Latin American Regional Manager" who may sit above the Brazil Country Manager and be responsible for multiple countries. That person is sometimes a US citizen or may have an office in the US, making the US regulators especially interested in what she knew, when she knew it, and whether her actions or knowledge tie the conduct more closely to US actions and US persons. These three individuals (Country Manager, Country Counsel, and LA Regional Manager) are presumptively not independent enough to be deciding the scope of the investigation or to be hearing its early, preliminary conclusions. This presumption is based on much more than the likelihood that they hired, promoted, and might be friends with some of the bad actors. Their non-independence also comes from the likelihood that they actually played a role in, or had some significant knowledge about, most of the major transactions that will be investigated. In most global organizations with serious internal controls, these individuals are likely to be key parts of those internal controls ­ but the investigators must remember that something still must have gone wrong if the problem occurred despite those controls. For example, these persons likely sat on a committee that had to approve all major transactions above a certain dollar level. They likely had to "sign off" on unusual transactions, on very large transactions, and on strange features of otherwise normal transactions. These categories include most consulting contracts and large promotional expenditures (either of which can be used to hide the recycling of corrupt monies). They likely had to approve new resellers or other partners (especially if there was a rush to complete a transaction before all the normal due diligence was completed). Along with the Regional Controller, they likely had to be consulted if financial rules were bent on seemingly normal transactions to allow credits, rebates, special marketing funds, or extensions of credit, or to approve unusual revenue recognition, overdue receivables, rebookings or reversals of normal transactions, or other unusual treatments. These concerns are not limited to situations where a government raid occurs, but any raid should heighten these concerns. If the involved transactions were all normal, and all small, the government likely would not have raided the Company
10. They understandably have concerns, as the country budget is often charged for the investigation, but this is an especially insufficient reason for allowing the Country Manager to control the scope of the investigation. That budget is being charged because the problem was caused locally, not because it is best managed locally. So, too, with the choice of counsel, which should not be left to the Country Manager and should not presumptively be the counsel who have traditionally a given advice to the subsidiary.

and there would likely not be a significant corruption investigation. But when there is a raid or an announced government investigation, or substantial hotline activity, investigators should assume and expect that some unusual transactions were specially approved and that the persons holding the titles described above were likely to be involved in those approvals. Whether they did so with corrupt knowledge or in dereliction of their duty to obtain adequate information before giving such approvals will be important issues in any final investigation report, and will probably raise issues about what remedial steps the Company needs to take. For all those reasons, these persons are presumptively important witnesses in the investigation and may become subjects of it. They cannot simultaneously control the investigation or be given all the information it is generating. 2. Immediately Obtain Key Accounting Information The external team should be constituted immediately, with high quality criminal and civil legal talent, plus accounting talent (depending upon the internal resources available). On the accounting side, the Team needs accounting expertise. This requires some Internal Audit and Finance staff members who know (1) the ins and outs of the Company's transaction recording system; (2) the Brazilian operation's prior audit history; and (3) how the Brazilian operation compares to what is done in other Company locations. Usually, this person will need to come from Internal Audit or from Regional Finance (outside the country) to ensure independence. That does not mean that the investigation should be directed or controlled by Internal Audit. Internal Audit's prior mistakes could become facts that might have to be reviewed by independent eyes, so the Team will need to be mindful of any issues that trigger a need for greater independence than internal accounting resources can provide. A final report may need to discuss how prior auditing had been conducted, how indicators of the current problem were previously handled, and how the current problems might have escaped discovery in ongoing audits.11 Those issues require the Investigation Team to have immediate and direct access to the prior audits and to current transaction records ­ especially the records necessary to precisely define when and how transaction revenue was recorded (for purposes of sales reports, employee bonuses, and performance reviews) and recognized for formal accounting purposes. Always remember the logic of why the FCPA statute includes both antibribery prohibitions and internal
11. The temptation to "save money" by using only the Internal Audit team must be dealt with carefully. Of course, duplication of effort should be avoided, but at some point the Investigative Teams may need to call for a new audit, or may need outside accounting help to fully evaluate how well Internal Audit did its prior work and to form credible criticisms and construct recommendations. In many companies, Internal Audit has reviewed not only the Company's records, but also the records of the Company's partners ­ often with difficulty.

13 ­ Best practices for investigations in Brazil

275

controls provisions, in a single statute. Failed controls are almost always part of the environment that enables and hides significant corruption, especially if it is ongoing. While an SEC prosecution for inadequate controls may be avoidable in any given bribery situation, the fact of serious bribes often indicates that some proper accounting controls either failed, were circumvented, or were missing. Even without any intentional accounting staff complicity, the facts often reflect negatively on the inside and outside auditors and finance staff who were supposed to have monitored and enforced the controls. Knowing that good FCPA investigations often will expose some rule bending or gaps in accounting controls, the Regional Controller and the Head of Internal Audit are best considered as helpful witnesses and resources than the body to whom the investigators report. Outside accountants will not always need to be involved, but the Team must be alert to possible indicators of a need for independent accountants or even a full independent forensic audit. 3. Make sure you have a Multi-Country and Multi-Disciplinary Legal Team The external team needs multiple legal capabilities from the very beginning. First, whenever there is a US parent or US operations, there must be seamlessly integrated investigative capability on both the US and Brazil sides. The perspectives of each country's regulators must be understood. Negotiations will likely be required with each regulator before the matter is concluded. And the relevant actors or documents are almost never located in only one of the countries.12 Second, experienced Brazilian employment lawyers must be involved from the very first minute. Among many other questions, they will need to advise about what actions can or should be taken against any employee, especially any employee who might have been arrested or questioned in any police raid or other government action. If an employee was arrested, or an employee's office was searched, or certain employees appear unwilling to cooperate in the investigation, local legal sophistication about Brazilian employment law, practices, and courts will be essential. Third, a corruption-investigating Team should know Brazilian public procurement and bid rigging rules, and have some experience with collusive practices. In public bidding settings ­ where most government procurement takes place ­ corruption can often be accomplished only with some collusion among competitors. The Team needs experience about what competitive contacts are
12. All appropriate precautions need to be taken to ensure that privileged communications with the Team remain privileged under the laws of both countries. Team reports should be made only to the client representatives who are authorized to receive them without compromising the privilege, and witnesses must be made to understand that the investigation is privileged and that the privilege belongs to the Corporation.

legitimate, what limits on resellers can and cannot be imposed by manufacturers, and what clues signal collusive behavior in the records.

3.2. Early Action Items
4. Secure the scene, triage the most immediate documentary needs, and preserve the universe of documents. An investigation's tone, and much of its success, may be determined in the first 48 hours, particularly if it was prompted by a government raid. There are five major "physical" tasks that need to immediately be done. The word "physical" captures the notion that these acts generally need to be done on-site and in-person, and not just through some instructions from a distance. a. Secure the on-site materials. This includes limiting and controlling the access of any individuals to the offices and documents in question, especially if any raid took place. But labor issues must be carefully watched when controlling such access. If the police treated the office as a crime scene, then the Company should do the same, quickly ensuring (1) that it knows and tries to generate a copy of whatever was taken by the police; (2) that nothing is removed or destroyed from the office, despite the many reasonable-sounding excuses that might be offered;13 and (3) that new persons are specially designated to review any "work-in-process" on which the involved employees may have been working at the time. Bids may be due the next day, or important negotiations may be underway, and a balance needs to be made to ensure those transactions are not just abandoned through neglect, but also are not continued if they are contaminated or are a continuation of past risks. b. Identify all recent and pending transactions and files related to the government agencies involved. In any raid or announced investigation, the police will have identified some agency, governmental official, employee, reseller, or agent who is an object of their interest (by virtue of their search warrant, announced arrests, or other clues in what they ask for). The Team should immediately list (and start gathering all the files for) all transactions in the last couple of years that involved that
13. The Company must not underestimate the likelihood that potentially involved offenders (who, of course, know where the bad documents are and how bad they are) will seek to get back into their offices, their computers, the Company's network, or other places where incriminating materials might be removed or altered ­ and the damage to the Investigation's credibility if the Team can not tell the authorities that the records were immediately preserved and protected, with the potential wrongdoers isolated. It is vital that these persons are immediately separated (revoked passwords, imposition of leave, etc.) from the relevant materials and that there be no other friendly actors or co-conspirators who might be accessing these items for them.

13 ­ Best practices for investigations in Brazil

277

customer, supplier, partner, or government entity or person. No one else should be allowed access to these identified and collected transactions, and their existing condition should be copied and preserved before the Team digs into them. c. Issue a preservation letter immediately and acquire the documents. US investigations and litigation continue to generate numerous examples of the trouble caused ­ including severe punishments ­ for Companies who fail to immediately preserve documents that the Company should have known were relevant to a dispute or governmental inquiry. These punishments, coupled with the potential damage to the credibility and completeness of the investigation, explain the importance of immediately issuing a "hold" letter that instructs all employees to preserve all electronic and hardcopy documents in any form, related to certain broadly stated subjects or topics.14 d. Stop and Prevent Any Questionable Conduct. Duly informed about what transactions are pending, who is under suspicion, and what customers or intermediaries seem to be presenting the acute risk, the Company must terminate any questionable activity. Government regulators are generally favorably impressed by proof that the Company immediately acted to prevent more wrongdoing. And they will be quite unfavorably impressed by any evidence that the Company took chances, left suspected employees in command, prolonged its evaluation of a continuing risk, or took any benefit from allowing similar conduct to continue. Fresh eyes should decide if pending transactions should continue, and the Investigative Team should be made aware of those transactions as the decisions are being made. e. Get up to speed immediately on the prior accounting and auditing. As noted earlier, the Team needs to know what prior findings were made in the previous internal audits, and whether previously noted weaknesses were rectified. This same need includes previous "partner audits" of the partners who might allegedly be involved in any corruption.15

14. Defining the scope of the hold is always difficult, but the Company faces more risk if it errs on the side of a narrow definition, rather than preserving some extra documents. Remember, good documents will likely get destroyed along with bad ones, impairing the investigation's ability to get all the relevant information. The destruction of relevant documents to frustrate an actual or anticipated government action is a form of obstruction of justice, punishable by criminal sanctions under the Sarbanes Oxley statute. See, e.g., United States v. Kernell, No. 10-6450 (6 th Cir Jan. 30, 2012). 15. Previously noted dishonesty by a reseller can often indicate a willingness to be dishonest on other subjects. For example, if a reseller agrees at the end of the year to artificially accelerate or defer purchases or payments to influence its own financial results and those of the manufacturer (a common problem) that reciprocity may indicate that the reseller might be more willing to tolerate or participate in other improprieties (such as corrupt payments).

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

5. Get Early and Continuing Brazilian Employment Advice on Personnel Issues and Involve Criminal Counsel in both jurisdictions from the outset. Experienced employment lawyers are needed immediately, as it is likely that any potentially "involved" employees will present challenges immediately. A few of the reasons for the warning that good labor counsel is essential can be found in the decisions that will have to be made quickly. a. Permissible "Leave" decisions. An employer's right to impose involuntary leave is limited and subject to a number of formalities in Brazil. While it is important to keep an arrested, questioned, or involved employee out of his office and away from his documents during the early stages of the investigation, a number of formalities need to be taken into consideration as the Company decides and notifies the employee of changes in his status. b. Be careful about where you cause the merits to be adjudicated. Brazil has Labor Courts that handle employee matters, and as a general rule, the Company will not be well served by having important issues prematurely and incompletely adjudicated in those Courts. As a general rule, it is unusually difficult to terminate Brazilian employees for suspicion, or even for their refusal to cooperate. A sudden or poorly-considered decision to terminate such an employee could result in claims in the Labor Courts, which might proceed more quickly than the investigation. The Company could be forced to defend itself with incomplete items that it would prefer not to expose publicly at an early stage. Secret or privileged facts in the investigation might have to be disclosed in such Courts, or the Court might declare ­ on an inadequate record ­ an inaccurate "finding" about what was or was not proven as to the underlying offense. c. Prepare for complicated obstacles to employee cooperation. US notions of employee cooperation in an investigation are not easily enforced in Brazil. In the US, employees can often be disciplined or terminated if they fail to cooperate (especially for any lying or refusal to answer questions during interviews). In Brazil, the expectations are quite different. Brazilian employees generally expect to keep their jobs or at least a favorable severance, even if they refuse to cooperate or are caught in false statements. While management employees (Country Manager level or above) may be differently motivated by their Company aspirations, other employees are often quite confident about their right to keep their jobs, even if they do not help the investigation. These fault lines in the normal foundation for a good investigation run deep. They can cause trouble on any number of small, but very important details.

13 ­ Best practices for investigations in Brazil

279

For example, the Company must be exceedingly careful about seizing laptops. Company reviews of individual laptops have been a hot topic in Brazil, and labor counsel will be needed to advise in each situation about the effectiveness of the Company's warnings to employees about the absence of laptop privacy and the limits of what the Company can do. The employees are likely to claim, first, that the laptop may not really be exclusively the Company's property. Even more likely, and regardless of whether the laptop is company property, difficulties can arise when an employee claims that she has personal banking records on the laptop. While such banking records might be exactly what the Company would like to see on some points in the investigation (and may actually reflect where improper transactions have occurred), Brazilian privacy and employment law are likely to keep the Company from viewing them and may in some cases even prevent the Company from seizing the laptop because it contains both business and personal information.16 Another potential clash between employee privacy rights and the Investigation arises when the Company tries to investigate (quite normally, by US standards) whether the lifestyle or apparent resources of an employee seem inconsistent with his salary and suggest the employee is somehow generating additional income. Personal information is hard to acquire and legal rules against accessing bank records must be followed. The Company will likely also learn that many, many individual employees in Brazil have their own incorporated "personal companies." Their explanations vary and often relate to prior jobs, but the Company is left with the unavoidable suspicion that such employees could easily receive side payments through entities that are not transparent to the Company. Again, the Company may seek to deal with some access issues long before it has a ripe investigation by carefully considering employment policies that are helpful to investigations such as requiring disclosures about such corporations. d. Avoid Exemplary Damages Brazil protects employees from hostility and embarrassment in the workplace, by allowing punitive damages for undefined abuses of the employees. Labor lawyers will warn the investigators against being too aggressive or risking the appearance that employees have been singled out in any unfair way. That fairness scale is tipped in favor of the employees, and they generally know it. If the Company wants to avoid adding punitive damage claims to the predictable labor claims, it must be careful about how it treats the employees, even in an
16. Employment practices in Brazil do not often emphasize provisions in employee manuals to anticipate and prevent such issues by clarifying the Company's absolute control of such property.

280

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

accelerated investigation. This can affect details such as whether interviews are conducted offsite (at a hotel, for example) to avoid embarrassment. There is a good offsetting argument that helpful whistleblowers may be more likely to come forward if they see that a vigorous on-site investigation is underway. That sometimes counsels in favor of the outside investigative Team having a strong presence (and availability) on-site where the records and employees are located. e. Involve Criminal Counsel The involved statutes are criminal. Search warrants and raids are serious problems that raise issues and exposures that should exceed the Country Manager's normal authority. These are not matters within the normal experience of the Country Manager's preferred local civil counsel; nor are they appropriately relegated to the US parent's general corporate counsel. They most often present criminal exposure in both countries, and a combination of qualified criminal counsel in both jurisdictions will be essential. Brazilian criminal counsel must be familiar with the local criminal bar and able to establish rapport with any attorneys who may become involved for employees who are accused of wrongdoing. US criminal counsel must be involved to deal with the very serious exposures presented by the anti-corruption and competition statutes in the US, under which the corporation can be a criminal defendant. Not every Brazilian investigation will be about offenses that are within the jurisdiction of the US authorities, but any conclusion on that issue should be made by competent US counsel who regularly deal with those issues and authorities. 6. Understand the Core Business The Team must understand how the Company's products and services are produced, priced, sold, and delivered. Corruption operates in many non-monetary currencies, including discounts, rebates, free trips, free "demo" machines, promotional allowances for resellers, and payments to real or fictitious third parties for services that were never rendered. These "currencies" can be directly given to end-users and decision-makers in government agencies, or routed through resellers who wash the trail a bit while corruptly influencing customers. The Team needs to quickly learn what the companies goods and services are, how its product markets are divided, where the profit margins are, how profit is defined and recognized, and what "normal" might be. The US Securities and Exchange Commission is concerned about seemingly excess margin being granted to resellers of manufactured products, which fuels or funds corrupt opportunities. Such margin can be similar to excess intermediary commissions that were the subject of many early investigations of how bribes were paid to officials. If the Team does not understand ­ and statistically

13 ­ Best practices for investigations in Brazil

281

sample ­ the margin levels on various transactions, it may have no norms to use for comparing and analyzing what might seem exceptional or troubling. This sampling and reviewing of transactions needs to consider other terms such as delivery and billing terms and the level of normal documentation. Outliers on any of these items can be cause for further inquiry. 7. Understand the Incentive Structure and the Accounting that Implements It Corruption is not always driven by a seller's desire to make more sales. Local employees and managers often benefit from placing a sale in a particular period (through revenue recognition, bonuses, and promotions). Corrupting a local buyer may simply help the employee keep his job, achieve his targets, or achieve certain levels in a particular reporting period. Thus, a reseller or end user may be encouraged to place an order in a particular period (with a promise that it can be later withdrawn), or may be asked to sign fake verification of delivery or installation. The Team needs to know what acts and paperwork actually drive revenue recognition and what drivers determine compensation, so it can know to look for certain kinds of unusual practices and inquire about the genuine nature of various certifications or approvals. 8. Look for Reseller Collusion In many non-US markets, market power is wielded more by resellers than by manufacturers, for a variety of reasons. Ironically, as the US FCPA forces US manufacturers to exercise more and more diligence about the quality and integrity of their "partners" in non-US markets, that process can become a kind of barrier-to-entry in either poorly developed distribution markets, or highly developed distribution markets. Both situations are conducive to the trouble, because in each, there may be a limit to the number of available high-quality distributors who have the ability and willingness to sign onto the high Compliance standards and rigorous accounting reviews that the US manufacturing entity seeks to impose as part of its Due Diligence in selecting Hand monitoring the partner resellers. There may be some large and high quality distributors in a developed market, but they may have exclusive dealing arrangements with the dominant manufacturers. In poorly developed distribution systems, the same problem can occur just because there are not yet many distributors willing and able to pass these tests. There may also not be many distributors who are willing to handle all the pre-sale duties and post-sale warranty obligations that are sometimes necessary in complicated government sales. As a result of any of these forces, the choices might be limited. When powerful manufacturers sell through a relatively limited number of quality resellers, cartel power may be reinforced (and may be greater) at the distribution level.

282

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

If local resellers are willing to protect each other, they can frustrate even very aggressive Compliance efforts by foreign manufacturers. Bids can be rigged, available contracts can be divided, and fake bids can be generated, with the result that an apparent competitive bidding environment becomes a cover for corruption. The Team must look for this conduct, which can often be found in bidding patterns or other records that indicate possible trade-offs of some bids for others. As surprising as it may be to the Investigation Team, local resellers may be more willing to manage a modestly sustainable division of government contracts among themselves, rather than seeking to seriously compete for the benefit of their manufacturer's brand. 9. Understand The Many Ways Government Contracting Can Be Corrupted Many bids for government contracts are done electronically over the internet in Brazil. Communications generated during those bids, and a full understanding of who was physically where (or talking to whom) during the bid, can be important to discerning collusion.17 Most Brazilian agencies offer significant public records of which bidders placed particular bids, and in what order. The Team should quickly acquire those records not only about any suspect transactions, but also about other transactions that might involve the same agency or the same resellers, or might reveal patterns. Investigators need to also understand that the bidding process in Brazil is often artificially shortened by the bidding authority to stimulate the bidders to move quickly to their best bids. If the person managing the actual conduct of the bid has been corrupted, these cut-off times can be used to rig what might otherwise seem to be a transparent award to the low bidder. Additionally, when reviewing Brazilian government procurement, an Investigation should look for collusion in the pre-bid estimates as well as in the final formal bids. These pre-bid indicators are often used to set budgets for the bid, and if they are tainted by collusion, they can be used to soften up the government agency to higher numbers, before any bid occurs. While this can be spoiled if a nonparticipating reseller then shows up for the final bid, a corrupt official might still steer the bid by disqualifying that competitor (for some pretext reason) or by rigging an early cutoff when the preferred bidder is winning. In any event, the healthy appearance of public bidding over the internet does not always reflect competition and an absence of corruption.
17. Some other flags stand out. Investigators should be quite concerned by the practice of key employees and resellers having special "second" phones. If the police have phone taps and reveal the content of recorded conversations, the Team should take obvious, negative inferences (as the police will) from odd language that sounds coded in these communications. Such practices are more appropriate for drug dealers than for equipment resellers, but they have been found in past Brazilian cases.

13 ­ Best practices for investigations in Brazil

283

10. Strengthen contract rights before any investigation Integrity investigations are just one kind of corporate crisis. Just as the corporation prepares for other forms of disaster response, it should take steps long before any investigation occurs. First, it should have an investigations policy, pre-determining who is required to be involved in certain kinds of investigations. Second, it should perform a tune-up on basic contract rights, such as the right to audit partner resellers. Those contracts frequently mention rights (such as a "right to audit"), but have few specifics and no teeth when it comes time to do anything on an expedited basis. Similarly, the employment manual provisions and disclosures that would help the investigators' access to laptops and other records should be reviewed and amended now, before they are needed.

4. Conclusion
The US Securities and Exchange Commission is expressing significant interest in Brazil. The US Justice Department's Antitrust Division has been quite interested in Brazil for many years. Cooperation between prosecutors in Brazil and these US prosecutors is increasingly common, easy, and targeted. No Company should assume that a raid or publicized investigation by Brazilian authorities will not come to the attention of the US authorities. Whether a Brazil investigation must be disclosed by a US entity still involves issues that call for due regard for materiality, certainty, and the parent-subsidiary relationship, but US companies are more often advising US authorities about investigations underway in non-US jurisdictions, and are doing so at earlier stages in the investigative process. Frequently, the US prosecutors are understaffed for the magnitude of the investigation required, and the situation sometimes takes on the appearance of an investigation being done by outside counsel partly for the Company and partly for the benefit of the authorities. This is because the promise of the Company's full and fair investigation is often part of how the agency is kept reasonably patient, rather than in aggressive prosecution mode. These situations, and the government deference reflected in them, depend heavily upon outside counsel and the entire Investigation Team having independence and credibility. Such independence and credibility depend upon adhering to high standarts and using best practices in any corruption investigation.

Análise de dados não estruturados. 3.3.2. Análise de dados estruturados. 4. Relatórios. 5. Considerações finais.

1. Introdução

C

omputação forense consiste no uso de métodos científicos que dizem respeito à preservação, coleta, restauração, identificação, análise e documentação de evidências computacionais, quer sejam componentes físicos ou dados que foram processados eletronicamente e armazenados em mídias computacionais. O propósito da computação forense é a coleta, procura e extração de evidências relacionadas com o caso investigado. Na maioria dos casos, a computação forense busca obter informação de valor probante ­ em conformidade com as regras e leis para a admissão destas em uma corte de justiça a ser utilizada em um processo trabalhista, cível ou criminal.

1. Sócio da área de Forensics Technlogy and Discovery Services da Ernst & Young no Brasil. 2. Gerente Sênior da área Forensics Technlogy and Discovery Services da Ernst & Young no Brasil.

285

286

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

A totalidade da informação disponível, desde que relevante para o caso em questão, deve ser coletada para posterior análise e estas fontes de dados devem ser adequadamente manuseadas, documentadas e devidamente preservadas, sendo de máxima importância que a integridade das fontes de dados seja preservada, adicionalmente, quando possível, a computação forense deve permitir o acesso e a análise de dados que tenham sido apagados ou que não sejam de fácil visualização para a maioria das pessoas. Outra característica da computação forense é que a análise das evidências pode resultar na descoberta de novas informações que podem demandar novas coletas e análises, formando assim um ciclo no processo de computação forense. A área de Compliance de uma empresa é normalmente a área responsável pela verificação da aderência dos funcionários às normas internas e ao ambiente legal no qual a empresa está inserida e, em alguns casos, também é responsável pela apuração de eventuais denúncias e violações destas normas e leis. Dessa maneira, podemos inferir que os resultados das atividades da área de Compliance poderão se tornar litígios trabalhistas, cíveis ou até mesmo criminais e, considerando que a maioria dos dados analisados em um trabalho de Compliance é digital, como veremos na próxima seção deste capítulo, temos que a utilização da computação forense se faz necessária para aumentar a possibilidade de que estas evidências sejam aceitas nestes processos e para obter dados que não estariam acessíveis caso outras técnicas fossem utilizadas. Nas próximas seções falaremos em mais detalhes sobre evidências digitais e sobre técnicas para a coleta e análise destes dados.

2. Dados e Evidências Digitais
Atualmente a maioria dos dados gerados por uma empresa durante as suas atividades são dados digitais. Com o crescimento da informática, os memorandos internos foram substituídos pelo correio eletrônico, os dados financeiros que no passado eram armazenados em livros físicos hoje são armazenados em sistemas informatizados e acessados através da internet, até mesmo as notas fiscais, cujos valores já eram armazenados nos sistemas financeiros, já estão sendo emitidas de forma eletrônica. No futuro, esta tendência deve eliminar completamente a presença de documentos físicos nas empresas. A informatização das empresas traz um impacto direto na forma como os trabalhos de Compliance são realizados, pois, o relatório contendo os resultados do trabalho será suportado, em grande parte, por dados digitais que eventualmente poderão ser tornar evidências digitais em processos legais. Evidências digitais podem ser definidas como o conjunto de dados e as informações associadas (metadados) a estes que foram coletadas através de técnicas de

14 ­ Introdução à Computação Forense

287

computação forense, por exemplo, ao coletarmos a imagem de um disco rígido ­ assunto que detalharemos mais adiante ­ teremos ao final do processo um conjunto de dados que corresponde de forma fiel aos dados que estavam armazenados no disco rígido de origem e, além disso, coletaremos diversas informações relacionadas ao próprio disco rígido como, por exemplo, número de série, modelo do disco rígido e fabricante. Outro exemplo dessa abstração seria a coleta de um arquivo do Microsoft Word, pois, devemos obter todos os dados que compõe o documento e também os metadados associados a ele, os quais são, dentre outros: · Nome do arquivo. · Data de criação, última escrita e último acesso. · Autor do documento. · Título do documento. A captura de tela abaixo exibe alguns destes metadados:

Essa abstração, dados e metadados associados a eles, pode ser aplicada a todos os tipos de evidências digitais e deve ser sempre considerada ao realizarmos uma coleta forense de dados digitais pois, se a ferramenta utilizada não realiza

288

TEMAS DE ANTICORRUPÇÃO E COMPLIANCE

a coleta completa de ambos ou altera algum destes dados durante a coleta, a utilização dela deverá ser avaliada com muito critério para que a evidência não seja invalidada. De forma geral a evidência digital possui algumas características próprias: · Na maioria dos casos, a evidência digital pode ser duplicada com exatidão, permitindo a preservação da evidência original durante a análise. · Utilizando-se dos métodos apropriados é possível determinar se uma evidência digital foi modificada. · Evidências digitais são extremamente voláteis e, caso os cuidados necessários não sejam tomados, elas podem ser facilmente alteradas durante o processo de coleta e análise. O conceito de "ordem de volatilidade", introduzido por Dan Farmer e Wietse Venema deve ser sempre considerado antes da realização dessas atividades. Tal conceito determina que o tempo de vida de uma evidência digital varia de acordo com o local onde ela está armazenada. Quanto maior a volatilidade de uma informação, mais difícil se torna sua extração e menos tempo há para capturá-la. O simples ato de observar informações altamente voláteis pode alterá-las, por exemplo. Em adição às características citadas acima, podemos classificar as evidências digitais como provenientes de dados estruturados e dados não estruturados. Dados estruturados podem ser definidos como dados que possuem uma estrutura de linhas e colunas e que podem ser manipulados através de sistemas de bancos de dados, mesmo que este dado esteja armazenado em outros sistemas, como, por exemplo, em uma planilha de Excel. Dados não estruturados são dados que não possuem uma estrutura de linhas e colunas e que normalmente são interpretados por editores de texto, clientes de correio eletrônico, dentre outros aplicativos. A figura abaixo apresenta um exemplo de dados estruturados:

Essa classificação é muito importante para a computação forense, pois ela afeta as ferramentas que serão utilizadas na coleta e análise dos dados digitais, como veremos a seguir:

3. Processo de Computação Forense
O processo de computação forense possui um fluxo que é composto de quatro macroatividades, são elas: · Identificação. · Coleta e preservação de dados. · Análise. · Relatórios e produção de documentos. O diagrama abaixo apresenta este fluxo e demonstra o relacionamento entre estas atividades:
Processo da Computação Forense

Produção de Relatórios e Documentos
